You may copy it, give it away or\r\nre-use it under the terms of the Project Gutenberg License included\r\nwith this eBook or online at www.gutenberg.org\r\n\r\n\r\nTitle: The Magnificent Ambersons\r\n\r\nAuthor: Booth Tarkington\r\n\r\nRelease Date: September, 2005 [EBook #8867]\r\nPosting Date: August 2, 2009\r\n\r\nLanguage: English\r\n\r\nCharacter set encoding: ASCII\r\n\r\n*** START OF THIS PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE MAGNIFICENT AMBERSONS ***\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nProduced by An Anonymous Volunteer\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE MAGNIFICENT AMBERSONS\r\n\r\n\r\nBy Booth Tarkington\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter I\r\n\r\n\r\nMajor Amberson had "made a fortune" in 1873, when other people were\r\nlosing fortunes, and the magnificence of the Ambersons began then.\r\nMagnificence, like the size of a fortune, is always comparative, as even\r\nMagnificent Lorenzo may now perceive, if he has happened to haunt New\r\nYork in 1916; and the Ambersons were magnificent in their day and place.\r\nTheir splendour lasted throughout all the years that saw their Midland\r\ntown spread and darken into a city, but reached its topmost during the\r\nperiod when every prosperous family with children kept a Newfoundland\r\ndog.\r\n\r\nIn that town, in those days, all the women who wore silk or velvet knew\r\nall the other women who wore silk or velvet, and when there was a new\r\npurchase of sealskin, sick people were got to windows to see it go by.\r\nTrotters were out, in the winter afternoons, racing light sleighs on\r\nNational Avenue and Tennessee Street; everybody recognized both\r\nthe trotters and the drivers; and again knew them as well on summer\r\nevenings, when slim buggies whizzed by in renewals of the snow-time\r\nrivalry. For that matter, everybody knew everybody else\'s family\r\nhorse-and-carriage, could identify such a silhouette half a mile down\r\nthe street, and thereby was sure who was going to market, or to a\r\nreception, or coming home from office or store to noon dinner or evening\r\nsupper.\r\n\r\nDuring the earlier years of this period, elegance of personal appearance\r\nwas believed to rest more upon the texture of garments than upon their\r\nshaping. A silk dress needed no remodelling when it was a year or so\r\nold; it remained distinguished by merely remaining silk. Old men and\r\ngovernors wore broadcloth; "full dress" was broadcloth with "doeskin"\r\ntrousers; and there were seen men of all ages to whom a hat meant only\r\nthat rigid, tall silk thing known to impudence as a "stove-pipe."\r\nIn town and country these men would wear no other hat, and, without\r\nself-consciousness, they went rowing in such hats.\r\n\r\nShifting fashions of shape replaced aristocracy of texture: dressmakers,\r\nshoemakers, hatmakers, and tailors, increasing in cunning and in power,\r\nfound means to make new clothes old. The long contagion of the "Derby"\r\nhat arrived: one season the crown of this hat would be a bucket; the\r\nnext it would be a spoon. Every house still kept its bootjack, but\r\nhigh-topped boots gave way to shoes and "congress gaiters"; and these\r\nwere played through fashions that shaped them now with toes like\r\nbox-ends and now with toes like the prows of racing shells.\r\n\r\nTrousers with a crease were considered plebeian; the crease proved that\r\nthe garment had lain upon a shelf, and hence was "ready-made"; these\r\nbetraying trousers were called "hand-me-downs," in allusion to the\r\nshelf. In the early \'eighties, while bangs and bustles were having\r\ntheir way with women, that variation of dandy known as the "dude" was\r\ninvented: he wore trousers as tight as stockings, dagger-pointed shoes,\r\na spoon "Derby," a single-breasted coat called a "Chesterfield," with\r\nshort flaring skirts, a torturing cylindrical collar, laundered to a\r\npolish and three inches high, while his other neckgear might be a heavy,\r\npuffed cravat or a tiny bow fit for a doll\'s braids. With evening dress\r\nhe wore a tan overcoat so short that his black coat-tails hung visible,\r\nfive inches below the over-coat; but after a season or two he lengthened\r\nhis overcoat till it touched his heels, and he passed out of his tight\r\ntrousers into trousers like great bags. Then, presently, he was seen\r\nno more, though the word that had been coined for him remained in the\r\nvocabularies of the impertinent.\r\n\r\nIt was a hairier day than this. Beards were to the wearers\' fancy,\r\nand things as strange as the Kaiserliche boar-tusk moustache were\r\ncommonplace. "Side-burns" found nourishment upon childlike profiles;\r\ngreat Dundreary whiskers blew like tippets over young shoulders;\r\nmoustaches were trained as lambrequins over forgotten mouths; and it\r\nwas possible for a Senator of the United States to wear a mist of white\r\nwhisker upon his throat only, not a newspaper in the land finding the\r\nornament distinguished enough to warrant a lampoon. Surely no more is\r\nneeded to prove that so short a time ago we were living in another age!\r\n\r\nAt the beginning of the Ambersons\' great period most of the houses of\r\nthe Midland town were of a pleasant architecture. They lacked style, but\r\nalso lacked pretentiousness, and whatever does not pretend at all has\r\nstyle enough. They stood in commodious yards, well shaded by leftover\r\nforest trees, elm and walnut and beech, with here and there a line of\r\ntall sycamores where the land had been made by filling bayous from the\r\ncreek. The house of a "prominent resident," facing Military Square, or\r\nNational Avenue, or Tennessee Street, was built of brick upon a stone\r\nfoundation, or of wood upon a brick foundation. Usually it had a "front\r\nporch" and a "back porch"; often a "side porch," too. There was a "front\r\nhall"; there was a "side hall"; and sometimes a "back hall." From the\r\n"front hall" opened three rooms, the "parlour," the "sitting room," and\r\nthe "library"; and the library could show warrant to its title--for some\r\nreason these people bought books. Commonly, the family sat more in\r\nthe library than in the "sitting room," while callers, when they came\r\nformally, were kept to the "parlour," a place of formidable polish and\r\ndiscomfort. The upholstery of the library furniture was a little shabby;\r\nbut the hostile chairs and sofa of the "parlour" always looked new. For\r\nall the wear and tear they got they should have lasted a thousand years.\r\n\r\nUpstairs were the bedrooms; "mother-and-father\'s room" the largest; a\r\nsmaller room for one or two sons another for one or two daughters; each\r\nof these rooms containing a double bed, a "washstand," a "bureau," a\r\nwardrobe, a little table, a rocking-chair, and often a chair or two that\r\nhad been slightly damaged downstairs, but not enough to justify either\r\nthe expense of repair or decisive abandonment in the attic. And there\r\nwas always a "spare-room," for visitors (where the sewing-machine\r\nusually was kept), and during the \'seventies there developed an\r\nappreciation of the necessity for a bathroom. Therefore the architects\r\nplaced bathrooms in the new houses, and the older houses tore out a\r\ncupboard or two, set up a boiler beside the kitchen stove, and sought\r\na new godliness, each with its own bathroom. The great American plumber\r\njoke, that many-branched evergreen, was planted at this time.\r\n\r\nAt the rear of the house, upstairs was a bleak little chamber, called\r\n"the girl\'s room," and in the stable there was another bedroom,\r\nadjoining the hayloft, and called "the hired man\'s room." House and\r\nstable cost seven or eight thousand dollars to build, and people with\r\nthat much money to invest in such comforts were classified as the Rich.\r\nThey paid the inhabitant of "the girl\'s room" two dollars a week, and,\r\nin the latter part of this period, two dollars and a half, and finally\r\nthree dollars a week. She was Irish, ordinarily, or German or it might\r\nbe Scandinavian, but never native to the land unless she happened to be\r\na person of colour. The man or youth who lived in the stable had like\r\nwages, and sometimes he, too, was lately a steerage voyager, but much\r\noftener he was coloured.\r\n\r\nAfter sunrise, on pleasant mornings, the alleys behind the stables were\r\ngay; laughter and shouting went up and down their dusty lengths, with\r\na lively accompaniment of curry-combs knocking against back fences and\r\nstable walls, for the darkies loved to curry their horses in the alley.\r\nDarkies always prefer to gossip in shouts instead of whispers; and\r\nthey feel that profanity, unless it be vociferous, is almost worthless.\r\nHorrible phrases were caught by early rising children and carried to\r\nolder people for definition, sometimes at inopportune moments; while\r\nless investigative children would often merely repeat the phrases in\r\nsome subsequent flurry of agitation, and yet bring about consequences so\r\nemphatic as to be recalled with ease in middle life.\r\n\r\nThey have passed, those darky hired-men of the Midland town; and the\r\nintrospective horses they curried and brushed and whacked and amiably\r\ncursed--those good old horses switch their tails at flies no more. For\r\nall their seeming permanence they might as well have been buffaloes--or\r\nthe buffalo laprobes that grew bald in patches and used to slide from\r\nthe careless drivers\' knees and hang unconcerned, half way to the\r\nground. The stables have been transformed into other likenesses, or\r\nswept away, like the woodsheds where were kept the stove-wood and\r\nkindling that the "girl" and the "hired-man" always quarrelled over: who\r\nshould fetch it. Horse and stable and woodshed, and the whole tribe of\r\nthe "hired-man," all are gone. They went quickly, yet so silently that\r\nwe whom they served have not yet really noticed that they are vanished.\r\n\r\nSo with other vanishings. There were the little bunty street-cars on the\r\nlong, single track that went its troubled way among the cobblestones.\r\nAt the rear door of the car there was no platform, but a step where\r\npassengers clung in wet clumps when the weather was bad and the car\r\ncrowded. The patrons--if not too absent-minded--put their fares into a\r\nslot; and no conductor paced the heaving floor, but the driver would rap\r\nremindingly with his elbow upon the glass of the door to his little open\r\nplatform if the nickels and the passengers did not appear to coincide in\r\nnumber. A lone mule drew the car, and sometimes drew it off the track,\r\nwhen the passengers would get out and push it on again. They really owed\r\nit courtesies like this, for the car was genially accommodating: a lady\r\ncould whistle to it from an upstairs window, and the car would halt\r\nat once and wait for her while she shut the window, put on her hat and\r\ncloak, went downstairs, found an umbrella, told the "girl" what to have\r\nfor dinner, and came forth from the house.\r\n\r\nThe previous passengers made little objection to such gallantry on the\r\npart of the car: they were wont to expect as much for themselves on like\r\noccasion. In good weather the mule pulled the car a mile in a little\r\nless than twenty minutes, unless the stops were too long; but when the\r\ntrolley-car came, doing its mile in five minutes and better, it would\r\nwait for nobody. Nor could its passengers have endured such a thing,\r\nbecause the faster they were carried the less time they had to spare! In\r\nthe days before deathly contrivances hustled them through their lives,\r\nand when they had no telephones--another ancient vacancy profoundly\r\nresponsible for leisure--they had time for everything: time to think, to\r\ntalk, time to read, time to wait for a lady!\r\n\r\nThey even had time to dance "square dances," quadrilles, and "lancers";\r\nthey also danced the "racquette," and schottisches and polkas, and\r\nsuch whims as the "Portland Fancy." They pushed back the sliding doors\r\nbetween the "parlour" and the "sitting room," tacked down crash over\r\nthe carpets, hired a few palms in green tubs, stationed three or four\r\nItalian musicians under the stairway in the "front hall"--and had great\r\nnights!\r\n\r\nBut these people were gayest on New Year\'s Day; they made it a true\r\nfestival--something no longer known. The women gathered to "assist" the\r\nhostesses who kept "Open House"; and the carefree men, dandified and\r\nperfumed, went about in sleighs, or in carriages and ponderous "hacks,"\r\ngoing from Open House to Open House, leaving fantastic cards in fancy\r\nbaskets as they entered each doorway, and emerging a little later, more\r\ncarefree than ever, if the punch had been to their liking. It always\r\nwas, and, as the afternoon wore on, pedestrians saw great gesturing and\r\nwaving of skin-tight lemon gloves, while ruinous fragments of song were\r\ndropped behind as the carriages rolled up and down the streets.\r\n\r\n"Keeping Open House" was a merry custom; it has gone, like the all-day\r\npicnic in the woods, and like that prettiest of all vanished customs,\r\nthe serenade. When a lively girl visited the town she did not long\r\ngo unserenaded, though a visitor was not indeed needed to excuse a\r\nserenade. Of a summer night, young men would bring an orchestra under\r\na pretty girl\'s window--or, it might be, her father\'s, or that of an\r\nailing maiden aunt--and flute, harp, fiddle, \'cello, cornet, and bass\r\nviol would presently release to the dulcet stars such melodies as sing\r\nthrough "You\'ll Remember Me," "I Dreamt That I Dwelt in Marble Halls,"\r\n"Silver Threads Among the Gold," "Kathleen Mavourneen," or "The\r\nSoldier\'s Farewell."\r\n\r\nThey had other music to offer, too, for these were the happy days\r\nof "Olivette" and "The Macotte" and "The Chimes of Normandy" and\r\n"Girofle-Girofla" and "Fra Diavola." Better than that, these were the\r\ndays of "Pinafore" and "The Pirates of Penzance" and of "Patience." This\r\nlast was needed in the Midland town, as elsewhere, for the "aesthetic\r\nmovement" had reached thus far from London, and terrible things were\r\nbeing done to honest old furniture. Maidens sawed what-nots in two, and\r\ngilded the remains. They took the rockers from rocking-chairs and gilded\r\nthe inadequate legs; they gilded the easels that supported the crayon\r\nportraits of their deceased uncles. In the new spirit of art they\r\nsold old clocks for new, and threw wax flowers and wax fruit, and the\r\nprotecting glass domes, out upon the trash-heap. They filled vases with\r\npeacock feathers, or cattails, or sumac, or sunflowers, and set the\r\nvases upon mantelpieces and marble-topped tables. They embroidered\r\ndaisies (which they called "marguerites") and sunflowers and sumac and\r\ncat-tails and owls and peacock feathers upon plush screens and upon\r\nheavy cushions, then strewed these cushions upon floors where fathers\r\nfell over them in the dark. In the teeth of sinful oratory, the\r\ndaughters went on embroidering: they embroidered daisies and sunflowers\r\nand sumac and cat-tails and owls and peacock feathers upon "throws"\r\nwhich they had the courage to drape upon horsehair sofas; they painted\r\nowls and daisies and sunflowers and sumac and cat-tails and peacock\r\nfeathers upon tambourines. They hung Chinese umbrellas of paper to\r\nthe chandeliers; they nailed paper fans to the walls. They "studied"\r\npainting on china, these girls; they sang Tosti\'s new songs; they\r\nsometimes still practiced the old, genteel habit of lady-fainting, and\r\nwere most charming of all when they drove forth, three or four in a\r\nbasket phaeton, on a spring morning.\r\n\r\nCroquet and the mildest archery ever known were the sports of people\r\nstill young and active enough for so much exertion; middle-age played\r\neuchre. There was a theatre, next door to the Amberson Hotel, and when\r\nEdwin Booth came for a night, everybody who could afford to buy a ticket\r\nwas there, and all the "hacks" in town were hired. "The Black Crook"\r\nalso filled the theatre, but the audience then was almost entirely of\r\nmen who looked uneasy as they left for home when the final curtain fell\r\nupon the shocking girls dressed as fairies. But the theatre did not\r\noften do so well; the people of the town were still too thrifty.\r\n\r\nThey were thrifty because they were the sons or grandsons of the "early\r\nsettlers," who had opened the wilderness and had reached it from the\r\nEast and the South with wagons and axes and guns, but with no money at\r\nall. The pioneers were thrifty or they would have perished: they had\r\nto store away food for the winter, or goods to trade for food, and they\r\noften feared they had not stored enough--they left traces of that fear\r\nin their sons and grandsons. In the minds of most of these, indeed,\r\ntheir thrift was next to their religion: to save, even for the sake\r\nof saving, was their earliest lesson and discipline. No matter how\r\nprosperous they were, they could not spend money either upon "art," or\r\nupon mere luxury and entertainment, without a sense of sin.\r\n\r\nAgainst so homespun a background the magnificence of the Ambersons was\r\nas conspicuous as a brass band at a funeral. Major Amberson bought two\r\nhundred acres of land at the end of National Avenue; and through this\r\ntract he built broad streets and cross-streets; paved them with cedar\r\nblock, and curbed them with stone. He set up fountains, here and there,\r\nwhere the streets intersected, and at symmetrical intervals placed\r\ncast-iron statues, painted white, with their titles clear upon the\r\npedestals: Minerva, Mercury, Hercules, Venus, Gladiator, Emperor\r\nAugustus, Fisher Boy, Stag-hound, Mastiff, Greyhound, Fawn, Antelope,\r\nWounded Doe, and Wounded Lion. Most of the forest trees had been left to\r\nflourish still, and, at some distance, or by moonlight, the place was\r\nin truth beautiful; but the ardent citizen, loving to see his city grow,\r\nwanted neither distance nor moonlight. He had not seen Versailles, but,\r\nstanding before the Fountain of Neptune in Amberson Addition, at bright\r\nnoon, and quoting the favourite comparison of the local newspapers,\r\nhe declared Versailles outdone. All this Art showed a profit from the\r\nstart, for the lots sold well and there was something like a rush\r\nto build in the new Addition. Its main thoroughfare, an oblique\r\ncontinuation of National Avenue, was called Amberson Boulevard, and\r\nhere, at the juncture of the new Boulevard and the Avenue, Major\r\nAmberson reserved four acres for himself, and built his new house--the\r\nAmberson Mansion, of course.\r\n\r\nThis house was the pride of the town. Faced with stone as far back\r\nas the dining-room windows, it was a house of arches and turrets and\r\ngirdling stone porches: it had the first porte-cochere seen in that\r\ntown. There was a central "front hall" with a great black walnut\r\nstairway, and open to a green glass skylight called the "dome," three\r\nstories above the ground floor. A ballroom occupied most of the\r\nthird story; and at one end of it was a carved walnut gallery for the\r\nmusicians. Citizens told strangers that the cost of all this black\r\nwalnut and wood-carving was sixty thousand dollars. "Sixty thousand\r\ndollars for the wood-work alone! Yes, sir, and hardwood floors all over\r\nthe house! Turkish rugs and no carpets at all, except a Brussels carpet\r\nin the front parlour--I hear they call it the \'reception-room.\' Hot and\r\ncold water upstairs and down, and stationary washstands in every last\r\nbedroom in the place! Their sideboard\'s built right into the house and\r\ngoes all the way across one end of the dining room. It isn\'t walnut,\r\nit\'s solid mahogany! Not veneering--solid mahogany! Well, sir, I presume\r\nthe President of the United States would be tickled to swap the\r\nWhite House for the new Amberson Mansion, if the Major\'d give him the\r\nchance--but by the Almighty Dollar, you bet your sweet life the Major\r\nwouldn\'t!"\r\n\r\nThe visitor to the town was certain to receive further enlightenment,\r\nfor there was one form of entertainment never omitted: he was always\r\npatriotically taken for "a little drive around our city," even if his\r\nhost had to hire a hack, and the climax of the display was the Amberson\r\nMansion. "Look at that greenhouse they\'ve put up there in the side\r\nyard," the escort would continue. "And look at that brick stable! Most\r\nfolks would think that stable plenty big enough and good enough to live\r\nin; it\'s got running water and four rooms upstairs for two hired men and\r\none of \'em\'s family to live in. They keep one hired man loafin\' in the\r\nhouse, and they got a married hired man out in the stable, and his wife\r\ndoes the washing. They got box-stalls for four horses, and they keep\r\na coupay, and some new kinds of fancy rigs you never saw the beat of!\r\n\'Carts\' they call two of \'em--\'way up in the air they are--too high for\r\nme! I guess they got every new kind of fancy rig in there that\'s been\r\ninvented. And harness--well, everybody in town can tell when Ambersons\r\nare out driving after dark, by the jingle. This town never did see so\r\nmuch style as Ambersons are putting on, these days; and I guess it\'s\r\ngoing to be expensive, because a lot of other folks\'ll try to keep up\r\nwith \'em. The Major\'s wife and the daughter\'s been to Europe, and my\r\nwife tells me since they got back they make tea there every afternoon\r\nabout five o\'clock, and drink it. Seems to me it would go against a\r\nperson\'s stomach, just before supper like that, and anyway tea isn\'t fit\r\nfor much--not unless you\'re sick or something. My wife says Ambersons\r\ndon\'t make lettuce salad the way other people do; they don\'t chop it\r\nup with sugar and vinegar at all. They pour olive oil on it with their\r\nvinegar, and they have it separate--not along with the rest of the meal.\r\nAnd they eat these olives, too: green things they are, something like a\r\nhard plum, but a friend of mine told me they tasted a good deal like a\r\nbad hickory-nut. My wife says she\'s going to buy some; you got to eat\r\nnine and then you get to like \'em, she says. Well, I wouldn\'t eat nine\r\nbad hickory-nuts to get to like them, and I\'m going to let these olives\r\nalone. Kind of a woman\'s dish, anyway, I suspect, but most everybody\'ll\r\nbe makin\' a stagger to worm through nine of \'em, now Ambersons brought\r\n\'em to town. Yes, sir, the rest\'ll eat \'em, whether they get sick or\r\nnot! Looks to me like some people in this city\'d be willing to go crazy\r\nif they thought that would help \'em to be as high-toned as Ambersons.\r\nOld Aleck Minafer--he\'s about the closest old codger we got--he come\r\nin my office the other day, and he pretty near had a stroke tellin\' me\r\nabout his daughter Fanny. Seems Miss Isabel Amberson\'s got some kind of\r\na dog--they call it a Saint Bernard--and Fanny was bound to have one,\r\ntoo. Well, old Aleck told her he didn\'t like dogs except rat-terriers,\r\nbecause a rat-terrier cleans up the mice, but she kept on at him, and\r\nfinally he said all right she could have one. Then, by George! she says\r\nAmbersons bought their dog, and you can\'t get one without paying for it:\r\nthey cost from fifty to a hundred dollars up! Old Aleck wanted to know\r\nif I ever heard of anybody buyin\' a dog before, because, of course, even\r\na Newfoundland or a setter you can usually get somebody to give you one.\r\nHe says he saw some sense in payin\' a nigger a dime, or even a\r\nquarter, to drown a dog for you, but to pay out fifty dollars and maybe\r\nmore--well, sir, he like to choked himself to death, right there in\r\nmy office! Of course everybody realizes that Major Amberson is a fine\r\nbusiness man, but what with throwin\' money around for dogs, and every\r\nwhich and what, some think all this style\'s bound to break him up, if\r\nhis family don\'t quit!"\r\n\r\nOne citizen, having thus discoursed to a visitor, came to a thoughtful\r\npause, and then added, "Does seem pretty much like squandering, yet when\r\nyou see that dog out walking with this Miss Isabel, he seems worth the\r\nmoney."\r\n\r\n"What\'s she look like?"\r\n\r\n"Well, sir," said the citizen, "she\'s not more than just about eighteen\r\nor maybe nineteen years old, and I don\'t know as I know just how to put\r\nit--but she\'s kind of a delightful lookin\' young lady!"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter II\r\n\r\n\r\nAnother citizen said an eloquent thing about Miss Isabel Amberson\'s\r\nlooks. This was Mrs. Henry Franklin Foster, the foremost literary\r\nauthority and intellectual leader of the community---for both the daily\r\nnewspapers thus described Mrs. Foster when she founded the Women\'s\r\nTennyson Club; and her word upon art, letters, and the drama was\r\naccepted more as law than as opinion. Naturally, when "Hazel Kirke"\r\nfinally reached the town, after its long triumph in larger places, many\r\npeople waited to hear what Mrs. Henry Franklin Foster thought of it\r\nbefore they felt warranted in expressing any estimate of the play. In\r\nfact, some of them waited in the lobby of the theatre, as they came out,\r\nand formed an inquiring group about her.\r\n\r\n"I didn\'t see the play," she informed them.\r\n\r\n"What! Why, we saw you, right in the middle of the fourth row!"\r\n\r\n"Yes," she said, smiling, "but I was sitting just behind Isabelle\r\nAmberson. I couldn\'t look at anything except her wavy brown hair and the\r\nwonderful back of her neck."\r\n\r\nThe ineligible young men of the town (they were all ineligible) were\r\nunable to content themselves with the view that had so charmed Mrs.\r\nHenry Franklin Foster: they spent their time struggling to keep Miss\r\nAmberson\'s face turned toward them. She turned it most often, observers\r\nsaid, toward two: one excelling in the general struggle by his sparkle,\r\nand the other by that winning if not winsome old trait, persistence. The\r\nsparkling gentleman "led germans" with her, and sent sonnets to her with\r\nhis bouquets--sonnets lacking neither music nor wit. He was generous,\r\npoor, well-dressed, and his amazing persuasiveness was one reason why\r\nhe was always in debt. No one doubted that he would be able to persuade\r\nIsabel, but he unfortunately joined too merry a party one night, and,\r\nduring a moonlight serenade upon the lawn before the Amberson Mansion,\r\nwas easily identified from the windows as the person who stepped through\r\nthe bass viol and had to be assisted to a waiting carriage. One of Miss\r\nAmberson\'s brothers was among the serenaders, and, when the party\r\nhad dispersed, remained propped against the front door in a state\r\nof helpless liveliness; the Major going down in a dressing-gown and\r\nslippers to bring him in, and scolding mildly, while imperfectly\r\nconcealing strong impulses to laughter. Miss Amberson also laughed\r\nat this brother, the next day, but for the suitor it was a different\r\nmatter: she refused to see him when he called to apologize. "You seem to\r\ncare a great deal about bass viols!" he wrote her. "I promise never\r\nto break another." She made no response to the note, unless it was an\r\nanswer, two weeks later, when her engagement was announced. She took the\r\npersistent one, Wilbur Minafer, no breaker of bass viols or of hearts,\r\nno serenader at all.\r\n\r\nA few people, who always foresaw everything, claimed that they were not\r\nsurprised, because though Wilbur Minafer "might not be an Apollo, as it\r\nwere," he was "a steady young business man, and a good church-goer," and\r\nIsabel Amberson was "pretty sensible--for such a showy girl." But the\r\nengagement astounded the young people, and most of their fathers and\r\nmothers, too; and as a topic it supplanted literature at the next\r\nmeeting of the "Women\'s Tennyson Club."\r\n\r\n"Wilbur Minafer!" a member cried, her inflection seeming to imply that\r\nWilbur\'s crime was explained by his surname. "Wilbur Minafer! It\'s the\r\nqueerest thing I ever heard! To think of her taking Wilbur Minafer, just\r\nbecause a man any woman would like a thousand times better was a little\r\nwild one night at a serenade!"\r\n\r\n"No," said Mrs. Henry Franklin Foster. "It isn\'t that. It isn\'t even\r\nbecause she\'s afraid he\'d be a dissipated husband and she wants to be\r\nsafe. It isn\'t because she\'s religious or hates wildness; it isn\'t even\r\nbecause she hates wildness in him."\r\n\r\n"Well, but look how she\'s thrown him over for it."\r\n\r\n"No, that wasn\'t her reason," said the wise Mrs. Henry Franklin Foster.\r\n"If men only knew it--and it\'s a good thing they don\'t--a woman doesn\'t\r\nreally care much about whether a man\'s wild or not, if it doesn\'t affect\r\nherself, and Isabel Amberson doesn\'t care a thing!"\r\n\r\n"Mrs. Foster!"\r\n\r\n"No, she doesn\'t. What she minds is his making a clown of himself in\r\nher front yard! It made her think he didn\'t care much about her. She\'s\r\nprobably mistaken, but that\'s what she thinks, and it\'s too late for\r\nher to think anything else now, because she\'s going to be married\r\nright away--the invitations will be out next week. It\'ll be a big\r\nAmberson-style thing, raw oysters floating in scooped-out blocks of\r\nice and a band from out-of-town--champagne, showy presents; a colossal\r\npresent from the Major. Then Wilbur will take Isabel on the carefulest\r\nlittle wedding trip he can manage, and she\'ll be a good wife to him, but\r\nthey\'ll have the worst spoiled lot of children this town will ever see."\r\n\r\n"How on earth do you make that out, Mrs. Foster?"\r\n\r\n"She couldn\'t love Wilbur, could she?" Mrs. Foster demanded, with no\r\nchallengers. "Well, it will all go to her children, and she\'ll ruin\r\n\'em!"\r\n\r\nThe prophetess proved to be mistaken in a single detail merely: except\r\nfor that, her foresight was accurate. The wedding was of Ambersonian\r\nmagnificence, even to the floating oysters; and the Major\'s colossal\r\npresent was a set of architect\'s designs for a house almost as elaborate\r\nand impressive as the Mansion, the house to be built in Amberson\r\nAddition by the Major. The orchestra was certainly not that local\r\none which had suffered the loss of a bass viol; the musicians came,\r\naccording to the prophecy and next morning\'s paper, from afar; and at\r\nmidnight the bride was still being toasted in champagne, though she had\r\ndeparted upon her wedding journey at ten. Four days later the pair had\r\nreturned to town, which promptness seemed fairly to demonstrate that\r\nWilbur had indeed taken Isabel upon the carefulest little trip he could\r\nmanage. According to every report, she was from the start "a good wife\r\nto him," but here in a final detail the prophecy proved inaccurate.\r\nWilbur and Isabel did not have children; they had only one.\r\n\r\n"Only one," Mrs. Henry Franklin Foster admitted. "But I\'d like to know\r\nif he isn\'t spoiled enough for a whole carload!"\r\n\r\nAgain she found none to challenge her.\r\n\r\nAt the age of nine, George Amberson Minafer, the Major\'s one grandchild,\r\nwas a princely terror, dreaded not only in Amberson Addition but in many\r\nother quarters through which he galloped on his white pony. "By golly,\r\nI guess you think you own this town!" an embittered labourer complained,\r\none day, as Georgie rode the pony straight through a pile of sand the\r\nman was sieving. "I will when I grow up," the undisturbed child replied.\r\n"I guess my grandpa owns it now, you bet!" And the baffled workman,\r\nhaving no means to controvert what seemed a mere exaggeration of the\r\nfacts could only mutter "Oh, pull down your vest!"\r\n\r\n"Don\'t haf to! Doctor says it ain\'t healthy!" the boy returned promptly.\r\n"But I\'ll tell you what I\'ll do: I\'ll pull down my vest if you\'ll wipe\r\noff your chin!"\r\n\r\nThis was stock and stencil: the accustomed argot of street badinage of\r\nthe period; and in such matters Georgie was an expert. He had no vest\r\nto pull down; the incongruous fact was that a fringed sash girdled the\r\njuncture of his velvet blouse and breeches, for the Fauntleroy period\r\nhad set in, and Georgie\'s mother had so poor an eye for appropriate\r\nthings, where Georgie was concerned, that she dressed him according to\r\nthe doctrine of that school in boy decoration. Not only did he wear a\r\nsilk sash, and silk stockings, and a broad lace collar, with his little\r\nblack velvet suit: he had long brown curls, and often came home with\r\nburrs in them.\r\n\r\nExcept upon the surface (which was not his own work, but his mother\'s)\r\nGeorgie bore no vivid resemblance to the fabulous little Cedric.\r\nThe storied boy\'s famous "Lean on me, grandfather," would have been\r\ndifficult to imagine upon the lips of Georgie. A month after his ninth\r\nbirthday anniversary, when the Major gave him his pony, he had already\r\nbecome acquainted with the toughest boys in various distant parts of\r\nthe town, and had convinced them that the toughness of a rich little boy\r\nwith long curls might be considered in many respects superior to their\r\nown. He fought them, learning how to go berserk at a certain point in a\r\nfight, bursting into tears of anger, reaching for rocks, uttering wailed\r\nthreats of murder and attempting to fulfil them. Fights often led to\r\nintimacies, and he acquired the art of saying things more exciting than\r\n"Don\'t haf to!" and "Doctor says it ain\'t healthy!" Thus, on a summer\r\nafternoon, a strange boy, sitting bored upon the gate-post of the\r\nReverend Malloch Smith, beheld George Amberson Minafer rapidly\r\napproaching on his white pony, and was impelled by bitterness to shout:\r\n"Shoot the ole jackass! Look at the girly curls! Say, bub, where\'d you\r\nsteal your mother\'s ole sash!"\r\n\r\n"Your sister stole it for me!" Georgie instantly replied, checking the\r\npony. "She stole it off our clo\'es-line an\' gave it to me."\r\n\r\n"You go get your hair cut!" said the stranger hotly. "Yah! I haven\'t got\r\nany sister!"\r\n\r\n"I know you haven\'t at home," Georgie responded. "I mean the one that\'s\r\nin jail."\r\n\r\n"I dare you to get down off that pony!"\r\n\r\nGeorgie jumped to the ground, and the other boy descended from the\r\nReverend Mr. Smith\'s gatepost--but he descended inside the gate. "I dare\r\nyou outside that gate," said Georgie.\r\n\r\n"Yah! I dare you half way here. I dare you--"\r\n\r\nBut these were luckless challenges, for Georgie immediately vaulted\r\nthe fence--and four minutes later Mrs. Malloch Smith, hearing strange\r\nnoises, looked forth from a window; then screamed, and dashed for the\r\npastor\'s study. Mr. Malloch Smith, that grim-bearded Methodist, came to\r\nthe front yard and found his visiting nephew being rapidly prepared by\r\nMaster Minafer to serve as a principal figure in a pageant of massacre.\r\nIt was with great physical difficulty that Mr. Smith managed to give\r\nhis nephew a chance to escape into the house, for Georgie was hard and\r\nquick, and, in such matters, remarkably intense; but the minister, after\r\na grotesque tussle, got him separated from his opponent, and shook him.\r\n\r\n"You stop that, you!" Georgie cried fiercely; and wrenched himself away.\r\n"I guess you don\'t know who I am!"\r\n\r\n"Yes, I do know!" the angered Mr. Smith retorted. "I know who you are,\r\nand you\'re a disgrace to your mother! Your mother ought to be ashamed of\r\nherself to allow--"\r\n\r\n"Shut up about my mother bein\' ashamed of herself!"\r\n\r\nMr. Smith, exasperated, was unable to close the dialogue with dignity.\r\n"She ought to be ashamed," he repeated. "A woman that lets a bad boy\r\nlike you--"\r\n\r\nBut Georgie had reached his pony and mounted. Before setting off at his\r\naccustomed gallop, he paused to interrupt the Reverend Malloch Smith\r\nagain. "You pull down your vest, you ole Billygoat, you!" he shouted,\r\ndistinctly. "Pull down your vest, wipe off your chin--an\' go to hell!"\r\n\r\nSuch precocity is less unusual, even in children of the Rich, than most\r\ngrown people imagine. However, it was a new experience for the Reverend\r\nMalloch Smith, and left him in a state of excitement. He at once wrote a\r\nnote to Georgie\'s mother, describing the crime according to his nephew\'s\r\ntestimony; and the note reached Mrs. Minafer before Georgie did. When he\r\ngot home she read it to him sorrowfully.\r\n\r\nDear Madam: Your son has caused a painful distress in my household. He\r\nmade an unprovoked attack upon a little nephew of mine who is visiting\r\nin my household, insulted him by calling him vicious names and\r\nfalsehoods, stating that ladies of his family were in jail. He then\r\ntried to make his pony kick him, and when the child, who is only eleven\r\nyears old, while your son is much older and stronger, endeavoured to\r\navoid his indignities and withdraw quietly, he pursued him into the\r\nenclosure of my property and brutally assaulted him. When I appeared\r\nupon this scene he deliberately called insulting words to me, concluding\r\nwith profanity, such as "go to hell," which was heard not only by myself\r\nbut by my wife and the lady who lives next door. I trust such a state of\r\nundisciplined behaviour may be remedied for the sake of the reputation\r\nfor propriety, if nothing higher, of the family to which this unruly\r\nchild belongs.\r\n\r\n\r\nGeorgie had muttered various interruptions, and as she concluded the\r\nreading he said: "He\'s an ole liar!"\r\n\r\n"Georgie, you mustn\'t say \'liar.\' Isn\'t this letter the truth?"\r\n\r\n"Well," said Georgie, "how old am I?"\r\n\r\n"Ten."\r\n\r\n"Well, look how he says I\'m older than a boy eleven years old."\r\n\r\n"That\'s true," said Isabel. "He does. But isn\'t some of it true,\r\nGeorgie?"\r\n\r\nGeorgie felt himself to be in a difficulty here, and he was silent.\r\n\r\n"Georgie, did you say what he says you did?"\r\n\r\n"Which one?"\r\n\r\n"Did you tell him to--to--Did you say, \'Go to hell?"\r\n\r\nGeorgie looked worried for a moment longer; then he brightened. "Listen\r\nhere, mamma; grandpa wouldn\'t wipe his shoe on that ole story-teller,\r\nwould he?"\r\n\r\n"Georgie, you mustn\'t--"\r\n\r\n"I mean: none of the Ambersons wouldn\'t have anything to do with him,\r\nwould they? He doesn\'t even know you, does he, mamma?"\r\n\r\n"That hasn\'t anything to do with it."\r\n\r\n"Yes, it has! I mean: none of the Amberson family go to see him, and\r\nthey never have him come in their house; they wouldn\'t ask him to, and\r\nthey prob\'ly wouldn\'t even let him."\r\n\r\n"That isn\'t what we\'re talking about."\r\n\r\n"I bet," said Georgie emphatically, "I bet if he wanted to see any of\r\n\'em, he\'d haf to go around to the side door!"\r\n\r\n"No, dear, they--"\r\n\r\n"Yes, they would, mamma! So what does it matter if I did say somep\'m\' to\r\nhim he didn\'t like? That kind o\' people, I don\'t see why you can\'t say\r\nanything you want to, to \'em!"\r\n\r\n"No, Georgie. And you haven\'t answered me whether you said that dreadful\r\nthing he says you did."\r\n\r\n"Well--" said Georgie. "Anyway, he said somep\'m\' to me that made me\r\nmad." And upon this point he offered no further details; he would not\r\nexplain to his mother that what had made him "mad" was Mr. Smith\'s hasty\r\ncondemnation of herself: "Your mother ought to be ashamed," and, "A\r\nwoman that lets a bad boy like you--" Georgie did not even consider\r\nexcusing himself by quoting these insolences.\r\n\r\nIsabel stroked his head. "They were terrible words for you to use, dear.\r\nFrom his letter he doesn\'t seem a very tactful person, but--"\r\n\r\n"He\'s just riffraff," said Georgie.\r\n\r\n"You mustn\'t say so," his mother gently agreed "Where did you learn\r\nthose bad words he speaks of? Where did you hear any one use them?"\r\n\r\n"Well, I\'ve heard \'em several places. I guess Uncle George Amberson was\r\nthe first I ever heard say \'em. Uncle George Amberson said \'em to papa\r\nonce. Papa didn\'t like it, but Uncle George was just laughin\' at papa,\r\nan\' then he said \'em while he was laughin\'."\r\n\r\n"That was wrong of him," she said, but almost instinctively he detected\r\nthe lack of conviction in her tone. It was Isabel\'s great failing that\r\nwhatever an Amberson did seemed right to her, especially if the Amberson\r\nwas either her brother George, or her son George. She knew that she\r\nshould be more severe with the latter now, but severity with him was\r\nbeyond her power; and the Reverend Malloch Smith had succeeded only\r\nin rousing her resentment against himself. Georgie\'s symmetrical\r\nface--altogether an Amberson face--had looked never more beautiful to\r\nher. It always looked unusually beautiful when she tried to be severe\r\nwith him. "You must promise me," she said feebly, "never to use those\r\nbad words again."\r\n\r\n"I promise not to," he said promptly--and he whispered an immediate\r\ncodicil under his breath: "Unless I get mad at somebody!" This satisfied\r\na code according to which, in his own sincere belief, he never told\r\nlies.\r\n\r\n"That\'s a good boy," she said, and he ran out to the yard, his\r\npunishment over. Some admiring friends were gathered there; they had\r\nheard of his adventure, knew of the note, and were waiting to see what\r\nwas going to "happen" to him. They hoped for an account of things, and\r\nalso that he would allow them to "take turns" riding his pony to the end\r\nof the alley and back.\r\n\r\nThey were really his henchmen: Georgie was a lord among boys. In fact,\r\nhe was a personage among certain sorts of grown people, and was often\r\nfawned upon; the alley negroes delighted in him, chuckled over him,\r\nflattered him slavishly. For that matter, he often heard well-dressed\r\npeople speaking of him admiringly: a group of ladies once gathered\r\nabout him on the pavement where he was spinning a top. "I know this\r\nis Georgie!" one exclaimed, and turned to the others with the\r\nimpressiveness of a showman. "Major Amberson\'s only grandchild!" The\r\nothers said, "It is?" and made clicking sounds with their mouths; two of\r\nthem loudly whispering, "So handsome!"\r\n\r\nGeorgie, annoyed because they kept standing upon the circle he had\r\nchalked for his top, looked at them coldly and offered a suggestion:\r\n\r\n"Oh, go hire a hall!"\r\n\r\nAs an Amberson, he was already a public character, and the story of\r\nhis adventure in the Reverend Malloch Smith\'s front yard became a town\r\ntopic. Many people glanced at him with great distaste, thereafter, when\r\nthey chanced to encounter him, which meant nothing to Georgie, because\r\nhe innocently believed most grown people to be necessarily cross-looking\r\nas a normal phenomenon resulting from the adult state; and he failed to\r\ncomprehend that the distasteful glances had any personal bearing upon\r\nhimself. If he had perceived such a bearing, he would have been affected\r\nonly so far, probably, as to mutter, "Riffraff!" Possibly he would have\r\nshouted it; and, certainly, most people believed a story that went round\r\nthe town just after Mrs. Amberson\'s funeral, when Georgie was eleven.\r\nGeorgie was reported to have differed with the undertaker about the\r\nseating of the family; his indignant voice had become audible: "Well,\r\nwho is the most important person at my own grandmother\'s funeral?"\r\nAnd later he had projected his head from the window of the foremost\r\nmourners\' carriage, as the undertaker happened to pass.\r\n\r\n"Riffraff!"\r\n\r\nThere were people--grown people they were--who expressed themselves\r\nlongingly: they did hope to live to see the day, they said, when that\r\nboy would get his come-upance! (They used that honest word, so much\r\nbetter than "deserts," and not until many years later to be more\r\nclumsily rendered as "what is coming to him.") Something was bound to\r\ntake him down, some day, and they only wanted to be there! But Georgie\r\nheard nothing of this, and the yearners for his taking down went\r\nunsatisfied, while their yearning grew the greater as the happy day\r\nof fulfilment was longer and longer postponed. His grandeur was not\r\ndiminished by the Malloch Smith story; the rather it was increased, and\r\namong other children (especially among little girls) there was added to\r\nthe prestige of his gilded position that diabolical glamour which must\r\ninevitably attend a boy who has told a minister to go to hell.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter III\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nUntil he reached the age of twelve, Georgie\'s education was a domestic\r\nprocess; tutors came to the house; and those citizens who yearned for\r\nhis taking down often said: "Just wait till he has to go to public\r\nschool; then he\'ll get it!" But at twelve Georgie was sent to a private\r\nschool in the town, and there came from this small and dependent\r\ninstitution no report, or even rumour, of Georgie\'s getting anything\r\nthat he was thought to deserve; therefore the yearning still persisted,\r\nthough growing gaunt with feeding upon itself. For, although Georgie\'s\r\npomposities and impudence in the little school were often almost\r\nunbearable, the teachers were fascinated by him. They did not like\r\nhim--he was too arrogant for that--but he kept them in such a state of\r\nemotion that they thought more about him than they did about all of the\r\nother ten pupils. The emotion he kept them in was usually one resulting\r\nfrom injured self-respect, but sometimes it was dazzled admiration. So\r\nfar as their conscientious observation went, he "studied" his lessons\r\nsparingly; but sometimes, in class, he flashed an admirable answer, with\r\na comprehension not often shown by the pupils they taught; and he passed\r\nhis examinations easily. In all, without discernible effort, he acquired\r\nat this school some rudiments of a liberal education and learned nothing\r\nwhatever about himself.\r\n\r\nThe yearners were still yearning when Georgie, at sixteen, was sent\r\naway to a great "Prep School." "Now," they said brightly, "he\'ll get it!\r\nHe\'ll find himself among boys just as important in their home towns as\r\nhe is, and they\'ll knock the stuffing out of him when he puts on his\r\nairs with them! Oh, but that would be worth something to see!" They were\r\nmistaken, it appeared, for when Georgie returned, a few months later,\r\nhe still seemed to have the same stuffing. He had been deported by the\r\nauthorities, the offense being stated as "insolence and profanity";\r\nin fact, he had given the principal of the school instructions almost\r\nidentical with those formerly objected to by the Reverend Malloch Smith.\r\n\r\nBut he had not got his come-upance, and those who counted upon it\r\nwere embittered by his appearance upon the down-town streets driving\r\na dog-cart at criminal speed, making pedestrians retreat from the\r\ncrossings, and behaving generally as if he "owned the earth." A\r\ndisgusted hardware dealer of middle age, one of those who hungered for\r\nGeorgie\'s downfall, was thus driven back upon the sidewalk to avoid\r\nbeing run over, and so far forgot himself as to make use of the pet\r\nstreet insult of the year: "Got \'ny sense! See here, bub, does your\r\nmother know you\'re out?"\r\n\r\nGeorgie, without even seeming to look at him, flicked the long lash of\r\nhis whip dexterously, and a little spurt of dust came from the hardware\r\nman\'s trousers, not far below the waist. He was not made of hardware:\r\nhe raved, looking for a missile; then, finding none, commanded himself\r\nsufficiently to shout after the rapid dog-cart: "Turn down your pants,\r\nyou would-be dude! Raining in dear ole Lunnon! Git off the earth!"\r\n\r\nGeorgie gave him no encouragement to think that he was heard. The\r\ndog-cart turned the next corner, causing indignation there, likewise,\r\nand, having proceeded some distance farther, halted in front of the\r\n"Amberson Block"--an old-fashioned four-story brick warren of lawyers\r\noffices, insurance and realestate offices, with a "drygoods store"\r\noccupying the ground floor. Georgie tied his lathered trotter to\r\na telegraph pole, and stood for a moment looking at the building\r\ncritically: it seemed shabby, and he thought his grandfather ought to\r\nreplace it with a fourteen-story skyscraper, or even a higher one, such\r\nas he had lately seen in New York--when he stopped there for a few days\r\nof recreation and rest on his way home from the bereaved school. About\r\nthe entryway to the stairs were various tin signs, announcing the\r\noccupation and location of upper-floor tenants, and Georgie decided to\r\ntake some of these with him if he should ever go to college. However,\r\nhe did not stop to collect them at this time, but climbed the worn\r\nstairs--there was no elevator--to the fourth floor, went down a dark\r\ncorridor, and rapped three times upon a door. It was a mysterious door,\r\nits upper half, of opaque glass, bearing no sign to state the business\r\nor profession of the occupants within; but overhead, upon the lintel,\r\nfour letters had been smearingly inscribed, partly with purple ink and\r\npartly with a soft lead pencil, "F. O. T. A." and upon the plaster wall,\r\nabove the lintel, there was a drawing dear to male adolescence: a skull\r\nand crossbones.\r\n\r\nThree raps, similar to Georgie\'s, sounded from within the room. Georgie\r\nthen rapped four times the rapper within the room rapped twice, and\r\nGeorgie rapped seven times. This ended precautionary measures; and a\r\nwell-dressed boy of sixteen opened the door; whereupon Georgie entered\r\nquickly, and the door was closed behind him. Seven boys of congenial\r\nage were seated in a semicircular row of damaged office chairs, facing a\r\nplatform whereon stood a solemn, red-haired young personage with a table\r\nbefore him. At one end of the room there was a battered sideboard, and\r\nupon it were some empty beer bottles, a tobacco can about two-thirds\r\nfull, with a web of mold over the surface of the tobacco, a dusty\r\ncabinet photograph (not inscribed) of Miss Lillian Russell, several\r\nwithered old pickles, a caseknife, and a half-petrified section of\r\nicing-cake on a sooty plate. At the other end of the room were two\r\nrickety card-tables and a stand of bookshelves where were displayed\r\nunder dust four or five small volumes of M. Guy de Maupassant\'s stories,\r\n"Robinson Crusoe," "Sappho," "Mr. Barnes of New York," a work by\r\nGiovanni Boccaccio, a Bible, "The Arabian Nights\' Entertainment,"\r\n"Studies of the Human Form Divine," "The Little Minister," and a clutter\r\nof monthly magazines and illustrated weeklies of about that crispness\r\none finds in such articles upon a doctor\'s ante-room table. Upon the\r\nwall, above the sideboard, was an old framed lithograph of Miss Della\r\nFox in "Wang"; over the bookshelves there was another lithograph\r\npurporting to represent Mr. John L. Sullivan in a boxing costume, and\r\nbeside it a halftone reproduction of "A Reading From Horner." The final\r\ndecoration consisted of damaged papiermache--a round shield with two\r\nbattle-axes and two cross-hilted swords, upon the wall over the little\r\nplatform where stood the red-haired presiding officer. He addressed\r\nGeorgie in a serious voice:\r\n\r\n"Welcome, Friend of the Ace."\r\n\r\n"Welcome, Friend of the Ace," Georgie responded, and all of the other\r\nboys repeated the words, "Welcome, Friend of the Ace."\r\n\r\n"Take your seat in the secret semicircle," said the presiding officer.\r\n"We will now proceed to--"\r\n\r\nBut Georgie was disposed to be informal. He interrupted, turning to\r\nthe boy who had admitted him: "Look here, Charlie Johnson, what\'s Fred\r\nKinney doing in the president\'s chair? That\'s my place, isn\'t it?\r\nWhat you men been up to here, anyhow? Didn\'t you all agree I was to be\r\npresident just the same, even if I was away at school?"\r\n\r\n"Well--" said Charlie Johnson uneasily. "Listen! I didn\'t have much to\r\ndo with it. Some of the other members thought that long as you weren\'t\r\nin town or anything, and Fred gave the sideboard, why--"\r\n\r\nMr. Kinney, presiding, held in his hand, in lieu of a gavel, and\r\nconsidered much more impressive, a Civil War relic known as a\r\n"horse-pistol." He rapped loudly for order. "All Friends of the Ace will\r\ntake their seats!" he said sharply. "I\'m president of the F. O. T. A.\r\nnow, George Minafer, and don\'t you forget it! You and Charlie Johnson\r\nsit down, because I was elected perfectly fair, and we\'re goin\' to hold\r\na meeting here."\r\n\r\n"Oh, you are, are you?" said George skeptically.\r\n\r\nCharlie Johnson thought to mollify him. "Well, didn\'t we call this\r\nmeeting just especially because you told us to? You said yourself we\r\nought to have a kind of celebration because you\'ve got back to town,\r\nGeorge, and that\'s what we\'re here for now, and everything. What do you\r\ncare about being president? All it amounts to is just calling the roll\r\nand--"\r\n\r\nThe president de facto hammered the table. "This meeting will now\r\nproceed to--"\r\n\r\n"No, it won\'t," said George, and he advanced to the desk, laughing\r\ncontemptuously. "Get off that platform."\r\n\r\n"This meeting will come to order!" Mr. Kinney commanded fiercely.\r\n\r\n"You put down that gavel," said George. "Whose is it, I\'d like to know?\r\nIt belongs to my grandfather, and you quit hammering it that way or\r\nyou\'ll break it, and I\'ll have to knock your head off."\r\n\r\n"This meeting will come to order! I was legally elected here, and I\'m\r\nnot going to be bulldozed!"\r\n\r\n"All right," said Georgie. "You\'re president. Now we\'ll hold another\r\nelection."\r\n\r\n"We will not!" Fred Kinney shouted. "We\'ll have our reg\'lar meeting,\r\nand then we\'ll play euchre & nickel a corner, what we\'re here for. This\r\nmeeting will now come to ord--"\r\n\r\nGeorgie addressed the members. "I\'d like to know who got up this thing\r\nin the first place," he said. "Who\'s the founder of the F.O.T.A., if you\r\nplease? Who got this room rent free? Who got the janitor to let us\r\nhave most of this furniture? You suppose you could keep this clubroom a\r\nminute if I told my grandfather I didn\'t want it for a literary club any\r\nmore? I\'d like to say a word on how you members been acting, too! When I\r\nwent away I said I didn\'t care if you had a vice-president or something\r\nwhile I was gone, but here I hardly turned my back and you had to go and\r\nelect Fred Kinney president! Well, if that\'s what you want, you can have\r\nit. I was going to have a little celebration down here some night pretty\r\nsoon, and bring some port wine, like we drink at school in our crowd\r\nthere, and I was going to get my grandfather to give the club an extra\r\nroom across the hall, and prob\'ly I could get my Uncle George to give us\r\nhis old billiard table, because he\'s got a new one, and the club could\r\nput it in the other room. Well, you got a new president now!" Here\r\nGeorgie moved toward the door and his tone became plaintive, though\r\nundeniably there was disdain beneath his sorrow. "I guess all I better\r\ndo is--resign!"\r\n\r\nAnd he opened the door, apparently intending to withdraw.\r\n\r\n"All in favour of having a new election," Charlie Johnson shouted\r\nhastily, "say, \'Aye\'!"\r\n\r\n"Aye" was said by everyone present except Mr. Kinney, who began a hot\r\nprotest, but it was immediately smothered.\r\n\r\n"All in favour of me being president instead of Fred Kinney," shouted\r\nGeorgie, "say \'Aye.\' The \'Ayes\' have it!"\r\n\r\n "I resign," said the red-headed boy, gulping as he descended from the\r\nplatform. "I resign from the club!"\r\n\r\nHot-eyed, he found his hat and departed, jeers echoing after him as he\r\nplunged down the corridor. Georgie stepped upon the platform, and took\r\nup the emblem of office.\r\n\r\n"Ole red-head Fred\'ll be around next week," said the new chairman.\r\n"He\'ll be around boot-lickin\' to get us to take him back in again, but I\r\nguess we don\'t want him: that fellow always was a trouble-maker. We will\r\nnow proceed with our meeting. Well, fellows, I suppose you want to hear\r\nfrom your president. I don\'t know that I have much to say, as I have\r\nalready seen most of you a few times since I got back. I had a good time\r\nat the old school, back East, but had a little trouble with the faculty\r\nand came on home. My family stood by me as well as I could ask, and I\r\nexpect to stay right here in the old town until whenever I decide to\r\nenter college. Now, I don\'t suppose there\'s any more business before the\r\nmeeting. I guess we might as well play cards. Anybody that\'s game for a\r\nlittle quarter-limit poker or any limit they say, why I\'d like to have\r\n\'em sit at the president\'s card-table."\r\n\r\nWhen the diversions of the Friends of the Ace were concluded for that\r\nafternoon, Georgie invited his chief supporter, Mr. Charlie Johnson, to\r\ndrive home with him to dinner, and as they jingled up National Avenue in\r\nthe dog-cart, Charlie asked:\r\n\r\n"What sort of men did you run up against at that school, George?"\r\n\r\n"Best crowd there: finest set of men I ever met."\r\n\r\n"How\'d you get in with \'em?"\r\n\r\nGeorgie laughed. "I let them get in with me, Charlie," he said in a tone\r\nof gentle explanation. "It\'s vulgar to do any other way. Did I tell you\r\nthe nickname they gave me--\'King\'? That was what they called me at that\r\nschool, \'King Minafer."\r\n\r\n"How\'d they happen to do that?" his friend asked innocently.\r\n\r\n"Oh, different things," George answered lightly. "Of course, any of\r\n\'em that came from anywhere out in this part the country knew about\r\nthe family and all that, and so I suppose it was a good deal on account\r\nof--oh, on account of the family and the way I do things, most likely."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter IV\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Mr. George Amberson Minafer came home for the holidays at\r\nChristmastide, in his sophomore year, probably no great change had taken\r\nplace inside him, but his exterior was visibly altered. Nothing about\r\nhim encouraged any hope that he had received his come-upance; on the\r\ncontrary, the yearners for that stroke of justice must yearn even\r\nmore itchingly: the gilded youth\'s manner had become polite, but his\r\npoliteness was of a kind which democratic people found hard to bear. In\r\na word, M. le Due had returned from the gay life of the capital to\r\nshow himself for a week among the loyal peasants belonging to the\r\nold chateau, and their quaint habits and costumes afforded him a mild\r\namusement.\r\n\r\nCards were out for a ball in his honour, and this pageant of the\r\ntenantry was held in the ballroom of the Amberson Mansion the night\r\nafter his arrival. It was, as Mrs. Henry Franklin Foster said of\r\nIsabel\'s wedding, "a big Amberson-style thing," though that wise Mrs.\r\nHenry Franklin Foster had long ago gone the way of all wisdom, having\r\nstepped out of the Midland town, unquestionably into heaven--a long\r\nstep, but not beyond her powers. She had successors, but no successor;\r\nthe town having grown too large to confess that it was intellectually\r\nled and literarily authoritated by one person; and some of these\r\nsuccessors were not invited to the ball, for dimensions were now so\r\nmetropolitan that intellectual leaders and literary authorities loomed\r\nin outlying regions unfamiliar to the Ambersons. However, all "old\r\ncitizens" recognizable as gentry received cards, and of course so did\r\ntheir dancing descendants.\r\n\r\nThe orchestra and the caterer were brought from away, in the Amberson\r\nmanner, though this was really a gesture--perhaps one more of habit\r\nthan of ostentation--for servitors of gaiety as proficient as these\r\nimportations were nowadays to be found in the town. Even flowers and\r\nplants and roped vines were brought from afar--not, however, until\r\nthe stock of the local florists proved insufficient to obliterate the\r\ninterior structure of the big house, in the Amberson way. It was\r\nthe last of the great, long remembered dances that "everybody talked\r\nabout"--there were getting to be so many people in town that no later\r\nthan the next year there were too many for "everybody" to hear of even\r\nsuch a ball as the Ambersons\'.\r\n\r\nGeorge, white-gloved, with a gardenia in his buttonhole, stood with his\r\nmother and the Major, embowered in the big red and gold drawing room\r\ndownstairs, to "receive" the guests; and, standing thus together, the\r\ntrio offered a picturesque example of good looks persistent through\r\nthree generations. The Major, his daughter, and his grandson were of a\r\ntype all Amberson: tall, straight, and regular, with dark eyes, short\r\nnoses, good chins; and the grandfather\'s expression, no less than\r\nthe grandson\'s, was one of faintly amused condescension. There was a\r\ndifference, however. The grandson\'s unlined young face had nothing to\r\noffer except this condescension; the grandfather\'s had other things to\r\nsay. It was a handsome, worldly old face, conscious of its importance,\r\nbut persuasive rather than arrogant, and not without tokens of\r\nsufferings withstood. The Major\'s short white hair was parted in the\r\nmiddle, like his grandson\'s, and in all he stood as briskly equipped to\r\nthe fashion as exquisite young George.\r\n\r\nIsabel, standing between her father and her son caused a vague amazement\r\nin the mind of the latter. Her age, just under forty, was for George\r\na thought of something as remote as the moons of Jupiter: he could not\r\npossibly have conceived such an age ever coming to be his own: five\r\nyears was the limit of his thinking in time. Five years ago he had been\r\na child not yet fourteen; and those five years were an abyss. Five years\r\nhence he would be almost twenty-four; what the girls he knew called "one\r\nof the older men." He could imagine himself at twenty-four, but beyond\r\nthat, his powers staggered and refused the task. He saw little essential\r\ndifference between thirty-eight and eighty-eight, and his mother was to\r\nhim not a woman but wholly a mother. He had no perception of her other\r\nthan as an adjunct to himself, his mother; nor could he imagine her\r\nthinking or doing anything--falling in love, walking with a friend, or\r\nreading a book--as a woman, and not as his mother. The woman, Isabel,\r\nwas a stranger to her son; as completely a stranger as if he had never\r\nin his life seen her or heard her voice. And it was to-night, while he\r\nstood with her, "receiving," that he caught a disquieting glimpse of\r\nthis stranger whom he thus fleetingly encountered for the first time.\r\n\r\nYouth cannot imagine romance apart from youth. That is why the roles of\r\nthe heroes and heroines of plays are given by the managers to the most\r\nyouthful actors they can find among the competent. Both middle-aged\r\npeople and young people enjoy a play about young lovers; but only\r\nmiddle-aged people will tolerate a play about middle-aged lovers; young\r\npeople will not come to see such a play, because, for them, middle-aged\r\nlovers are a joke--not a very funny one. Therefore, to bring both the\r\nmiddle-aged people and the young people into his house, the manager\r\nmakes his romance as young as he can. Youth will indeed be served, and\r\nits profound instinct is to be not only scornfully amused but vaguely\r\nangered by middle-age romance. So, standing beside his mother, George\r\nwas disturbed by a sudden impression, coming upon him out of nowhere,\r\nso far as he could detect, that her eyes were brilliant, that she was\r\ngraceful and youthful--in a word, that she was romantically lovely.\r\n\r\nHe had one of those curious moments that seem to have neither a\r\ncause nor any connection with actual things. While it lasted, he was\r\ndisquieted not by thoughts--for he had no definite thoughts--but by a\r\nslight emotion like that caused in a dream by the presence of something\r\ninvisible soundless, and yet fantastic. There was nothing different or\r\nnew about his mother, except her new black and silver dress: she was\r\nstanding there beside him, bending her head a little in her greetings,\r\nsmiling the same smile she had worn for the half-hour that people had\r\nbeen passing the "receiving" group. Her face was flushed, but the room\r\nwas warm; and shaking hands with so many people easily accounted for the\r\npretty glow that was upon her. At any time she could have "passed" for\r\ntwenty-five or twenty-six--a man of fifty would have honestly guessed\r\nher to be about thirty but possibly two or three years younger--and\r\nthough extraordinary in this, she had been extraordinary in it for\r\nyears. There was nothing in either her looks or her manner to explain\r\nGeorge\'s uncomfortable feeling; and yet it increased, becoming suddenly\r\na vague resentment, as if she had done something unmotherly to him.\r\n\r\nThe fantastic moment passed; and even while it lasted, he was doing his\r\nduty, greeting two pretty girls with whom he had grown up, as people\r\nsay, and warmly assuring them that he remembered them very well--an\r\nassurance which might have surprised them "in anybody but Georgie\r\nMinafer!" It seemed unnecessary, since he had spent many hours with\r\nthem no longer ago than the preceding August, They had with them their\r\nparents and an uncle from out of town; and George negligently gave the\r\nparents the same assurance he had given the daughters, but murmured\r\nanother form of greeting to the out-of-town uncle, whom he had\r\nnever seen before. This person George absently took note of as a\r\n"queer-looking duck." Undergraduates had not yet adopted "bird." It was\r\na period previous to that in which a sophomore would have thought of the\r\nSharon girls\' uncle as a "queer-looking bird," or, perhaps a "funny-face\r\nbird." In George\'s time, every human male was to be defined, at\r\npleasure, as a "duck"; but "duck" was not spoken with admiring\r\naffection, as in its former feminine use to signify a "dear"--on the\r\ncontrary, "duck" implied the speaker\'s personal detachment and humorous\r\nsuperiority. An indifferent amusement was what George felt when\r\nhis mother, with a gentle emphasis, interrupted his interchange of\r\ncourtesies with the nieces to present him to the queer-looking duck\r\ntheir uncle. This emphasis of Isabel\'s, though slight, enabled George\r\nto perceive that she considered the queer-looking duck a person of some\r\nimportance; but it was far from enabling him to understand why. The\r\nduck parted his thick and longish black hair on the side; his tie was\r\na forgetful looking thing, and his coat, though it fitted a good enough\r\nmiddle-aged figure, no product of this year, or of last year either.\r\nOne of his eyebrows was noticeably higher than the other; and there were\r\nwhimsical lines between them, which gave him an apprehensive expression;\r\nbut his apprehensions were evidently more humorous than profound, for\r\nhis prevailing look was that of a genial man of affairs, not much afraid\r\nof anything whatever Nevertheless, observing only his unfashionable\r\nhair, his eyebrows, his preoccupied tie and his old coat, the olympic\r\nGeorge set him down as a queer-looking duck, and having thus completed\r\nhis portrait, took no interest in him.\r\n\r\nThe Sharon girls passed on, taking the queer-looking duck with them, and\r\nGeorge became pink with mortification as his mother called his attention\r\nto a white-bearded guest waiting to shake his hand. This was George\'s\r\ngreat-uncle, old John Minafer: it was old John\'s boast that in spite\r\nof his connection by marriage with the Ambersons, he never had worn and\r\nnever would wear a swaller-tail coat. Members of his family had exerted\r\ntheir influence uselessly--at eighty-nine conservative people seldom\r\nform radical new habits, and old John wore his "Sunday suit" of black\r\nbroadcloth to the Amberson ball. The coat was square, with skirts to the\r\nknees; old John called it a "Prince Albert" and was well enough pleased\r\nwith it, but his great-nephew considered it the next thing to an insult.\r\nGeorge\'s purpose had been to ignore the man, but he had to take his\r\nhand for a moment; whereupon old John began to tell George that he was\r\nlooking well, though there had been a time, during his fourth month,\r\nwhen he was so puny that nobody thought he would live. The great-nephew,\r\nin a fury of blushes, dropped old John\'s hand with some vigour, and\r\nseized that of the next person in the line. "Member you v\'ry well\r\n\'ndeed!" he said fiercely.\r\n\r\nThe large room had filled, and so had the broad hall and the rooms\r\non the other side of the hall, where there were tables for whist. The\r\nimported orchestra waited in the ballroom on the third floor, but\r\na local harp, \'cello, violin, and flute were playing airs from "The\r\nFencing Master" in the hall, and people were shouting over the music.\r\nOld John Minafer\'s voice was louder and more penetrating than any other,\r\nbecause he had been troubled with deafness for twenty-five years, heard\r\nhis own voice but faintly, and liked to hear it. "Smell o\' flowers like\r\nthis always puts me in mind o\' funerals," he kept telling his niece,\r\nFanny Minafer, who was with him; and he seemed to get a great deal of\r\nsatisfaction out of this reminder. His tremulous yet strident voice\r\ncut through the voluminous sound that filled the room, and he was\r\nheard everywhere: "Always got to think o\' funerals when I smell so\r\nmany flowers!" And, as the pressure of people forced Fanny and himself\r\nagainst the white marble mantelpiece, he pursued this train of cheery\r\nthought, shouting, "Right here\'s where the Major\'s wife was laid out at\r\nher funeral. They had her in a good light from that big bow window."\r\nHe paused to chuckle mournfully. "I s\'pose that\'s where they\'ll put the\r\nMajor when his time comes."\r\n\r\nPresently George\'s mortification was increased to hear this sawmill\r\ndroning harshly from the midst of the thickening crowd: "Ain\'t the\r\ndancin\' broke out yet, Fanny? Hoopla! Le\'s push through and go see the\r\nyoung women-folks crack their heels! Start the circus! Hoopse-daisy!"\r\nMiss Fanny Minafer, in charge of the lively veteran, was almost as\r\ndistressed as her nephew George, but she did her duty and managed to get\r\nold John through the press and out to the broad stairway, which numbers\r\nof young people were now ascending to the ballroom. And here the sawmill\r\nvoice still rose over all others: "Solid black walnut every inch of it,\r\nbalustrades and all. Sixty thousand dollars\' worth o\' carved woodwork\r\nin the house! Like water! Spent money like water! Always did! Still do!\r\nLike water! God knows where it all comes from!"\r\n\r\nHe continued the ascent, barking and coughing among the gleaming young\r\nheads, white shoulders, jewels, and chiffon, like an old dog slowly\r\nswimming up the rapids of a sparkling river; while down below, in the\r\ndrawing room, George began to recover from the degradation into which\r\nthis relic of early settler days had dragged him. What restored him\r\ncompletely was a dark-eyed little beauty of nineteen, very knowing in\r\nlustrous blue and jet; at sight of this dashing advent in the line of\r\nguests before him, George was fully an Amberson again.\r\n\r\n"Remember you very well indeed!" he said, his graciousness more earnest\r\nthan any he had heretofore displayed. Isabel heard him and laughed.\r\n\r\n"But you don\'t, George!" she said. "You don\'t remember her yet, though\r\nof course you will! Miss Morgan is from out of town, and I\'m afraid this\r\nis the first time you\'ve ever seen her. You might take her up to the\r\ndancing; I think you\'ve pretty well done your duty here."\r\n\r\n"Be d\'lighted," George responded formally, and offered his arm, not with\r\na flourish, certainly, but with an impressiveness inspired partly by the\r\nappearance of the person to whom he offered it, partly by his being the\r\nhero of this fete, and partly by his youthfulness--for when manners are\r\nnew they are apt to be elaborate. The little beauty entrusted her gloved\r\nfingers to his coat-sleeve, and they moved away together.\r\n\r\nTheir progress was necessarily slow, and to George\'s mind it did not\r\nlack stateliness. How could it? Musicians, hired especially for him,\r\nwere sitting in a grove of palms in the hall and now tenderly playing\r\n"Oh, Promise Me" for his pleasuring; dozens and scores of flowers had\r\nbeen brought to life and tended to this hour that they might sweeten\r\nthe air for him while they died; and the evanescent power that music\r\nand floral scents hold over youth stirred his appreciation of strange,\r\nbeautiful qualities within his own bosom: he seemed to himself to be\r\nmysteriously angelic, and about to do something which would overwhelm\r\nthe beautiful young stranger upon his arm.\r\n\r\nElderly people and middle-aged people moved away to let him pass with\r\nhis honoured fair beside him. Worthy middle-class creatures, they\r\nseemed, leading dull lives but appreciative of better things when they\r\nsaw them--and George\'s bosom was fleetingly touched with a pitying\r\nkindness. And since the primordial day when caste or heritage first\r\nset one person, in his own esteem, above his fellow-beings, it is to\r\nbe doubted if anybody ever felt more illustrious, or more negligently\r\ngrand, than George Amberson Minafer felt at this party.\r\n\r\nAs he conducted Miss Morgan through the hall, toward the stairway, they\r\npassed the open double doors of a card room, where some squadrons of\r\nolder people were preparing for action, and, leaning gracefully upon\r\nthe mantelpiece of this room, a tall man, handsome, high-mannered, and\r\nsparklingly point-device, held laughing converse with that queer-looking\r\nduck, the Sharon girls\' uncle. The tall gentleman waved a gracious\r\nsalutation to George, and Miss Morgan\'s curiosity was stirred. "Who is\r\nthat?"\r\n\r\n"I didn\'t catch his name when my mother presented him to me," said\r\nGeorge. "You mean the queer-looking duck."\r\n\r\n"I mean the aristocratic duck."\r\n\r\n"That\'s my Uncle George Honourable George Amberson. I thought everybody\r\nknew him."\r\n\r\n"He looks as though everybody ought to know him," she said. "It seems to\r\nrun in your family."\r\n\r\nIf she had any sly intention, it skipped over George harmlessly. "Well,\r\nof course, I suppose most everybody does," he admitted--"out in this\r\npart of the country especially. Besides, Uncle George is in Congress;\r\nthe family like to have someone there."\r\n\r\n"Why?"\r\n\r\n"Well, it\'s sort of a good thing in one way. For instance, my Uncle\r\nSydney Amberson and his wife, Aunt Amelia, they haven\'t got much of\r\nanything to do with themselves--get bored to death around here, of\r\ncourse. Well, probably Uncle George\'ll have Uncle Sydney appointed\r\nminister or ambassador, or something like that, to Russia or Italy or\r\nsomewhere, and that\'ll make it pleasant when any of the rest of the\r\nfamily go travelling, or things like that. I expect to do a good deal of\r\ntravelling myself when I get out of college."\r\n\r\nOn the stairway he pointed out this prospective ambassadorial couple,\r\nSydney and Amelia. They were coming down, fronting the ascending tide,\r\nand as conspicuous over it as a king and queen in a play. Moreover,\r\nas the clear-eyed Miss Morgan remarked, the very least they looked was\r\nambassadorial. Sydney was an Amberson exaggerated, more pompous than\r\ngracious; too portly, flushed, starched to a shine, his stately\r\njowl furnished with an Edward the Seventh beard. Amelia, likewise\r\nfull-bodied, showed glittering blond hair exuberantly dressed; a pink,\r\nfat face cold under a white-hot tiara; a solid, cold bosom under a\r\nwhite-hot necklace; great, cold, gloved arms, and the rest of her\r\nbeautifully upholstered. Amelia was an Amberson born, herself, Sydney\'s\r\nsecond-cousin: they had no children, and Sydney was without a business\r\nor a profession; thus both found a great deal of time to think about the\r\nappropriateness of their becoming Excellencies. And as George ascended\r\nthe broad stairway, they were precisely the aunt and uncle he was most\r\npleased to point out, to a girl from out of town, as his appurtenances\r\nin the way of relatives. At sight of them the grandeur of the Amberson\r\nfamily was instantly conspicuous as a permanent thing: it was impossible\r\nto doubt that the Ambersons were entrenched, in their nobility and\r\nriches, behind polished and glittering barriers which were as solid as\r\nthey were brilliant, and would last.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter V\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nThe hero of the fete, with the dark-eyed little beauty upon his arm,\r\nreached the top of the second flight of stairs; and here, beyond a\r\nspacious landing, where two proud-like darkies tended a crystalline\r\npunch bowl, four wide archways in a rose-vine lattice framed gliding\r\nsilhouettes of waltzers, already smoothly at it to the castanets of\r\n"La Paloma." Old John Minafer, evidently surfeited, was in the act of\r\nleaving these delights. "D\'want \'ny more o\' that!" he barked. "Just\r\nslidin\' around! Call that dancin\'? Rather see a jig any day in the\r\nworld! They ain\'t very modest, some of \'em. I don\'t mind that, though.\r\nNot me!"\r\n\r\nMiss Fanny Minafer was no longer in charge of him: he emerged from the\r\nballroom escorted by a middle-aged man of commonplace appearance. The\r\nescort had a dry, lined face upon which, not ornamentally but as a\r\nmatter of course, there grew a business man\'s short moustache; and his\r\nthin neck showed an Adam\'s apple, but not conspicuously, for there\r\nwas nothing conspicuous about him. Baldish, dim, quiet, he was an\r\nunnoticeable part of this festival, and although there were a dozen or\r\nmore middle-aged men present, not casually to be distinguished from him\r\nin general aspect, he was probably the last person in the big house at\r\nwhom a stranger would have glanced twice. It did not enter George\'s mind\r\nto mention to Miss Morgan that this was his father, or to say anything\r\nwhatever about him.\r\n\r\nMr. Minafer shook his son\'s hand unobtrusively in passing.\r\n\r\n"I\'ll take Uncle John home," he said, in a low voice. "Then I guess\r\nI\'ll go on home myself--I\'m not a great hand at parties, you know.\r\nGood-night, George."\r\n\r\nGeorge murmured a friendly enough good-night without pausing. Ordinarily\r\nhe was not ashamed of the Minafers; he seldom thought about them at\r\nall, for he belonged, as most American children do, to the mother\'s\r\nfamily--but he was anxious not to linger with Miss Morgan in the\r\nvicinity of old John, whom he felt to be a disgrace.\r\n\r\nHe pushed brusquely through the fringe of calculating youths who were\r\ngathered in the arches, watching for chances to dance only with girls\r\nwho would soon be taken off their hands, and led his stranger lady out\r\nupon the floor. They caught the time instantly, and were away in the\r\nwaltz.\r\n\r\nGeorge danced well, and Miss Morgan seemed to float as part of the\r\nmusic, the very dove itself of "La Paloma."
