She\r\nlooked instantly away, and, turning, ran out from the palm grove, where\r\nthey stood, to the dancing-floor.\r\n\r\n"Come on!" she cried. "Let\'s dance!"\r\n\r\nHe followed her.\r\n\r\n"See here--I--I--" he stammered. "You mean--Do you--"\r\n\r\n"No, no!" she laughed. "Let\'s dance!"\r\n\r\nHe put his arm about her almost tremulously, and they began to waltz. It\r\nwas a happy dance for both of them.\r\n\r\nChristmas day is the children\'s, but the holidays are youth\'s\r\ndancing-time. The holidays belong to the early twenties and the \'teens,\r\nhome from school and college. These years possess the holidays for a\r\nlittle while, then possess them only in smiling, wistful memories of\r\nholly and twinkling lights and dance-music, and charming faces\r\nall aglow. It is the liveliest time in life, the happiest of the\r\nirresponsible times in life. Mothers echo its happiness--nothing is like\r\na mother who has a son home from college, except another mother with a\r\nson home from college. Bloom does actually come upon these mothers; it\r\nis a visible thing; and they run like girls, walk like athletes, laugh\r\nlike sycophants. Yet they give up their sons to the daughters of other\r\nmothers, and find it proud rapture enough to be allowed to sit and\r\nwatch.\r\n\r\nThus Isabel watched George and Lucy dancing, as together they danced\r\naway the holidays of that year into the past.\r\n\r\n"They seem to get along better than they did at first, those two\r\nchildren," Fanny Minafer said sitting beside her at the Sharons\' dance,\r\na week after the Assembly. "They seemed to be always having little\r\nquarrels of some sort, at first. At least George did: he seemed to be\r\ncontinually pecking at that lovely, dainty, little Lucy, and being cross\r\nwith her over nothing."\r\n\r\n"Pecking?" Isabel laughed. "What a word to use about Georgie! I think I\r\nnever knew a more angelically amiable disposition in my life!"\r\n\r\nMiss Fanny echoed her sister-in-law\'s laugh, but it was a rueful echo,\r\nand not sweet. "He\'s amiable to you!" she said. "That\'s all the side of\r\nhim you ever happen to see. And why wouldn\'t he be amiable to anybody\r\nthat simply fell down and worshipped him every minute of her life? Most\r\nof us would!"\r\n\r\n"Isn\'t he worth worshipping? Just look at him! Isn\'t he charming with\r\nLucy! See how hard he ran to get it when she dropped her handkerchief\r\nback there."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I\'m not going to argue with you about George!" said Miss Fanny.\r\n"I\'m fond enough of him, for that matter. He can be charming, and he\'s\r\ncertainly stunning looking, if only--"\r\n\r\n"Let the \'if only\' go, dear," Isabel suggested good-naturedly. "Let\'s\r\ntalk about that dinner you thought I should--"\r\n\r\n"I?" Miss Fanny interrupted quickly. "Didn\'t you want to give it\r\nyourself?"\r\n\r\n"Indeed, I did, my dear!" said Isabel heartily. "I only meant that\r\nunless you had proposed it, perhaps I wouldn\'t--"\r\n\r\nBut here Eugene came for her to dance, and she left the sentence\r\nuncompleted. Holiday dances can be happy for youth renewed as well as\r\nfor youth in bud--and yet it was not with the air of a rival that Miss\r\nFanny watched her brother\'s wife dancing with the widower. Miss Fanny\'s\r\neyes narrowed a little, but only as if her mind engaged in a hopeful\r\ncalculation. She looked pleased.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter X\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nA few days after George\'s return to the university it became evident\r\nthat not quite everybody had gazed with complete benevolence upon the\r\nvarious young collegians at their holiday sports. The Sunday edition\r\nof the principal morning paper even expressed some bitterness under the\r\nheading, "Gilded Youths of the Fin-de-Siecle"--this was considered the\r\nknowing phrase of the time, especially for Sunday supplements--and there\r\nis no doubt that from certain references in this bit of writing some\r\npeople drew the conclusion that Mr. George Amberson Minafer had not yet\r\ngot his comeuppance, a postponement still irritating. Undeniably, Fanny\r\nMinafer was one of the people who drew this conclusion, for she cut the\r\narticle out and enclosed it in a letter to her nephew, having written on\r\nthe border of the clipping, "I wonder whom it can mean!"\r\n\r\nGeorge read part of it.\r\n\r\nWe debate sometimes what is to be the future of this nation when we\r\nthink that in a few years public affairs may be in the hands of the\r\nfin-de-siecle gilded youths we see about us during the Christmas\r\nholidays. Such foppery, such luxury, such insolence, was surely never\r\npractised by the scented, overbearing patricians of the Palatine, even\r\nin Rome\'s most decadent epoch. In all the wild orgy of wastefulness and\r\nluxury with which the nineteenth century reaches its close, the gilded\r\nyouth has been surely the worst symptom. With his airs of young milord,\r\nhis fast horses, his gold and silver cigarette-cases, his clothes from\r\na New York tailor, his recklessness of money showered upon him by\r\nindulgent mothers or doting grandfathers, he respects nothing and\r\nnobody. He is blase if you please. Watch him at a social function how\r\ncondescendingly he deigns to select a partner for the popular waltz or\r\ntwo step how carelessly he shoulders older people out of his way, with\r\nwhat a blank stare he returns the salutation of some old acquaintance\r\nwhom he may choose in his royal whim to forget! The unpleasant part\r\nof all this is that the young women he so condescendingly selects as\r\npartners for the dance greet him with seeming rapture, though in their\r\nhearts they must feel humiliated by his languid hauteur, and many older\r\npeople beam upon him almost fawningly if he unbends so far as to throw\r\nthem a careless, disdainful word!\r\n\r\nOne wonders what has come over the new generation. Of such as these the\r\nRepublic was not made. Let us pray that the future of our country is\r\nnot in the hands of these fin-de-siecle gilded youths, but rather in the\r\ncalloused palms of young men yet unknown, labouring upon the farms of\r\nthe land. When we compare the young manhood of Abraham Lincoln with the\r\nspecimens we are now producing, we see too well that it bodes ill for\r\nthe twentieth century--\r\n\r\nGeorge yawned, and tossed the clipping into his waste-basket, wondering\r\nwhy his aunt thought such dull nonsense worth the sending. As for her\r\ninsinuation, pencilled upon the border, he supposed she meant to joke--a\r\nsupposition which neither surprised him nor altered his lifelong opinion\r\nof her wit.\r\n\r\nHe read her letter with more interest:\r\n\r\nThe dinner your mother gave for the Morgans was a lovely affair. It was\r\nlast Monday evening, just ten days after you left. It was peculiarly\r\nappropriate that your mother should give this dinner, because her\r\nbrother George, your uncle, was Mr. Morgan\'s most intimate friend before\r\nhe left here a number of years ago, and it was a pleasant occasion for\r\nthe formal announcement of some news which you heard from Lucy Morgan\r\nbefore you returned to college. At least she told me she had told you\r\nthe night before you left that her father had decided to return here to\r\nlive. It was appropriate that your mother, herself an old friend, should\r\nassemble a representative selection of Mr. Morgan\'s old friends around\r\nhim at such a time. He was in great spirits and most entertaining.\r\nAs your time was so charmingly taken up during your visit home with a\r\nyounger member of his family, you probably overlooked opportunities of\r\nhearing him talk, and do not know what an interesting man he can be.\r\n\r\nHe will soon begin to build his factory here for the manufacture\r\nof automobiles, which he says is a term he prefers to "horseless\r\ncarriages." Your Uncle George told me he would like to invest in this\r\nfactory, as George thinks there is a future for automobiles; perhaps\r\nnot for general use, but as an interesting novelty, which people with\r\nsufficient means would like to own for their amusement and the sake of\r\nvariety. However, he said Mr. Morgan laughingly declined his offer, as\r\nMr. M. was fully able to finance this venture, though not starting in\r\na very large way. Your uncle said other people are manufacturing\r\nautomobiles in different parts of the country with success. Your father\r\nis not very well, though he is not actually ill, and the doctor tells\r\nhim he ought not to be so much at his office, as the long years of\r\napplication indoors with no exercise are beginning to affect him\r\nunfavourably, but I believe your father would die if he had to give\r\nup his work, which is all that has ever interested him outside of his\r\nfamily. I never could understand it. Mr. Morgan took your mother and me\r\nwith Lucy to see Modjeska in "Twelfth Night" yesterday evening, and\r\nLucy said she thought the Duke looked rather like you, only much more\r\ndemocratic in his manner. I suppose you will think I have written a\r\ngreat deal about the Morgans in this letter, but thought you would be\r\ninterested because of your interest in a younger member of his family.\r\nHoping that you are finding college still as attractive as ever,\r\n\r\n             Affectionately,\r\n                        Aunt Fanny.\r\n\r\nGeorge read one sentence in this letter several times. Then he dropped\r\nthe missive in his wastebasket to join the clipping, and strolled down\r\nthe corridor of his dormitory to borrow a copy of "Twelfth Night."\r\nHaving secured one, he returned to his study and refreshed his memory of\r\nthe play--but received no enlightenment that enabled him to comprehend\r\nLucy\'s strange remark. However, he found himself impelled in the\r\ndirection of correspondence, and presently wrote a letter--not a reply\r\nto his Aunt Fanny.\r\n\r\nDear Lucy: No doubt you will be surprised at hearing from me so soon\r\nagain, especially as this makes two in answer to the one received from\r\nyou since getting back to the old place. I hear you have been making\r\ncomments about me at the theatre, that some actor was more democratic in\r\nhis manners than I am, which I do not understand. You know my theory of\r\nlife because I explained it to you on our first drive together, when I\r\ntold you I would not talk to everybody about things I feel like the way\r\nI spoke to you of my theory of life. I believe those who are able should\r\nhave a true theory of life, and I developed my theory of life long, long\r\nago.\r\n\r\nWell, here I sit smoking my faithful briar pipe, indulging in the\r\nfragrance of my tobacco as I look out on the campus from my many-paned\r\nwindow, and things are different with me from the way they were way back\r\nin Freshman year. I can see now how boyish in many ways I was then. I\r\nbelieve what has changed me as much as anything was my visit home at the\r\ntime I met you. So I sit here with my faithful briar and dream the old\r\ndreams over as it were, dreaming of the waltzes we waltzed together and\r\nof that last night before we parted, and you told me the good news you\r\nwere going to live there, and I would find my friend waiting for me,\r\nwhen I get home next summer.\r\n\r\nI will be glad my friend will be waiting for me. I am not capable of\r\nfriendship except for the very few, and, looking back over my life,\r\nI remember there were times when I doubted if I could feel a great\r\nfriendship for anybody--especially girls. I do not take a great interest\r\nin many people, as you know, for I find most of them shallow. Here in\r\nthe old place I do not believe in being hail-fellow-well-met with every\r\nTom, Dick, and Harry just because he happens to be a classmate, any more\r\nthan I do at home, where I have always been careful who I was seen with,\r\nlargely on account of the family, but also because my disposition ever\r\nsince my boyhood has been to encourage real intimacy from but the few.\r\n\r\nWhat are you reading now? I have finished both "Henry Esmond" and "The\r\nVirginians." I like Thackeray because he is not trashy, and because he\r\nwrites principally of nice people. My theory of literature is an author\r\nwho does not indulge in trashiness--writes about people you could\r\nintroduce into your own home. I agree with my Uncle Sydney, as I once\r\nheard him say he did not care to read a book or go to a play about\r\npeople he would not care to meet at his own dinner table. I believe we\r\nshould live by certain standards and ideals, as you know from my telling\r\nyou my theory of life.\r\n\r\nWell, a letter is no place for deep discussions, so I will not go into\r\nthe subject. From several letters from my mother, and one from Aunt\r\nFanny, I hear you are seeing a good deal of the family since I left.\r\nI hope sometimes you think of the member who is absent. I got a silver\r\nframe for your photograph in New York, and I keep it on my desk. It\r\nis the only girl\'s photograph I ever took the trouble to have framed,\r\nthough, as I told you frankly, I have had any number of other girls\'\r\nphotographs, yet all were only passing fancies, and oftentimes I have\r\nquestioned in years past if I was capable of much friendship toward the\r\nfeminine sex, which I usually found shallow until our own friendship\r\nbegan. When I look at your photograph, I say to myself, "At last, at\r\nlast here is one that will not prove shallow."\r\n\r\nMy faithful briar has gone out. I will have to rise and fill it, then\r\nonce more in the fragrance of My Lady Nicotine, I will sit and dream the\r\nold dreams over, and think, too, of the true friend at home awaiting my\r\nreturn in June for the summer vacation.\r\n\r\n             Friend, this is from your friend,\r\n                                            G.A.M.\r\n\r\n\r\nGeorge\'s anticipations were not disappointed. When he came home in June\r\nhis friend was awaiting him; at least, she was so pleased to see him\r\nagain that for a few minutes after their first encounter she was a\r\nlittle breathless, and a great deal glowing, and quiet withal. Their\r\nsentimental friendship continued, though sometimes he was irritated by\r\nher making it less sentimental than he did, and sometimes by what he\r\ncalled her "air of superiority." Her air was usually, in truth, that\r\nof a fond but amused older sister; and George did not believe such an\r\nattitude was warranted by her eight months of seniority.\r\n\r\nLucy and her father were living at the Amberson Hotel, while Morgan got\r\nhis small machine-shops built in a western outskirt of the town; and\r\nGeorge grumbled about the shabbiness and the old-fashioned look of\r\nthe hotel, though it was "still the best in the place, of course." He\r\nremonstrated with his grandfather, declaring that the whole Amberson\r\nEstate would be getting "run-down and out-at-heel, if things weren\'t\r\ntaken in hand pretty soon." He urged the general need of rebuilding,\r\nrenovating, varnishing, and lawsuits. But the Major, declining to hear\r\nhim out, interrupted querulously, saying that he had enough to bother\r\nhim without any advice from George; and retired to his library, going so\r\nfar as to lock the door audibly.\r\n\r\n"Second childhood!" George muttered, shaking his head; and he thought\r\nsadly that the Major had not long to live. However, this surmise\r\ndepressed him for only a moment or so. Of course, people couldn\'t be\r\nexpected to live forever, and it would be a good thing to have someone\r\nin charge of the Estate who wouldn\'t let it get to looking so rusty that\r\nriffraff dared to make fun of it. For George had lately undergone the\r\nannoyance of calling upon the Morgans, in the rather stuffy red velours\r\nand gilt parlour of their apartment at the hotel, one evening when Mr.\r\nFrederick Kinney also was a caller, and Mr. Kinney had not been tactful.\r\nIn fact, though he adopted a humorous tone of voice, in expressing his,\r\nsympathy for people who, through the city\'s poverty in hotels, were\r\nobliged to stay at the Amberson, Mr. Kinney\'s intention was interpreted\r\nby the other visitor as not at all humorous, but, on the contrary,\r\npersonal and offensive.\r\n\r\nGeorge rose abruptly, his face the colour of wrath. "Good-night, Miss\r\nMorgan. Good-night, Mr. Morgan," he said. "I shall take pleasure in\r\ncalling at some other time when a more courteous sort of people may be\r\npresent."\r\n\r\n"Look here!" the hot-headed Fred burst out. "Don\'t you try to make me\r\nout a boor, George Minafer! I wasn\'t hinting anything at you; I simply\r\nforgot all about your grandfather owning this old building. Don\'t you\r\ntry to put me in the light of a boor! I won\'t--"\r\n\r\nBut George walked out in the very course of this vehement protest, and\r\nit was necessarily left unfinished.\r\n\r\nMr. Kinney remained only a few moments after George\'s departure; and as\r\nthe door closed upon him, the distressed Lucy turned to her father.\r\nShe was plaintively surprised to find him in a condition of immoderate\r\nlaughter.\r\n\r\n"I didn\'t--I didn\'t think I could hold out!" he gasped, and, after\r\nchoking until tears came to his eyes, felt blindly for the chair from\r\nwhich he had risen to wish Mr. Kinney an indistinct good-night. His\r\nhand found the arm of the chair; he collapsed feebly, and sat uttering\r\nincoherent sounds.\r\n\r\n"Papa!"\r\n\r\n"It brings things back so!" he managed to explain, "This very Fred\r\nKinney\'s father and young George\'s father, Wilbur Minafer, used to do\r\njust such things when they were at that age--and, for that matter, so\r\ndid George Amberson and I, and all the rest of us!" And, in spite of his\r\nexhaustion, he began to imitate: "Don\'t you try to put me in the light\r\nof a boor!" "I shall take pleasure in calling at some time when a more\r\ncourteous sort of people--" He was unable to go on.\r\n\r\nThere is a mirth for every age, and Lucy failed to comprehend her\r\nfather\'s, but tolerated it a little ruefully.\r\n\r\n"Papa, I think they were shocking. Weren\'t they awful!"\r\n\r\n"Just--just boys!" he moaned, wiping his eyes. But Lucy could not smile\r\nat all; she was beginning to look indignant. "I can forgive that poor\r\nFred Kinney," she said. "He\'s just blundering--but George--oh, George\r\nbehaved outrageously!"\r\n\r\n"It\'s a difficult age," her father observed, his calmness somewhat\r\nrestored. "Girls don\'t seem to have to pass through it quite as boys do,\r\nor their savoir faire is instinctive--or something!" And he gave away to\r\na return of his convulsion.\r\n\r\nShe came and sat upon the arm of his chair. "Papa, why should George\r\nbehave like that?"\r\n\r\n"He\'s sensitive."\r\n\r\n"Rather! But why is he? He does anything he likes to, without any regard\r\nfor what people think. Then why should he mind so furiously when\r\nthe least little thing reflects upon him, or on anything or anybody\r\nconnected with him?"\r\n\r\nEugene patted her hand. "That\'s one of the greatest puzzles of human\r\nvanity, dear; and I don\'t pretend to know the answer. In all my life,\r\nthe most arrogant people that I\'ve known have been the most sensitive.\r\nThe people who have done the most in contempt of other people\'s opinion,\r\nand who consider themselves the highest above it, have been the most\r\nfurious if it went against them. Arrogant and domineering people can\'t\r\nstand the least, lightest, faintest breath of criticism. It just kills\r\nthem."\r\n\r\n"Papa, do you think George is arrogant and domineering?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, he\'s still only a boy," said Eugene consolingly. "There\'s plenty\r\nof fine stuff in him--can\'t help but be, because he\'s Isabel Amberson\'s\r\nson."\r\n\r\nLucy stroked his hair, which was still almost as dark as her own. "You\r\nliked her pretty well once, I guess, papa."\r\n\r\n"I do still," he said quietly.\r\n\r\n"She\'s lovely--lovely! Papa--" she paused, then continued--"I wonder\r\nsometimes--"\r\n\r\n"What?"\r\n\r\n"I wonder just how she happened to marry Mr. Minafer."\r\n\r\n"Oh, Minafer\'s all right," said Eugene. "He\'s a quiet sort of man, but\r\nhe\'s a good man and a kind man. He always was, and those things count."\r\n\r\n"But in a way--well, I\'ve heard people say there wasn\'t anything to him\r\nat all except business and saving money. Miss Fanny Minafer herself told\r\nme that everything George and his mother have of their own--that is,\r\njust to spend as they like--she says it has always come from Major\r\nAmberson."\r\n\r\n"Thrift, Horatio!" said Eugene lightly. "Thrift\'s an inheritance, and a\r\ncommon enough one here. The people who settled the country had to save,\r\nso making and saving were taught as virtues, and the people, to the\r\nthird generation, haven\'t found out that making and saving are only\r\nmeans to an end. Minafer doesn\'t believe in money being spent. He\r\nbelieves God made it to be invested and saved."\r\n\r\n"But George isn\'t saving. He\'s reckless, and even if he is arrogant and\r\nconceited and bad-tempered, he\'s awfully generous."\r\n\r\n"Oh, he\'s an Amberson," said her father. "The Ambersons aren\'t saving.\r\nThey\'re too much the other way, most of them."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t think I should have called George bad-tempered," Lucy said\r\nthoughtfully. "No. I don\'t think he is."\r\n\r\n"Only when he\'s cross about something?" Morgan suggested, with a\r\nsemblance of sympathetic gravity.\r\n\r\n"Yes," she said brightly, not perceiving that his intention was\r\nhumorous. "All the rest of the time he\'s really very amiable. Of course,\r\nhe\'s much more a perfect child, the whole time, than he realizes! He\r\ncertainly behaved awfully to-night." She jumped up, her indignation\r\nreturning. "He did, indeed, and it won\'t do to encourage him in it. I\r\nthink he\'ll find me pretty cool--for a week or so!"\r\n\r\nWhereupon her father suffered a renewal of his attack of uproarious\r\nlaughter.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XI\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIn the matter of coolness, George met Lucy upon her own predetermined\r\nground; in fact, he was there first, and, at their next encounter,\r\nproved loftier and more formal than she did. Their estrangement lasted\r\nthree weeks, and then disappeared without any preliminary treaty: it had\r\nworn itself out, and they forgot it.\r\n\r\nAt times, however, George found other disturbances to the friendship.\r\nLucy was "too much the village belle," he complained; and took a satiric\r\nattitude toward his competitors, referring to them as her "local swains\r\nand bumpkins," sulking for an afternoon when she reminded him that he,\r\ntoo, was at least "local." She was a belle with older people as well;\r\nIsabel and Fanny were continually taking her driving, bringing her home\r\nwith them to lunch or dinner, and making a hundred little engagements\r\nwith her, and the Major had taken a great fancy to her, insisting upon\r\nher presence and her father\'s at the Amberson family dinner at the\r\nMansion every Sunday evening. She knew how to flirt with old people, he\r\nsaid, as she sat next him at the table on one of these Sunday occasions;\r\nand he had always liked her father, even when Eugene was a "terror" long\r\nago. "Oh, yes, he was!" the Major laughed, when she remonstrated. "He\r\ncame up here with my son George and some others for a serenade one\r\nnight, and Eugene stepped into a bass fiddle, and the poor musicians\r\njust gave up! I had a pretty half-hour getting my son George upstairs.\r\nI remember! It was the last time Eugene ever touched a drop--but he\'d\r\ntouched plenty before that, young lady, and he daren\'t deny it! Well,\r\nwell; there\'s another thing that\'s changed: hardly anybody drinks\r\nnowadays. Perhaps it\'s just as well, but things used to be livelier.\r\nThat serenade was just before Isabel was married--and don\'t you fret,\r\nMiss Lucy: your father remembers it well enough!" The old gentleman\r\nburst into laughter, and shook his finger at Eugene across the table.\r\n"The fact is," the Major went on hilariously, "I believe if Eugene\r\nhadn\'t broken that bass fiddle and given himself away, Isabel would\r\nnever have taken Wilbur! I shouldn\'t be surprised if that was about all\r\nthe reason that Wilbur got her! What do you think. Wilbur?"\r\n\r\n"I shouldn\'t be surprised," said Wilbur placidly. "If your notion is\r\nright, I\'m glad \'Gene broke the fiddle. He was giving me a hard run!"\r\n\r\nThe Major always drank three glasses of champagne at his Sunday dinner,\r\nand he was finishing the third. "What do you say about it, Isabel? By\r\nJove!" he cried, pounding the table. "She\'s blushing!"\r\n\r\nIsabel did blush, but she laughed. "Who wouldn\'t blush!" she cried, and\r\nher sister-in-law came to her assistance.\r\n\r\n"The important thing," said Fanny jovially, "is that Wilbur did get her,\r\nand not only got her, but kept her!"\r\n\r\nEugene was as pink as Isabel, but he laughed without any sign of\r\nembarrassment other than his heightened colour. "There\'s another\r\nimportant thing--that is, for me," he said. "It\'s the only thing that\r\nmakes me forgive that bass viol for getting in my way."\r\n\r\n"What is it?" the Major asked.\r\n\r\n"Lucy," said Morgan gently.\r\n\r\nIsabel gave him a quick glance, all warm approval, and there was a\r\nmurmur of friendliness round the table.\r\n\r\nGeorge was not one of those who joined in this applause. He considered\r\nhis grandfather\'s nonsense indelicate, even for second childhood, and he\r\nthought that the sooner the subject was dropped the better. However, he\r\nhad only a slight recurrence of the resentment which had assailed him\r\nduring the winter at every sign of his mother\'s interest in Morgan;\r\nthough he was still ashamed of his aunt sometimes, when it seemed to him\r\nthat Fanny was almost publicly throwing herself at the widower\'s head.\r\nFanny and he had one or two arguments in which her fierceness again\r\nastonished and amused him.\r\n\r\n"You drop your criticisms of your relatives," she bade him, hotly, one\r\nday, "and begin thinking a little about your own behaviour! You say\r\npeople will \'talk\' about my--about my merely being pleasant to an old\r\nfriend! What do I care how they talk? I guess if people are talking\r\nabout anybody in this family they\'re talking about the impertinent\r\nlittle snippet that hasn\'t any respect for anything, and doesn\'t even\r\nknow enough to attend to his own affairs!"\r\n\r\n"Snippet,\' Aunt Fanny!" George laughed. "How elegant! And \'little\r\nsnippet\'--when I\'m over five-feet-eleven?"\r\n\r\n"I said it!" she snapped, departing. "I don\'t see how Lucy can stand\r\nyou!"\r\n\r\n"You\'d make an amiable stepmother-in-law!" he called after her. "I\'ll be\r\ncareful about proposing to Lucy!"\r\n\r\nThese were but roughish spots in a summer that glided by evenly and\r\nquickly enough, for the most part, and, at the end, seemed to fly. On\r\nthe last night before George went back to be a Junior, his mother asked\r\nhim confidently if it had not been a happy summer.\r\n\r\nHe hadn\'t thought about it, he answered. "Oh,\' I suppose so. Why?"\r\n\r\n"I just thought it would be: nice to hear you say so," she said,\r\nsmiling. "I mean, it\'s pleasant for people of my age to know that people\r\nof your age realize that they\'re happy."\r\n\r\n"People of your age!" he repeated. "You know you don\'t look precisely\r\nlike an old woman, mother. Not precisely!"\r\n\r\n"No," she said. "And I suppose I feel about as young as you do, inside,\r\nbut it won\'t be many years before I must begin to look old. It does\r\ncome!" She sighed, still smiling. "It\'s seemed to me that, it must have\r\nbeen a happy summer for you--a real \'summer of roses and wine\'--without\r\nthe wine, perhaps. \'Gather ye roses while ye may\'--or was it primroses?\r\nTime does really fly, or perhaps it\'s more like the sky--and smoke--"\r\n\r\nGeorge was puzzled. "What do you mean: time being like the sky and\r\nsmoke?"\r\n\r\n"I mean the things that we have and that we think are so solid--they\'re\r\nlike smoke, and time is like the sky that the smoke disappears into. You\r\nknow how wreath of smoke goes up from a chimney, and seems all thick and\r\nblack and busy against the sky, as if it were going to do such important\r\nthings and last forever, and you see it getting thinner and thinner--and\r\nthen, in such a little while, it isn\'t there at all; nothing is left but\r\nthe sky, and the sky keeps on being just the same forever."\r\n\r\n"It strikes me you\'re getting mixed up," said George cheerfully. "I\r\ndon\'t see much resemblance between time and the sky, or between things\r\nand smoke-wreaths; but I do see one reason you like \'Lucy Morgan so\r\nmuch. She talks that same kind of wistful, moony way sometimes--I don\'t\r\nmean to say I mind it in either of you, because I rather like to listen\r\nto it, and you\'ve got a very good voice, mother. It\'s nice to listen to,\r\nno matter how much smoke and sky, and so on, you talk. So\'s Lucy\'s for\r\nthat matter; and I see why you\'re congenial. She talks that way to her\r\nfather, too; and he\'s right there with the same kind of guff. Well, it\'s\r\nall right with me!" He laughed, teasingly, and allowed her to retain his\r\nhand, which she had fondly seized. "I\'ve got plenty to think about when\r\npeople drool along!"\r\n\r\nShe pressed his hand to her cheek, and a tear made a tiny warm streak\r\nacross one of his knuckles.\r\n\r\n"For heaven\'s sake!" he said. "What\'s the matter? Isn\'t everything all\r\nright?"\r\n\r\n"You\'re going away!"\r\n\r\n"Well, I\'m coming back, don\'t you suppose? Is that all that worries\r\nyou?"\r\n\r\nShe cheered up, and smiled again, but shook her head. "I never can bear\r\nto see you go--that\'s the most of it. I\'m a little bothered about your\r\nfather, too."\r\n\r\n"Why?"\r\n\r\n"It seems to me he looks so badly. Everybody thinks so."\r\n\r\n"What nonsense!" George laughed. "He\'s been looking that way all summer.\r\nHe isn\'t much different from the way he\'s looked all his life, that I\r\ncan see. What\'s the matter with him?"\r\n\r\n"He never talks much about his business to me but I think he\'s been\r\nworrying about some investments he made last year. I think his worry has\r\naffected his health."\r\n\r\n"What investments?" George demanded. "He hasn\'t gone into Mr. Morgan\'s\r\nautomobile concern, has he?"\r\n\r\n"No," Isabel smiled. "The \'automobile concern\' is all Eugene\'s, and it\'s\r\nso small I understand it\'s taken hardly anything. No; your father\r\nhas always prided himself on making only the most absolutely safe\r\ninvestments, but two or three years ago he and your Uncle George both\r\nput a great deal--pretty much everything they could get together, I\r\nthink--into the stock of rolling-mills some friends of theirs owned, and\r\nI\'m afraid the mills haven\'t been doing well."\r\n\r\n"What of that? Father needn\'t worry. You and I could take care of him\r\nthe rest of his life on what grandfather--"\r\n\r\n"Of course," she agreed. "But your father\'s always lived so for his\r\nbusiness and taken such pride in his sound investments; it\'s a passion\r\nwith him. I--"\r\n\r\n"Pshaw! He needn\'t worry! You tell him we\'ll look after him: we\'ll build\r\nhim a little stone bank in the backyard, if he busts up, and he can go\r\nand put his pennies in it every morning. That\'ll keep him just as happy\r\nas he ever was!" He kissed her. "Good-night, I\'m going to tell Lucy\r\ngood-bye. Don\'t sit up for me."\r\n\r\nShe walked to the front gate with him, still holding his hand, and he\r\ntold her again not to "sit up" for him.\r\n\r\n"Yes, I will," she laughed. "You won\'t be very late."\r\n\r\n"Well--it\'s my last night."\r\n\r\n"But I know Lucy, and she knows I want to see you, too, your last night.\r\nYou\'ll see: she\'ll send you home promptly at eleven!"\r\n\r\nBut she was mistaken: Lucy sent him home promptly at ten.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XII\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIsabel\'s uneasiness about her husband\'s health--sometimes reflected in\r\nher letters to George during the winter that followed--had not been\r\nalleviated when the accredited Senior returned for his next summer\r\nvacation, and she confided to him in his room, soon after his arrival,\r\nthat "something" the doctor had said to her lately had made her more\r\nuneasy than ever.\r\n\r\n"Still worrying over his rolling-mills investments?" George asked, not\r\nseriously impressed.\r\n\r\n"I\'m afraid it\'s past that stage from what Dr Rainey says. His worries\r\nonly aggravate his condition now. Dr. Rainey says we ought to get him\r\naway."\r\n\r\n"Well, let\'s do it, then."\r\n\r\n"He won\'t go."\r\n\r\n"He\'s a man awfully set in his ways; that\'s true," said George. "I don\'t\r\nthink there\'s anything much the matter with him, though, and he looks\r\njust the same to me. Have you seen Lucy lately? How is she?"\r\n\r\n"Hasn\'t she written you?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, about once a month," he answered carelessly. "Never says much about\r\nherself. How\'s she look?"\r\n\r\n"She looks--pretty!" said Isabel. "I suppose she wrote you they\'ve\r\nmoved?"\r\n\r\n"Yes; I\'ve got her address. She said they were building."\r\n\r\n"They did. It\'s all finished, and they\'ve been in it a month. Lucy is so\r\ncapable; she keeps house exquisitely. It\'s small, but oh, such a pretty\r\nlittle house!"\r\n\r\n"Well, that\'s fortunate," George said. "One thing I\'ve always felt they\r\ndidn\'t know a great deal about is architecture."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t they?" asked Isabel, surprised. "Anyhow, their house is charming.\r\nIt\'s way out beyond the end of Amberson Boulevard; it\'s quite near that\r\nbig white house with a gray-green roof somebody built out there a year\r\nor so ago. There are any number of houses going up, out that way; and\r\nthe trolley-line runs within a block of them now, on the next street,\r\nand the traction people are laying tracks more than three miles beyond.\r\nI suppose you\'ll be driving out to see Lucy to-morrow."\r\n\r\n"I thought--" George hesitated. "I thought perhaps I\'d go after dinner\r\nthis evening."\r\n\r\nAt this his mother laughed, not astonished. "It was only my feeble joke\r\nabout \'to-morrow,\' Georgie! I was pretty sure you couldn\'t wait that\r\nlong. Did Lucy write you about the factory?"\r\n\r\n"No. What factory?"\r\n\r\n"The automobile shops. They had rather a dubious time at first, I\'m\r\nafraid, and some of Eugene\'s experiments turned out badly, but this\r\nspring they\'ve finished eight automobiles and sold them all, and they\'ve\r\ngot twelve more almost finished, and they\'re sold already! Eugene\'s so\r\ngay over it!"\r\n\r\n"What do his old sewing-machines look like? Like that first one he had\r\nwhen they came here?"\r\n\r\n"No, indeed! These have rubber tires blown up with air--pneumatic! And\r\nthey aren\'t so high; they\'re very easy to get into, and the engine\'s\r\nin front--Eugene thinks that\'s a great improvement. They\'re very\r\ninteresting to look at; behind the driver\'s seat there\'s a sort of box\r\nwhere four people can sit, with a step and a little door in the rear,\r\nand--"\r\n\r\n"I know all about it," said George. "I\'ve seen any number like that,\r\nEast. You can see all you want of \'em, if you stand on Fifth Avenue half\r\nan hour, any afternoon. I\'ve seen half-a-dozen go by almost at the same\r\ntime--within a few minutes, anyhow; and of course electric hansoms are\r\na common sight there any day. I hired one, myself, the last time I was\r\nthere. How fast do Mr. Morgan\'s machines go?"\r\n\r\n"Much too fast! It\'s very exhilarating--but rather frightening; and they\r\ndo make a fearful uproar. He says, though, he thinks he sees a way to\r\nget around the noisiness in time."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t mind the noise," said George. "Give me a horse, for mine,\r\nthough, any day. I must get up a race with one of these things:\r\nPendennis\'ll leave it one mile behind in a two-mile run. How\'s\r\ngrandfather?"\r\n\r\n"He looks well, but he complains sometimes of his heart: I suppose\r\nthat\'s natural at his age--and it\'s an Amberson trouble." Having\r\nmentioned this, she looked anxious instantly. "Did you ever feel any\r\nweakness there, Georgie?"\r\n\r\n"No!" he laughed.\r\n\r\n"Are you sure, dear?"\r\n\r\n"No!" And he laughed again. "Did you?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, I think not--at least, the doctor told me he thought my heart was\r\nabout all right. He said I needn\'t be alarmed."\r\n\r\n"I should think not! Women do seem to be always talking about health: I\r\nsuppose they haven\'t got enough else to think of!"\r\n\r\n"That must be it," she said gayly. "We\'re an idle lot!"\r\n\r\nGeorge had taken off his coat. "I don\'t like to hint to a lady," he\r\nsaid, "but I do want to dress before dinner."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t be long; I\'ve got to do a lot of looking at you, dear!" She\r\nkissed him and ran away singing.\r\n\r\nBut his Aunt Fanny was not so fond; and at the dinner-table there came\r\na spark of liveliness into her eye when George patronizingly asked her\r\nwhat was the news in her own "particular line of sport."\r\n\r\n"What do you mean, Georgie?" she asked quietly.\r\n\r\n"Oh I mean: What\'s the news in the fast set generally? You been causing\r\nany divorces lately?"\r\n\r\n"No," said Fanny, the spark in her eye getting brighter. "I haven\'t been\r\ncausing anything."\r\n\r\n"Well, what\'s the gossip? You usually hear pretty much everything that\r\ngoes on around the nooks and crannies in this town, I hear. What\'s the\r\nlast from the gossips\' corner, auntie?"\r\n\r\nFanny dropped her eyes, and the spark was concealed, but a movement\r\nof her lower lip betokened a tendency to laugh, as she replied. "There\r\nhasn\'t been much gossip lately, except the report that Lucy Morgan and\r\nFred Kinney are engaged--and that\'s quite old, by this time."\r\n\r\nUndeniably, this bit of mischief was entirely successful, for there was\r\na clatter upon George\'s plate. "What--what do you think you\'re talking\r\nabout?" he gasped.\r\n\r\nMiss Fanny looked up innocently. "About the report of Lucy Morgan\'s\r\nengagement to Fred Kinney."\r\n\r\nGeorge turned dumbly to his mother, and Isabel shook her head\r\nreassuringly. "People are always starting rumours," she said. "I haven\'t\r\npaid any attention to this one."\r\n\r\n"But you--you\'ve heard it?" he stammered.\r\n\r\n"Oh, one hears all sorts of nonsense, dear. I haven\'t the slightest idea\r\nthat it\'s true."\r\n\r\n"Then you have heard it!"\r\n\r\n"I wouldn\'t let it take my appetite," his father suggested drily. "There\r\nare plenty of girls in the world!"\r\n\r\nGeorge turned pale.\r\n\r\n"Eat your dinner, Georgie," his aunt said sweetly. "Food will do you\r\ngood. I didn\'t say I knew this rumour was true. I only said I\'d heard\r\nit."\r\n\r\n"When? When did you hear it!"\r\n\r\n"Oh, months ago!" And Fanny found any further postponement of laughter\r\nimpossible.\r\n\r\n"Fanny, you\'re a hard-hearted creature," Isabel said gently. "You really\r\nare. Don\'t pay any attention to her, George. Fred Kinney\'s only a clerk\r\nin his uncle\'s hardware place: he couldn\'t marry for ages--even if\r\nanybody would accept him!"\r\n\r\nGeorge breathed tumultuously. "I don\'t care anything about \'ages\'!\r\nWhat\'s that got to do with it?" he said, his thoughts appearing to\r\nbe somewhat disconnected. "Ages,\' don\'t mean anything! I only want to\r\nknow--I want to know--I want--" He stopped.\r\n\r\n"What do you want?" his father asked crossly.\r\n\r\n"Why don\'t you say it? Don\'t make such a fuss."\r\n\r\n"I\'m not--not at all," George declared, pushing his chair back from the\r\ntable.\r\n\r\n"You must finish your dinner, dear," his mother urged. "Don\'t--"\r\n\r\n"I have finished. I\'ve eaten all I want. I don\'t want any more than\r\nI wanted. I don\'t want--I--" He rose, still incoherent. "I prefer--I\r\nwant--Please excuse me!"\r\n\r\nHe left the room, and a moment later the screens outside the open front\r\ndoor were heard to slam:\r\n\r\n"Fanny! You shouldn\'t--"\r\n\r\n"Isabel, don\'t reproach me, he did have plenty of dinner, and I only\r\ntold the truth: everybody has been saying--"\r\n\r\n"But there isn\'t any truth in it."\r\n\r\n"We don\'t actually know there isn\'t," Miss Fanny insisted, giggling.\r\n"We\'ve never asked Lucy."\r\n\r\n"I wouldn\'t ask her anything so absurd!"\r\n\r\n"George would," George\'s father remarked. "That\'s what he\'s gone to do."\r\n\r\nMr. Minafer was not mistaken: that was what his son had gone to do. Lucy\r\nand her father were just rising from their dinner table when the stirred\r\nyouth arrived at the front door of the new house. It was a cottage,\r\nhowever, rather than a house; and Lucy had taken a free hand with the\r\narchitect, achieving results in white and green, outside, and white and\r\nblue, inside, to such effect of youth and daintiness that her father\r\ncomplained of "too much spring-time!" The whole place, including his own\r\nbedroom, was a young damsel\'s boudoir, he said, so that nowhere could\r\nhe smoke a cigar without feeling like a ruffian. However, he was\r\nsmoking when George arrived, and he encouraged George to join him in\r\nthe pastime, but the caller, whose air was both tense and preoccupied,\r\ndeclined with something like agitation.\r\n\r\n"I never smoke--that is, I\'m seldom--I mean, no thanks," he said. "I\r\nmean not at all. I\'d rather not."\r\n\r\n"Aren\'t you well, George?" Eugene asked, looking at him in perplexity.\r\n"Have you been overworking at college? You do look rather pa--"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t work," said George. "I mean I don\'t work. I think, but I don\'t\r\nwork. I only work at the end of the term. There isn\'t much to do."\r\n\r\nEugene\'s perplexity was little decreased, and a tinkle of the door-bell\r\nafforded him obvious relief. "It\'s my foreman," he said, looking at his\r\nwatch. "I\'ll take him out in the yard to talk. This is no place for a\r\nforeman." And he departed, leaving the "living room" to Lucy and George.\r\nIt was a pretty room, white panelled and blue curtained--and no place\r\nfor a foreman, as Eugene said. There was a grand piano, and Lucy stood\r\nleaning back against it, looking intently at George, while her fingers,\r\nbehind her, absently struck a chord or two. And her dress was the dress\r\nfor that room, being of blue and white, too; and the high colour in\r\nher cheeks was far from interfering with the general harmony of\r\nthings--George saw with dismay that she was prettier than ever, and\r\nnaturally he missed the reassurance he might have felt had he been able\r\nto guess that Lucy, on her part, was finding him better looking than\r\never. For, however unusual the scope of George\'s pride, vanity of beauty\r\nwas not included; he did not think about his looks.\r\n\r\n"What\'s wrong, George?" she asked softly.\r\n\r\n"What do you mean: \'What\'s wrong?"\r\n\r\n"You\'re awfully upset about something. Didn\'t you get though your\r\nexamination all right?"\r\n\r\n"Certainly I did. What makes you think anything\'s \'wrong\' with me?"\r\n\r\n"You do look pale, as papa said, and it seemed to me that the way you\r\ntalked sounded--well, a little confused."\r\n\r\n"Confused\'! I said I didn\'t care to smoke. What in the world is confused\r\nabout that?"\r\n\r\n"Nothing. But--"\r\n\r\n"See here!" George stepped close to her. "Are you glad to see me?"\r\n\r\n"You needn\'t be so fierce about it!" Lucy protested, laughing at his\r\ndramatic intensity. "Of course I am! How long have I been looking\r\nforward to it?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know," he said sharply, abating nothing of his fierceness. "How\r\nlong have you?"\r\n\r\n"Why--ever since you went away!"\r\n\r\n"Is that true? Lucy, is that true?"\r\n\r\n"You are funny!" she said. "Of course it\'s true. Do tell me what\'s the\r\nmatter with you, George!"\r\n\r\n"I will!" he exclaimed. "I was a boy when I saw you last. I see that\r\nnow, though I didn\'t then. Well, I\'m not a boy any longer. I\'m a man,\r\nand a man has a right to demand a totally different treatment."\r\n\r\n"Why has he?"\r\n\r\n"What?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t seem to be able to understand you at all, George. Why shouldn\'t\r\na boy be treated just as well as a man?"\r\n\r\nGeorge seemed to find himself at a loss. "Why shouldn\'t--Well, he\r\nshouldn\'t, because a man has a right to certain explanations."\r\n\r\n"What explanations?"\r\n\r\n"Whether he\'s been made a toy of!" George almost shouted. "That\'s what I\r\nwant to know!"\r\n\r\nLucy shook her head despairingly. "You are the queerest person! You say\r\nyou\'re a man now, but you talk more like a boy than ever. What does make\r\nyou so excited?"\r\n\r\n"\'Excited!\'" he stormed. "Do you dare to stand there and call me\r\n\'excited\'? I tell you, I never have been more calm or calmer in my\r\nlife! I don\'t know that a person needs to be called \'excited\' because he\r\ndemands explanations that are his simple due!"\r\n\r\n"What in the world do you want me to explain?"\r\n\r\n"Your conduct with Fred Kinney!" George shouted.\r\n\r\nLucy uttered a sudden cry of laughter; she was delighted. "It\'s been\r\nawful!" she said. "I don\'t know that I ever heard of worse misbehaviour!\r\nPapa and I have been twice to dinner with his family, and I\'ve been\r\nthree times to church with Fred--and once to the circus! I don\'t know\r\nwhen they\'ll be here to arrest me!"\r\n\r\n"Stop that!" George commanded fiercely. "I want to know just one thing,\r\nand I mean to know it, too!"\r\n\r\n"Whether I enjoyed the circus?"\r\n\r\n"I want to know if you\'re engaged to him!"\r\n\r\n"No!" she cried and lifting her face close to his for the shortest\r\ninstant possible, she gave him a look half merry, half defiant, but all\r\nfond. It was an adorable look.\r\n\r\n"Lucy!" he said huskily.\r\n\r\nBut she turned quickly from him, and ran to the other end of the room.\r\nHe followed awkwardly, stammering:\r\n\r\n"Lucy, I want--I want to ask you. Will you--will you--will you be\r\nengaged to me?"\r\n\r\nShe stood at a window, seeming to look out into the summer darkness, her\r\nback to him.\r\n\r\n"Will you, Lucy?"\r\n\r\n"No," she murmured, just audibly.\r\n\r\n"Why not?"\r\n\r\n"I\'m older than you."\r\n\r\n"Eight months!"\r\n\r\n"You\'re too young."\r\n\r\n"Is that--" he said, gulping--"is that the only reason you won\'t?"\r\n\r\nShe did not answer.\r\n\r\nAs she stood, persistently staring out of the window, with her back to\r\nhim, she did not see how humble his attitude had become; but his voice\r\nwas low, and it shook so that she could have no doubt of his emotion.\r\n"Lucy, please forgive me for making such a row," he said, thus gently.\r\n"I\'ve been--I\'ve been terribly upset--terribly! You know how I feel\r\nabout you, and always have felt about you. I\'ve shown it in every single\r\nthing I\'ve done since the first time I met you, and I know you know it.\r\nDon\'t you?"\r\n\r\nStill she did not move or speak.\r\n\r\n"Is the only reason you won\'t be engaged to me you think I\'m too young,\r\nLucy?"\r\n\r\n"It\'s--it\'s reason enough," she said faintly.\r\n\r\nAt that he caught one of her hands, and she turned to him: there were\r\ntears in her eyes, tears which he did not understand at all.\r\n\r\n"Lucy, you little dear!" he cried. "I knew you--"\r\n\r\n"No, no!" she said, and she pushed him away, withdrawing her hand.\r\n"George, let\'s not talk of solemn things."\r\n\r\n"Solemn things!\' Like what?"\r\n\r\n"Like--being engaged."\r\n\r\nBut George had become altogether jubilant, and he laughed triumphantly.\r\n"Good gracious, that isn\'t solemn!"\r\n\r\n"It is, too!" she said, wiping her eyes. "It\'s too solemn for us."\r\n\r\n"No, it isn\'t! I--"\r\n\r\n"Let\'s sit down and be sensible, dear," she said. "You sit over there--"\r\n\r\n"I will if you\'ll call me, \'dear\' again."\r\n\r\n"No," she said. "I\'ll only call you that once again this summer--the\r\nnight before you go away."\r\n\r\n"That will have to do, then," he laughed, "so long as I know we\'re\r\nengaged."\r\n\r\n"But we\'re not!" she protested. "And we never will be, if you don\'t\r\npromise not to speak of it again until--until I tell you to!"\r\n\r\n"I won\'t promise that," said the happy George. "I\'ll only promise not to\r\nspeak of it till the next time you call me \'dear\'; and you\'ve promised\r\nto call me that the night before I leave for my senior year."\r\n\r\n"Oh, but I didn\'t!" she said earnestly, then hesitated. "Did I?"\r\n\r\n"Didn\'t you?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t think I meant it," she murmured, her wet lashes flickering\r\nabove troubled eyes.\r\n\r\n"I know one thing about you," he said gayly, his triumph increasing.\r\n"You never went back on anything you said, yet, and I\'m not afraid of\r\nthis being the first time!"\r\n\r\n"But we mustn\'t let--" she faltered; then went on tremulously, "George,\r\nwe\'ve got on so well together, we won\'t let this make a difference\r\nbetween us, will we?" And she joined in his laughter.\r\n\r\n"It will all depend on what you tell me the night before I go away. You\r\nagree we\'re going to settle things then, don\'t you, Lucy?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t promise."\r\n\r\n"Yes, you do! Don\'t you?"\r\n\r\n"Well--"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XIII\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTonight George began a jubilant warfare upon his Aunt Fanny, opening\r\nthe campaign upon his return home at about eleven o\'clock. Fanny had\r\nretired, and was presumably asleep, but George, on the way to his own\r\nroom, paused before her door, and serenaded her in a full baritone:\r\n\r\n    "As I walk along the Boy de Balong\r\n     With my independent air,\r\n     The people all declare,\r\n     \'He must be a millionaire!\'\r\n     Oh, you hear them sigh, and wish to die,\r\n     And see them wink the other eye.\r\n     At the man that broke the bank at Monte Carlo!"\r\n\r\nIsabel came from George\'s room, where she had been reading, waiting for\r\nhim. "I\'m afraid you\'ll disturb your father, dear. I wish you\'d sing\r\nmore, though--in the daytime! You have a splendid voice."\r\n\r\n"Good-night, old lady!"\r\n\r\n"I thought perhaps I--Didn\'t you want me to come in with you and talk a\r\nlittle?"\r\n\r\n"Not to-night. You go to bed. Good-night, old lady!"\r\n\r\nHe kissed her hilariously, entered his room with a skip, closed his door\r\nnoisily; and then he could be heard tossing things about, loudly humming\r\n"The Man that Broke the Bank at Monte Carlo."\r\n\r\nSmiling, his mother knelt outside his door to pray; then, with her\r\n"Amen," pressed her lips to the bronze door-knob; and went silently to\r\nher own apartment.\r\n\r\nAfter breakfasting in bed, George spent the next morning at his\r\ngrandfather\'s and did not encounter his Aunt Fanny until lunch, when she\r\nseemed to be ready for him.\r\n\r\n"Thank you so much for the serenade, George!" she said. "Your poor\r\nfather tells me he\'d just got to sleep for the first time in two nights,\r\nbut after your kind attentions he lay awake the rest of last night."\r\n\r\n"Perfectly true," Mr. Minafer said grimly.\r\n\r\n"Of course, I didn\'t know, sir," George hastened to assure him. "I\'m\r\nawfully sorry. But Aunt Fanny was so gloomy and excited before I went\r\nout, last evening, I thought she needed cheering up."\r\n\r\n"I!" Fanny jeered. "I was gloomy? I was excited? You mean about that\r\nengagement?"\r\n\r\n"Yes. Weren\'t you? I thought I heard you worrying over somebody\'s being\r\nengaged. Didn\'t I hear you say you\'d heard Mr. Eugene Morgan was engaged\r\nto marry some pretty little seventeen-year-old girl?"\r\n\r\nFanny was stung, but she made a brave effort. "Did you ask Lucy?" she\r\nsaid, her voice almost refusing the teasing laugh she tried to make it\r\nutter. "Did you ask her when Fred Kinney and she--"\r\n\r\n"Yes. That story wasn\'t true. But the other one--" Here he stared at\r\nFanny, and then affected dismay. "Why, what\'s the matter with your face,\r\nAunt Fanny? It seems agitated!"\r\n\r\n"Agitated!" Fanny said disdainfully, but her voice undeniably lacked\r\nsteadiness. "Agitated!"\r\n\r\n"Oh, come!" Mr. Minafer interposed. "Let\'s have a little peace!"\r\n\r\n"I\'m willing," said George. "I don\'t want to see poor Aunt Fanny all\r\nstirred up over a rumour I just this minute invented myself. She\'s so\r\nexcitable--about certain subjects--it\'s hard to control her." He turned\r\nto his mother. "What\'s the matter with grandfather?"\r\n\r\n"Didn\'t you see him this morning?" Isabel asked.\r\n\r\n"Yes. He was glad to see me, and all that, but he seemed pretty fidgety.\r\nHas he been having trouble with his heart again?"\r\n\r\n"Not lately. No."\r\n\r\n"Well, he\'s not himself. I tried to talk to him about the estate; it\'s\r\ndisgraceful--it really is--the way things are looking. He wouldn\'t\r\nlisten, and he seemed upset. What\'s he upset over?"\r\n\r\nIsabel looked serious; however, it was her husband who suggested\r\ngloomily, "I suppose the Major\'s bothered about this Sydney and Amelia\r\nbusiness, most likely."\r\n\r\n"What Sydney and Amelia business?" George asked.\r\n\r\n"Your mother can tell you, if she wants to," Minafer said. "It\'s not my\r\nside of the family, so I keep off."\r\n\r\n"It\'s rather disagreeable for all of us, Georgie," Isabel began. "You\r\nsee, your Uncle Sydney wanted a diplomatic position, and he thought\r\nbrother George, being in Congress, could arrange it. George did get him\r\nthe offer of a South American ministry, but Sydney wanted a European\r\nambassadorship, and he got quite indignant with poor George for thinking\r\nhe\'d take anything smaller--and he believes George didn\'t work hard\r\nenough for him. George had done his best, of course, and now he\'s out\r\nof Congress, and won\'t run again--so there\'s Sydney\'s idea of a big\r\ndiplomatic position gone for good. Well, Sydney and your Aunt Amelia are\r\nterribly disappointed, and they say they\'ve been thinking for years\r\nthat this town isn\'t really fit to live in--\'for a gentleman,\' Sydney\r\nsays--and it is getting rather big and dirty. So they\'ve sold their\r\nhouse and decided to go abroad to live permanently; there\'s a villa near\r\nFlorence they\'ve often talked of buying. And they want father to let\r\nthem have their share of the estate now, instead of waiting for him to\r\nleave it to them in his will."\r\n\r\n"Well, I suppose that\'s fair enough," George said. "That is, in case he\r\nintended to leave them a certain amount in his will."\r\n\r\n"Of course that\'s understood, Georgie. Father explained his will to us\r\nlong ago; a third to them, and a third to brother George, and a third to\r\nus."\r\n\r\nHer son made a simple calculation in his mind. Uncle George was a\r\nbachelor, and probably would never marry; Sydney and Amelia were\r\nchildless. The Major\'s only grandchild appeared to remain the eventual\r\nheir of the entire property, no matter if the Major did turn over\r\nto Sydney a third of it now. And George had a fragmentary vision\r\nof himself, in mourning, arriving to take possession of a historic\r\nFlorentine villa--he saw himself walking up a cypress-bordered path,\r\nwith ancient carven stone balustrades in the distance, and servants\r\nin mourning livery greeting the new signore. "Well, I suppose it\'s\r\ngrandfather\'s own affair. He can do it or not, just as he likes. I don\'t\r\nsee why he\'d mind much."\r\n\r\n"He seemed rather confused and pained about it," Isabel said. "I think\r\nthey oughtn\'t to urge it. George says that the estate won\'t stand taking\r\nout the third that Sydney wants, and that Sydney and Amelia are behaving\r\nlike a couple of pigs." She laughed, continuing, "Of course I don\'t know\r\nwhether they are or not: I never have understood any more about business\r\nmyself than a little pig would! But I\'m on George\'s side, whether he\'s\r\nright or wrong; I always was from the time we were children: and Sydney\r\nand Amelia are hurt with me about it, I\'m afraid. They\'ve stopped\r\nspeaking to George entirely. Poor father Family rows at his time of\r\nlife."\r\n\r\nGeorge became thoughtful. If Sydney and Amelia were behaving like pigs,\r\nthings might not be so simple as at first they seemed to be. Uncle\r\nSydney and Aunt Amelia might live an awful long while, he thought; and\r\nbesides, people didn\'t always leave their fortunes to relatives. Sydney\r\nmight die first, leaving everything to his widow, and some curly-haired\r\nItalian adventurer might get round her, over there in Florence; she\r\nmight be fool enough to marry again--or even adopt somebody!\r\n\r\nHe became more and more thoughtful, forgetting entirely a plan he had\r\nformed for the continued teasing of his Aunt Fanny; and, an hour after\r\nlunch, he strolled over to his grandfather\'s, intending to apply for\r\nfurther information, as a party rightfully interested.\r\n\r\nHe did not carry out this intention, however. Going into the big house\r\nby a side entrance, he was informed that the Major was upstairs in his\r\nbedroom, that his sons Sydney and George were both with him, and that a\r\nserious argument was in progress. "You kin stan\' right in de middle dat\r\nbig, sta\'y-way," said Old Sam, the ancient negro, who was his informant,\r\n"an\' you kin heah all you a-mind to wivout goin\' on up no fudda. Mist\'\r\nSydney an\' Mist\' Jawge talkin\' louduh\'n I evuh heah nobody ca\'y on in\r\nnish heah house! Quollin\', honey, big quollin\'!"\r\n\r\n"All right," said George shortly. "You go on back to your own part of\r\nthe house, and don\'t make any talk. Hear me?"\r\n\r\n"Yessuh, yessuh," Sam chuckled, as he shuffled away. "Plenty talkin\'\r\nwivout Sam! Yessuh!"\r\n\r\nGeorge went to the foot of the great stairway. He could hear angry\r\nvoices overhead--those of his two uncles--and a plaintive murmur, as if\r\nthe Major tried to keep the peace. Such sounds were far from encouraging\r\nto callers, and George decided not to go upstairs until this interview\r\nwas over. His decision was the result of no timidity, nor of a too\r\nsensitive delicacy. What he felt was, that if he interrupted the scene\r\nin his grandfather\'s room, just at this time, one of the three gentlemen\r\nengaging in it might speak to him in a peremptory manner (in the heat\r\nof the moment) and George saw no reason for exposing his dignity to such\r\nmischances. Therefore he turned from the stairway, and going quietly\r\ninto the library, picked up a magazine--but he did not open it, for his\r\nattention was instantly arrested by his Aunt Amelia\'s voice, speaking in\r\nthe next room. The door was open and George heard her distinctly.\r\n\r\n"Isabel does? Isabel!" she exclaimed, her tone high and shrewish. "You\r\nneedn\'t tell me anything about Isabel Minafer, I guess, my dear old\r\nFrank Bronson! I know her a little better than you do, don\'t you think?"\r\n\r\nGeorge heard the voice of Mr. Bronson replying--a voice familiar to him\r\nas that of his grandfather\'s attorney-in-chief and chief intimate as\r\nwell. He was a contemporary of the Major\'s, being over seventy, and they\r\nhad been through three years of the War in the same regiment. Amelia\r\naddressed him now, with an effect of angry mockery, as "my dear old\r\nFrank Bronson"; but that (without the mockery) was how the Amberson\r\nfamily almost always spoke of him: "dear old Frank Bronson." He was a\r\nhale, thin old man, six feet three inches tall, and without a stoop.\r\n\r\n"I doubt your knowing Isabel," he said stiffly. "You speak of her as you\r\ndo because she sides with her brother George, instead of with you and\r\nSydney."\r\n\r\n"Pooh!" Aunt Amelia was evidently in a passion. "You know what\'s been\r\ngoing on over there, well enough, Frank Bronson!"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t even know what you\'re talking about."\r\n\r\n"Oh, you don\'t? You don\'t know that Isabel takes George\'s side simply\r\nbecause he\'s Eugene Morgan\'s best friend?"\r\n\r\n"It seems to me you\'re talking pure nonsense," said Bronson sharply.\r\n"Not impure nonsense, I hope!"\r\n\r\nAmelia became shrill. "I thought you were a man of the world: don\'t\r\ntell me you\'re blind! For nearly two years Isabel\'s been pretending\r\nto chaperone Fanny Minafer with Eugene, and all the time she\'s been\r\ndragging that poor fool Fanny around to chaperone her and Eugene! Under\r\nthe circumstances, she knows people will get to thinking Fanny\'s a\r\npretty slim kind of chaperone, and Isabel wants to please George because\r\nshe thinks there\'ll be less talk if she can keep her own brother around,\r\nseeming to approve. \'Talk!\' She\'d better look out! The whole town will\r\nbe talking, the first thing she knows! She--"\r\n\r\nAmelia stopped, and stared at the doorway in a panic, for her nephew\r\nstood there.\r\n\r\nShe kept her eyes upon his white face for a few strained moments, then,\r\nregaining her nerve, looked away and shrugged her shoulders.\r\n\r\n"You weren\'t intended to hear what I\'ve been saying, George," she\r\nsaid quietly. "But since you seem to--"\r\n\r\n"Yes, I did."\r\n\r\n"So!" She shrugged her shoulders again. "After all, I don\'t know but\r\nit\'s just as well, in the long run."\r\n\r\nHe walked up to where she sat. "You--you--" he said thickly. "It\r\nseems--it seems to me you\'re--you\'re pretty common!"\r\n\r\nAmelia tried to give the impression of an unconcerned person laughing\r\nwith complete indifference, but the sounds she produced were disjointed\r\nand uneasy. She fanned herself, looking out of the open window near her.\r\n"Of course, if you want to make more trouble in the family than we\'ve\r\nalready got, George, with your eavesdropping, you can go and repeat--"\r\n\r\nOld Bronson had risen from his chair in great distress. "Your aunt was\r\ntalking nonsense because she\'s piqued over a business matter, George,"\r\nhe said. "She doesn\'t mean what she said, and neither she nor any one\r\nelse gives the slightest credit to such foolishness--no one in the\r\nworld!"\r\n\r\nGeorge gulped, and wet lines shone suddenly along his lower eyelids.\r\n"They--they\'d better not!" he said, then stalked out of the room, and\r\nout of the house. He stamped fiercely across the stone slabs of the\r\nfront porch, descended the steps, and halted abruptly, blinking in the\r\nstrong sunshine.\r\n\r\nIn front of his own gate, beyond the Major\'s broad lawn, his mother was\r\njust getting into her victoria, where sat already his Aunt Fanny\r\nand Lucy Morgan. It was a summer fashion-picture: the three ladies\r\ncharmingly dressed, delicate parasols aloft; the lines of the victoria\r\ngraceful as those of a violin; the trim pair of bays in glistening\r\nharness picked out with silver, and the serious black driver whom\r\nIsabel, being an Amberson, dared even in that town to put into a black\r\nlivery coat, boots, white breeches, and cockaded hat. They jingled\r\nsmartly away, and, seeing George standing on the Major\'s lawn, Lucy\r\nwaved, and Isabel threw him a kiss.\r\n\r\nBut George shuddered, pretending not to see them, and stooped as if\r\nsearching for something lost in the grass, protracting that posture\r\nuntil the victoria was out of hearing. And ten minutes later, George\r\nAmberson, somewhat in the semblance of an angry person plunging out of\r\nthe Mansion, found a pale nephew waiting to accost him.\r\n\r\n"I haven\'t time to talk, Georgie."\r\n\r\n"Yes, you have. You\'d better!"\r\n\r\n"What\'s the matter, then?"\r\n\r\nHis namesake drew him away from the vicinity of the house. "I want to\r\ntell you something I just heard Aunt Amelia say, in there."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t want to hear it," said Amberson. "I\'ve been hearing entirely\r\ntoo much of what \'Aunt Amelia, says, lately."\r\n\r\n"She says my mother\'s on your side about this division of the property\r\nbecause you\'re Eugene Morgan\'s best friend."\r\n\r\n"What in the name of heaven has that got to do with your mother\'s being\r\non my side?"\r\n\r\n"She said--" George paused to swallow. "She said--" He faltered.\r\n\r\n"You look sick," said his uncle; and laughed shortly. "If it\'s because\r\nof anything Amelia\'s been saying, I don\'t blame you! What else did she\r\nsay?"\r\n\r\nGeorge swallowed again, as with nausea, but under his uncle\'s\r\nencouragement he was able to be explicit. "She said my mother wanted you\r\nto be friendly to her about Eugene Morgan. She said my mother had been\r\nusing Aunt Fanny as a chaperone."\r\n\r\nAmberson emitted a laugh of disgust. "It\'s wonderful what tommy-rot a\r\nwoman in a state of spite can think of! I suppose you don\'t doubt that\r\nAmelia Amberson created this specimen of tommy-rot herself?"\r\n\r\n"I know she did."\r\n\r\n"Then what\'s the matter?"\r\n\r\n"She said--" George faltered again. "She said--she implied people\r\nwere--were talking about it."\r\n\r\n"Of all the damn nonsense!" his uncle exclaimed. George looked at him\r\nhaggardly. "You\'re sure they\'re not?"\r\n\r\n"Rubbish! Your mother\'s on my side about this division because she knows\r\nSydney\'s a pig and always has been a pig, and so has his spiteful wife.\r\nI\'m trying to keep them from getting the better of your mother as well\r\nas from getting the better of me, don\'t you suppose? Well, they\'re in\r\na rage because Sydney always could do what he liked with father unless\r\nyour mother interfered, and they know I got Isabel to ask him not to\r\ndo what they wanted. They\'re keeping up the fight and they\'re sore--and\r\nAmelia\'s a woman who always says any damn thing that comes into her\r\nhead! That\'s all there is to it."\r\n\r\n"But she said," George persisted wretchedly; "she said there was talk.\r\nShe said--"\r\n\r\n"Look here, young fellow!" Amberson laughed good-naturedly. "There\r\nprobably is some harmless talk about the way your Aunt Fanny goes after\r\npoor Eugene, and I\'ve no doubt I\'ve abetted it myself. People can\'t help\r\nbeing amused by a thing like that. Fanny was always languishing at him,\r\ntwenty-odd years ago, before he left here. Well, we can\'t blame the poor\r\nthing if she\'s got her hopes up again, and I don\'t know that I blame\r\nher, myself, for using your mother the way she does."\r\n\r\n"How do you mean?"\r\n\r\nAmberson put his hand on George\'s shoulder. "You like to tease Fanny,"\r\nhe said, "but I wouldn\'t tease her about this, if I were you. Fanny\r\nhasn\'t got much in her life. You know, Georgie, just being an aunt isn\'t\r\nreally the great career it may sometimes appear to you! In fact, I\r\ndon\'t know of anything much that Fanny has got, except her feeling\r\nabout Eugene. She\'s always had it--and what\'s funny to us is pretty much\r\nlife-and-death to her, I suspect. Now, I\'ll not deny that Eugene Morgan\r\nis attracted to your mother. He is; and that\'s another case of \'always\r\nwas\'; but I know him, and he\'s a knight, George--a crazy one, perhaps,\r\nif you\'ve read \'Don Quixote.\' And I think your mother likes him better\r\nthan she likes any man outside her own family, and that he interests her\r\nmore than anybody else--and \'always has.\' And that\'s all there is to it,\r\nexcept--"\r\n\r\n"Except what?" George asked quickly, as he paused.\r\n\r\n"Except that I suspect--" Amberson chuckled, and began over: "I\'ll tell\r\nyou in confidence. I think Fanny\'s a fairly tricky customer, for such an\r\ninnocent old girl! There isn\'t any real harm in her, but she\'s a great\r\ndiplomatist--lots of cards up her lace sleeves, Georgie! By the way,\r\ndid you ever notice how proud she is of her arms? Always flashing \'em at\r\npoor Eugene!" And he stopped to laugh again.\r\n\r\n"I don\'t see anything confidential about that," George complained. "I\r\nthought--"\r\n\r\n"Wait a minute! My idea is--don\'t forget it\'s a confidential one, but\r\nI\'m devilish right about it, young Georgie!--it\'s this: Fanny uses your\r\nmother for a decoy duck. She does everything in the world she can to\r\nkeep your mother\'s friendship with Eugene going, because she thinks\r\nthat\'s what keeps Eugene about the place, so to speak. Fanny\'s always\r\nwith your mother, you see; and whenever he sees Isabel he sees Fanny.\r\nFanny thinks he\'ll get used to the idea of her being around, and some\r\nday her chance may come! You see, she\'s probably afraid--perhaps she\r\neven knows, poor thing!--that she wouldn\'t get to see much of Eugene if\r\nit weren\'t for Isabel\'s being such a friend of his. There! D\'you see?"\r\n\r\n"Well--I suppose so." George\'s brow was still dark, however. "If you\'re\r\nsure whatever talk there is, is about Aunt Fanny. If that\'s so--"\r\n\r\n"Don\'t be an ass," his uncle advised him lightly, moving away. "I\'m off\r\nfor a week\'s fishing to forget that woman in there, and her pig of a\r\nhusband." (His gesture toward the Mansion indicated Mr. and Mrs. Sydney\r\nAmberson.) "I recommend a like course to you, if you\'re silly enough to\r\npay any attention to such rubbishings! Good-bye!"\r\n\r\nGeorge was partially reassured, but still troubled: a word haunted him\r\nlike the recollection of a nightmare. "Talk!"\r\n\r\nHe stood looking at the houses across the street from the Mansion;\r\nand though the sunshine was bright upon them, they seemed mysteriously\r\nthreatening. He had always despised them, except the largest of them,\r\nwhich was the home of his henchman, Charlie Johnson. The Johnsons had\r\noriginally owned a lot three hundred feet wide, but they had sold all of\r\nit except the meager frontage before the house itself, and five\r\nhouses were now crowded into the space where one used to squire it so\r\nspaciously. Up and down the street, the same transformation had taken\r\nplace: every big, comfortable old brick house now had two or three\r\nsmaller frame neighbours crowding up to it on each side, cheap-looking\r\nneighbours, most of them needing paint and not clean--and yet, though\r\nthey were cheap looking, they had cost as much to build as the big brick\r\nhouses, whose former ample yards they occupied. Only where George stood\r\nwas there left a sward as of yore; the great, level, green lawn that\r\nserved for both the Major\'s house and his daughter\'s. This serene\r\ndomain--unbroken, except for the two gravelled carriage-drives--alone\r\nremained as it had been during the early glories of the Amberson\r\nAddition.\r\n\r\nGeorge stared at the ugly houses opposite, and hated them more than\r\never; but he shivered. Perhaps the riffraff living in those houses sat\r\nat the windows to watch their betters; perhaps they dared to gossip--\r\n\r\nHe uttered an exclamation, and walked rapidly toward his own front gate.\r\nThe victoria had returned with Miss Fanny alone; she jumped out briskly\r\nand the victoria waited.\r\n\r\n"Where\'s mother?" George asked sharply, as he met her.\r\n\r\n"At Lucy\'s. I only came back to get some embroidery, because we found\r\nthe sun too hot for driving. I\'m in a hurry."\r\n\r\nBut, going into the house with her, he detained her when she would have\r\nhastened upstairs.\r\n\r\n"I haven\'t time to talk now, Georgie; I\'m going right back. I promised\r\nyour mother--"\r\n\r\n"You listen!" said George.\r\n\r\n"What on earth--"\r\n\r\nHe repeated what Amelia had said. This time, however, he spoke coldly,\r\nand without the emotion he had exhibited during the recital to his\r\nuncle: Fanny was the one who showed agitation during this interview, for\r\nshe grew fiery red, and her eyes dilated. "What on earth do you want to\r\nbring such trash to me for?" she demanded, breathing fast.\r\n\r\n"I merely wished to know two things: whether it is your duty or mine to\r\nspeak to father of what Aunt Amelia--"\r\n\r\nFanny stamped her foot. "You little fool!" she cried. "You awful little\r\nfool!"\r\n\r\n"I decline--"\r\n\r\n"Decline, my hat! Your father\'s a sick man, and you--"\r\n\r\n"He doesn\'t seem so to me."\r\n\r\n"Well, he does to me! And you want to go troubling him with an Amberson\r\nfamily row! It\'s just what that cat would love you to do!"\r\n\r\n"Well, I--"\r\n\r\n"Tell your father if you like! It will only make him a little sicker to\r\nthink he\'s got a son silly enough to listen to such craziness!"\r\n\r\n"Then you\'re sure there isn\'t any talk?" Fanny disdained a reply in\r\nwords. She made a hissing sound of utter contempt and snapped her\r\nfingers. Then she asked scornfully: "What\'s the other thing you wanted\r\nto know?"\r\n\r\nGeorge\'s pallor increased. "Whether it mightn\'t be better, under the\r\ncircumstances," he said, "if this family were not so intimate with the\r\nMorgan family--at least for a time. It might be better--"\r\n\r\nFanny stared at him incredulously. "You mean you\'d quit seeing Lucy?"\r\n\r\n"I hadn\'t thought of that side of it, but if such a thing were necessary\r\non account of talk about my mother, I--I--" He hesitated unhappily. "I\r\nsuggested that if all of us--for a time--perhaps only for a time--it\r\nmight be better if--"\r\n\r\n"See here," she interrupted. "We\'ll settle this nonsense right now. If\r\nEugene Morgan comes to this house, for instance, to see me, your mother\r\ncan\'t get up and leave the place the minute he gets here, can she? What\r\ndo you want her to do: insult him? Or perhaps you\'d prefer she\'d insult\r\nLucy? That would do just as well. What is it you\'re up to, anyhow? Do\r\nyou really love your Aunt Amelia so much that you want to please her?\r\nOr do you really hate your Aunt Fanny so much that you want to--that you\r\nwant to--"\r\n\r\nShe choked and sought for her handkerchief; suddenly she began to cry.\r\n\r\n"Oh, see here," George said. "I don\'t hate you," Aunt Fanny. "That\'s\r\nsilly. I don\'t--"\r\n\r\n"You do! You do! You want to--you want to destroy the only thing--that\r\nI--that I ever--" And, unable to continue, she became inaudible in her\r\nhandkerchief.\r\n\r\nGeorge felt remorseful, and his own troubles were lightened: all at\r\nonce it became clear to him that he had been worrying about nothing. He\r\nperceived that his Aunt Amelia was indeed an old cat, and that to give\r\nher scandalous meanderings another thought would be the height of folly.\r\nBy no means unsusceptible to such pathos as that now exposed before\r\nhim, he did not lack pity for Fanny, whose almost spoken confession was\r\nlamentable; and he was granted the vision to understand that his mother\r\nalso pitied Fanny infinitely more than he did. This seemed to explain\r\neverything.\r\n\r\nHe patted the unhappy lady awkwardly upon her shoulder. "There, there!"\r\nhe said. "I didn\'t mean anything. Of course the only thing to do about\r\nAunt Amelia is to pay no attention to her. It\'s all right, Aunt Fanny.\r\nDon\'t cry. I feel a lot better now, myself. Come on; I\'ll drive back\r\nthere with you. It\'s all over, and nothing\'s the matter. Can\'t you cheer\r\nup?"\r\n\r\nFanny cheered up; and presently the customarily hostile aunt and\r\nnephew were driving out Amberson Boulevard amiably together in the hot\r\nsunshine.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XIV\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"Almost" was Lucy\'s last word on the last night of George\'s\r\nvacation--that vital evening which she had half consented to agree upon\r\nfor "settling things" between them. "Almost engaged," she meant. And\r\nGeorge, discontented with the "almost," but contented that she seemed\r\nglad to wear a sapphire locket with a tiny photograph of George Amberson\r\nMinafer inside it, found himself wonderful in a new world at the final\r\ninstant of their parting. For, after declining to let him kiss\r\nher "good-bye," as if his desire for such a ceremony were the most\r\npreposterous absurdity in the world, she had leaned suddenly close to\r\nhim and left upon his cheek the veriest feather from a fairy\'s wing.\r\n\r\nShe wrote him a month later:\r\n\r\nNo. It must keep on being almost.\r\n\r\nIsn\'t almost pretty pleasant? You know well enough that I care for you.\r\nI did from the first minute I saw you, and I\'m pretty sure you\r\nknew it--I\'m afraid you did. I\'m afraid you always knew it. I\'m not\r\nconventional and cautious about being engaged, as you say I am, dear. (I\r\nalways read over the "dears" in your letters a time or two, as you say\r\nyou do in mine--only I read all of your letters a time or two!) But\r\nit\'s such a solemn thing it scares me. It means a good deal to a lot\r\nof people besides you and me, and that scares me, too. You write that I\r\ntake your feeling for me "too lightly" and that I "take the whole affair\r\ntoo lightly." Isn\'t that odd! Because to myself I seem to take it\r\nas something so much more solemn than you do. I shouldn\'t be a bit\r\nsurprised to find myself an old lady, some day, still thinking of\r\nyou--while you\'d be away and away with somebody else perhaps, and me\r\nforgotten ages ago! "Lucy Morgan," you\'d say, when you saw my obituary.\r\n"Lucy Morgan? Let me see: I seem to remember the name. Didn\'t I know\r\nsome Lucy Morgan or other, once upon a time?" Then you\'d shake your\r\nbig white head and stroke your long white beard--you\'d have such a\r\ndistinguished long white beard! and you\'d say, \'No. I don\'t seem to\r\nremember any Lucy Morgan; I wonder what made me think I did?\' And poor\r\nme! I\'d be deep in the ground, wondering if you\'d heard about it and\r\nwhat you were saying! Good-bye for to-day. Don\'t work too hard--dear!\r\n\r\nGeorge immediately seized pen and paper, plaintively but vigorously\r\nrequesting Lucy not to imagine him with a beard, distinguished or\r\notherwise, even in the extremities of age. Then, after inscribing his\r\nprotest in the matter of this visioned beard, he concluded his missive\r\nin a tone mollified to tenderness, and proceeded to read a letter from\r\nhis mother which had reached him simultaneously with Lucy\'s. Isabel\r\nwrote from Asheville, where she had just arrived with her husband.\r\n\r\nI think your father looks better already, darling, though we\'ve been\r\nhere only a few hours It may be we\'ve found just the place to build him\r\nup. The doctors said they hoped it would prove to be, and if it is, it\r\nwould be worth the long struggle we had with him to get him to give up\r\nand come. Poor dear man, he was so blue, not about his health but about\r\ngiving up the worries down at his office and forgetting them for a\r\ntime--if he only will forget them! It took the pressure of the family\r\nand all his best friends, to get him to come--but father and brother\r\nGeorge and Fanny and Eugene Morgan all kept at him so constantly that he\r\njust had to give in. I\'m afraid that in my anxiety to get him to do what\r\nthe doctors wanted him to, I wasn\'t able to back up brother George as I\r\nshould in his difficulty with Sydney and Amelia. I\'m so sorry! George\r\nis more upset than I\'ve ever seen him--they\'ve got what they wanted, and\r\nthey\'re sailing before long, I hear, to live in Florence. Father said he\r\ncouldn\'t stand the constant persuading--I\'m afraid the word he used was\r\n"nagging." I can\'t understand people behaving like that. George says\r\nthey may be Ambersons, but they\'re vulgar! I\'m afraid I almost agree\r\nwith him. At least, I think they were inconsiderate. But I don\'t see\r\nwhy I\'m unburdening myself of all this to you, poor darling! We\'ll have\r\nforgotten all about it long before you come home for the holidays, and\r\nit should mean little or nothing to you, anyway. Forget that I\'ve been\r\nso foolish!\r\n\r\nYour father is waiting for me to take a walk with him--that\'s a splendid\r\nsign, because he hasn\'t felt he could walk much, at home, lately.
