"Now that\r\nit\'s done and too late I know what it was! I didn\'t have sense enough\r\njust to let things go on. I didn\'t have any business to interfere, and I\r\ndidn\'t mean to interfere--I only wanted to talk, and let out a little! I\r\ndid think you already knew everything I told you. I did! And I\'d rather\r\nhave cut my hand off than stir you up to doing what you have done! I was\r\njust suffering so that I wanted to let out a little--I didn\'t mean any\r\nreal harm. But now I see what\'s happened--oh, I was a fool! I hadn\'t any\r\nbusiness interfering. Eugene never would have looked at me, anyhow, and,\r\noh, why couldn\'t I have seen that before! He never came here a single\r\ntime in his life except on her account, never! and I might have let them\r\nalone, because he wouldn\'t have looked at me even if he\'d never seen\r\nIsabel. And they haven\'t done any harm: she made Wilbur happy, and she\r\nwas a true wife to him as long as he lived. It wasn\'t a crime for her to\r\ncare for Eugene all the time; she certainly never told him she did--and\r\nshe gave me every chance in the world! She left us alone together every\r\ntime she could--even since Wilbur died--but what was the use? And here\r\nI go, not doing myself a bit of good by it, and just"--Fanny wrung her\r\nhands again--"just ruining them!"\r\n\r\n"I suppose you mean I\'m doing that," George said bitterly.\r\n\r\n"Yes, I do!" she sobbed, and drooped upon the stairway railing,\r\nexhausted.\r\n\r\n"On the contrary, I mean to save my mother from a calamity."\r\n\r\nFanny looked at him wanly, in a tired despair; then she stepped by him\r\nand went slowly to her own door, where she paused and beckoned to him.\r\n\r\n"What do you want?"\r\n\r\n"Just come here a minute."\r\n\r\n"What for?" he asked impatiently.\r\n\r\n"I just wanted to say something to you."\r\n\r\n"Well, for heaven\'s sake, say it! There\'s nobody to hear." Nevertheless,\r\nafter a moment, as she beckoned him again, he went to her, profoundly\r\nannoyed. "Well, what is it?"\r\n\r\n"George," she said in a low voice, "I think you ought to be told\r\nsomething. If I were you, I\'d let my mother alone."\r\n\r\n"Oh, my Lord!" he groaned. "I\'m doing these things for her, not against\r\nher!"\r\n\r\nA mildness had come upon Fanny, and she had controlled her weeping. She\r\nshook her head gently. "No, I\'d let her alone if I were you. I don\'t\r\nthink she\'s very well, George."\r\n\r\n"She! I never saw a healthier person in my life."\r\n\r\n"No. She doesn\'t let anybody know, but she goes to the doctor\r\nregularly."\r\n\r\n"Women are always going to doctors regularly."\r\n\r\n"No. He told her to."\r\n\r\nGeorge was not impressed. "It\'s nothing at all; she spoke of it to me\r\nyears ago--some kind of family failing. She said grandfather had it,\r\ntoo; and look at him! Hasn\'t proved very serious with him! You act as if\r\nI\'d done something wrong in sending that man about his business, and as\r\nif I were going to persecute my mother, instead of protecting her. By\r\nJove, it\'s sickening! You told me how all the riffraff in town were busy\r\nwith her name, and then the minute I lift my hand to protect her, you\r\nbegin to attack me and--"\r\n\r\n"Sh!" Fanny checked him, laying her hand on his arm. "Your uncle is\r\ngoing."\r\n\r\nThe library doors were heard opening, and a moment later there came the\r\nsound of the front door closing.\r\n\r\nGeorge moved toward the head of the stairs, then stood listening; but\r\nthe house was silent.\r\n\r\nFanny made a slight noise with her lips to attract his attention, and,\r\nwhen he glanced toward her, shook her head at him urgently. "Let her\r\nalone," she whispered. "She\'s down there by herself. Don\'t go down. Let\r\nher alone."\r\n\r\nShe moved a few steps toward him and halted, her face pallid and\r\nawestruck, and then both stood listening for anything that might break\r\nthe silence downstairs. No sound came to them; that poignant silence was\r\ncontinued throughout long, long minutes, while the two listeners\r\nstood there under its mysterious spell; and in its plaintive\r\neloquence--speaking, as it did, of the figure alone in the big,\r\ndark library, where dead Wilbur\'s new silver frame gleamed in the\r\ndimness--there was something that checked even George.\r\n\r\nAbove the aunt and nephew, as they kept this strange vigil, there was\r\na triple window of stained glass, to illumine the landing and upper\r\nreaches of the stairway. Figures in blue and amber garments posed\r\ngracefully in panels, conceived by some craftsman of the Eighties to\r\nrepresent Love and Purity and Beauty, and these figures, leaded to\r\nunalterable attitudes, were little more motionless than the two human\r\nbeings upon whom fell the mottled faint light of the window. The colours\r\nwere growing dull; evening was coming on.\r\n\r\nFanny Minafer broke the long silence with a sound from her throat, a\r\nstilled gasp; and with that great companion of hers, her handkerchief,\r\nretired softly to the loneliness of her own chamber. After she had gone\r\nGeorge looked about him bleakly, then on tiptoe crossed the hall and\r\nwent into his own room, which was filled with twilight. Still tiptoeing,\r\nthough he could not have said why, he went across the room and sat down\r\nheavily in a chair facing the window. Outside there was nothing but the\r\ndarkening air and the wall of the nearest of the new houses. He had\r\nnot slept at all, the night before, and he had eaten nothing since the\r\npreceding day at lunch, but he felt neither drowsiness nor hunger. His\r\nset determination filled him, kept him but too wide awake, and his gaze\r\nat the grayness beyond the window was wide-eyed and bitter.\r\n\r\nDarkness had closed in when there was a step in the room behind him.\r\nThen someone knelt beside the chair, two arms went round him with\r\ninfinite compassion, a gentle head rested against his shoulder, and\r\nthere came the faint scent as of apple-blossoms far away.\r\n\r\n"You mustn\'t be troubled, darling," his mother whispered.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXVI\r\n\r\n\r\nGeorge choked. For an instant he was on the point of breaking down, but\r\nhe commanded himself, bravely dismissing the self-pity roused by her\r\ncompassion. "How can I help but be?" he said.\r\n\r\n"No, no." She soothed him. "You mustn\'t. You mustn\'t be troubled, no\r\nmatter what happens."\r\n\r\n"That\'s easy enough to say!" he protested; and he moved as if to rise.\r\n\r\n"Just let\'s stay like this a little while, dear. Just a minute or two.\r\nI want to tell you: brother George has been here, and he told me\r\neverything about--about how unhappy you\'d been--and how you went so\r\ngallantly to that old woman with the operaglasses." Isabel gave a sad\r\nlittle laugh. "What a terrible old woman she is! What a really terrible\r\nthing a vulgar old woman can be!"\r\n\r\n"Mother, I--" And again he moved to rise.\r\n\r\n"Must you? It seemed to me such a comfortable way to talk. Well--" She\r\nyielded; he rose, helped her to her feet, and pressed the light into\r\nbeing.\r\n\r\nAs the room took life from the sudden lines of fire within the bulbs\r\nIsabel made a deprecatory gesture, and, with a faint laugh of apologetic\r\nprotest, turned quickly away from George. What she meant was: "You\r\nmustn\'t see my face until I\'ve made it nicer for you." Then she turned\r\nagain to him, her eyes downcast, but no sign of tears in them, and she\r\ncontrived to show him that there was the semblance of a smile upon her\r\nlips. She still wore her hat, and in her unsteady fingers she held a\r\nwhite envelope, somewhat crumpled.\r\n\r\n"Now, mother--"\r\n\r\n"Wait, dearest," she said; and though he stood stone cold, she lifted\r\nher arms, put them round him again, and pressed her cheek lightly to\r\nhis. "Oh, you do look so troubled, poor dear! One thing you couldn\'t\r\ndoubt, beloved boy: you know I could never care for anything in the\r\nworld as I care for you--never, never!"\r\n\r\n"Now, mother--"\r\n\r\nShe released him, and stepped back. "Just a moment more, dearest. I want\r\nyou to read this first. We can get at things better." She pressed into\r\nhis hand the envelope she had brought with her, and as he opened it, and\r\nbegan to read the long enclosure, she walked slowly to the other end of\r\nthe room; then stood there, with her back to him, and her head drooping\r\na little, until he had finished.\r\n\r\nThe sheets of paper were covered with Eugene\'s handwriting.\r\n\r\nGeorge Amberson will bring you this, dear Isabel. He is waiting while I\r\nwrite. He and I have talked things over, and before he gives this to you\r\nhe will tell you what has happened. Of course I\'m rather confused, and\r\nhaven\'t had time to think matters out very definitely, and yet I believe\r\nI should have been better prepared for what took place to-day--I ought\r\nto have known it was coming, because I have understood for quite a long\r\ntime that young George was getting to dislike me more and more. Somehow,\r\nI\'ve never been able to get his friendship; he\'s always had a latent\r\ndistrust of me--or something like distrust--and perhaps that\'s made me\r\nsometimes a little awkward and diffident with him. I think it may be\r\nhe felt from the first that I cared a great deal about you, and he\r\nnaturally resented it. I think perhaps he felt this even during all the\r\ntime when I was so careful--at least I thought I was--not to show, even\r\nto you, how immensely I did care. And he may have feared that you were\r\nthinking too much about me--even when you weren\'t and only liked me as\r\nan old friend. It\'s perfectly comprehensible to me, also, that at his\r\nage one gets excited about gossip. Dear Isabel, what I\'m trying to\r\nget at, in my confused way, is that you and I don\'t care about this\r\nnonsensical gossip, ourselves, at all. Yesterday I thought the time had\r\ncome when I could ask you to marry me, and you were dear enough to tell\r\nme "sometime it might come to that." Well, you and I, left to ourselves,\r\nand knowing what we have been and what we are, we\'d pay as much\r\nattention to "talk" as we would to any other kind of old cats\' mewing!\r\nWe\'d not be very apt to let such things keep us from the plenty of life\r\nwe have left to us for making up to ourselves for old unhappinesses and\r\nmistakes. But now we\'re faced with--not the slander and not our own\r\nfear of it, because we haven\'t any, but someone else\'s fear of it--your\r\nson\'s. And, oh, dearest woman in the world, I know what your son is to\r\nyou, and it frightens me! Let me explain a little: I don\'t think he\'ll\r\nchange--at twenty-one or twenty-two so many things appear solid and\r\npermanent and terrible which forty sees are nothing but disappearing\r\nmiasma. Forty can\'t tell twenty about this; that\'s the pity of it!\r\nTwenty can find out only by getting to be forty. And so we come to this,\r\ndear: Will you live your own life your way, or George\'s way? I\'m going\r\na little further, because it would be fatal not to be wholly frank\r\nnow. George will act toward you only as your long worship of him, your\r\nsacrifices--all the unseen little ones every day since he was born--will\r\nmake him act. Dear, it breaks my heart for you, but what you have to\r\noppose now is the history of your own selfless and perfect motherhood. I\r\nremember saying once that what you worshipped in your son was the angel\r\nyou saw in him--and I still believe that is true of every mother. But in\r\na mother\'s worship she may not see that the Will in her son should not\r\nalways be offered incense along with the angel. I grow sick with fear\r\nfor you--for both you and me--when I think how the Will against us two\r\nhas grown strong through the love you have given the angel--and how\r\nlong your own sweet Will has served that other. Are you strong enough,\r\nIsabel? Can you make the fight? I promise you that if you will take\r\nheart for it, you will find so quickly that it has all amounted\r\nto nothing. You shall have happiness, and, in a little while, only\r\nhappiness. You need only to write me a line--I can\'t come to your\r\nhouse--and tell me where you will meet me. We will come back in a month,\r\nand the angel in your son will bring him to you; I promise it. What\r\nis good in him will grow so fine, once you have beaten the turbulent\r\nWill--but it must be beaten!\r\n\r\nYour brother, that good friend, is waiting with such patience; I should\r\nnot keep him longer--and I am saying too much for wisdom, I fear. But,\r\noh, my dear, won\'t you be strong--such a little short strength it would\r\nneed! Don\'t strike my life down twice, dear--this time I\'ve not deserved\r\nit.                                  Eugene.\r\n\r\nConcluding this missive, George tossed it abruptly from him so that one\r\nsheet fell upon his bed and the others upon the floor; and at the faint\r\nnoise of their falling Isabel came, and, kneeling, began to gather them\r\nup.\r\n\r\n"Did you read it, dear?"\r\n\r\nGeorge\'s face was pale no longer, but pink with fury. "Yes, I did."\r\n\r\n"All of it?" she asked gently, as she rose.\r\n\r\n"Certainly!"\r\n\r\nShe did not look at him, but kept her eyes downcast upon the letter in\r\nher hands, tremulously rearranging the sheets in order as she spoke--and\r\nthough she smiled, her smile was as tremulous as her hands. Nervousness\r\nand an irresistible timidity possessed her. "I--I wanted to say,\r\nGeorge," she faltered. "I felt that if--if some day it should happen--I\r\nmean, if you came to feel differently about it, and Eugene and I--that\r\nis if we found that it seemed the most sensible thing to do--I was\r\nafraid you might think it would be a little queer about--Lucy, I mean\r\nif--if she were your step-sister. Of course, she\'d not be even legally\r\nrelated to you, and if you--if you cared for her--"\r\n\r\nThus far she got stumblingly with what she wanted to say, while George\r\nwatched her with a gaze that grew harder and hotter; but here he cut her\r\noff. "I have already given up all idea of Lucy," he said. "Naturally,\r\nI couldn\'t have treated her father as I deliberately did treat him--I\r\ncould hardly have done that and expected his daughter ever to speak to\r\nme again."\r\n\r\nIsabel gave a quick cry of compassion, but he allowed her no opportunity\r\nto speak. "You needn\'t think I\'m making any particular sacrifice," he\r\nsaid sharply, "though I would, quickly enough, if I thought it necessary\r\nin a matter of honour like this. I was interested in her, and I could\r\neven say I did care for her; but she proved pretty satisfactorily that\r\nshe cared little enough about me! She went away right in the midst of\r\na--of a difference of opinion we were having; she didn\'t even let me\r\nknow she was going, and never wrote a line to me, and then came back\r\ntelling everybody she\'d had \'a perfectly gorgeous time!\' That\'s quite\r\nenough for me. I\'m not precisely the sort to arrange for that kind of\r\nthing to be done to me more than once! The truth is, we\'re not congenial\r\nand we\'d found that much out, at least, before she left. We should\r\nnever have been happy; she was \'superior\' all the time, and critical of\r\nme--not very pleasant, that! I was disappointed in her, and I might as\r\nwell say it. I don\'t think she has the very deepest nature in the world,\r\nand--"\r\n\r\nBut Isabel put her hand timidly on his arm. "Georgie, dear, this is only\r\na quarrel: all young people have them before they get adjusted, and you\r\nmustn\'t let--"\r\n\r\n"If you please!" he said emphatically, moving back from her. "This isn\'t\r\nthat kind. It\'s all over, and I don\'t care to speak of it again. It\'s\r\nsettled. Don\'t you understand?"\r\n\r\n"But, dear--"\r\n\r\n"No. I want to talk to you about this letter of her father\'s."\r\n\r\n"Yes, dear, that\'s why--"\r\n\r\n"It\'s simply the most offensive piece of writing that I\'ve ever held in\r\nmy hands!"\r\n\r\nShe stepped back from him, startled. "But, dear, I thought--"\r\n\r\n"I can\'t understand your even showing me such a thing!" he cried. "How\r\ndid you happen to bring it to me?"\r\n\r\n"Your uncle thought I\'d better. He thought it was the simplest thing to\r\ndo, and he said that he\'d suggested it to Eugene, and Eugene had agreed.\r\nThey thought--"\r\n\r\n"Yes!" George said bitterly. "I should like to hear what they thought!"\r\n\r\n"They thought it would be the most straightforward thing."\r\n\r\nGeorge drew a long breath. "Well, what do you think, mother?"\r\n\r\n"I thought it would be the simplest and most straightforward thing; I\r\nthought they were right."\r\n\r\n"Very well! We\'ll agree it was simple and straightforward. Now, what do\r\nyou think of that letter itself?"\r\n\r\nShe hesitated, looking away. "I--of course I don\'t agree with him in the\r\nway he speaks of you, dear--except about the angel! I don\'t agree with\r\nsome of the things he implies. You\'ve always been unselfish--nobody\r\nknows that better than your mother. When Fanny was left with nothing,\r\nyou were so quick and generous to give up what really should have come\r\nto you, and--"\r\n\r\n"And yet," George broke in, "you see what he implies about me. Don\'t you\r\nthink, really, that this was a pretty insulting letter for that man to\r\nbe asking you to hand your son?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, no!" she cried. "You can see how fair he means to be, and he didn\'t\r\nask for me to give it to you. It was brother George who--"\r\n\r\n"Never mind that, now! You say he tries to be fair, and yet do you\r\nsuppose it ever occurs to him that I\'m doing my simple duty? That I\'m\r\ndoing what my father would do if he were alive? That I\'m doing what my\r\nfather would ask me to do if he could speak from his grave out yonder?\r\nDo you suppose it ever occurs to that man for one minute that I\'m\r\nprotecting my mother?" George raised his voice, advancing upon the\r\nhelpless lady fiercely; and she could only bend her head before him. "He\r\ntalks about my \'Will\'--how it must be beaten down; yes, and he asks my\r\nmother to do that little thing to please him! What for? Why does he want\r\nme \'beaten\' by my mother? Because I\'m trying to protect her name! He\'s\r\ngot my mother\'s name bandied up and down the streets of this town till I\r\ncan\'t step in those streets without wondering what every soul I meet is\r\nthinking of me and of my family, and now he wants you to marry him so\r\nthat every gossip in town will say \'There! What did I tell you? I guess\r\nthat proves it\'s true!\' You can\'t get away from it; that\'s exactly what\r\nthey\'d say, and this man pretends he cares for you, and yet asks you to\r\nmarry him and give them the right to say it. He says he and you don\'t\r\ncare what they say, but I know better! He may not care--probably he\'s\r\nthat kind--but you do. There never was an Amberson yet that would let\r\nthe Amberson name go trailing in the dust like that! It\'s the proudest\r\nname in this town and it\'s going to stay the proudest; and I tell you\r\nthat\'s the deepest thing in my nature--not that I\'d expect Eugene Morgan\r\nto understand--the very deepest thing in my nature is to protect that\r\nname, and to fight for it to the last breath when danger threatens it,\r\nas it does now--through my mother!" He turned from her, striding up\r\nand down and tossing his arms about, in a tumult of gesture. "I can\'t\r\nbelieve it of you, that you\'d think of such a sacrilege! That\'s what it\r\nwould be--sacrilege! When he talks about your unselfishness toward me,\r\nhe\'s right--you have been unselfish and you have been a perfect mother.\r\nBut what about him? Is it unselfish of him to want you to throw away\r\nyour good name just to please him? That\'s all he asks of you--and to\r\nquit being my mother! Do you think I can believe you really care for\r\nhim? I don\'t! You are my mother and you\'re an Amberson--and I believe\r\nyou\'re too proud! You\'re too proud to care for a man who could write\r\nsuch a letter as that!" He stopped, faced her, and spoke with more\r\nself-control: "Well, what are you going to do about it, mother?"\r\n\r\nGeorge was right about his mother\'s being proud. And even when she\r\nlaughed with a negro gardener, or even those few times in her life\r\nwhen people saw her weep, Isabel had a proud look--something that was\r\nindependent and graceful and strong. But she did not have it now: she\r\nleaned against the wall, beside his dressing-table, and seemed beset\r\nwith humility and with weakness. Her head drooped.\r\n\r\n"What answer are you going to make to such a letter?" George demanded,\r\nlike a judge on the bench.\r\n\r\n"I--I don\'t quite know, dear," she murmured.\r\n\r\n"Wait," she begged him. "I\'m so--confused."\r\n\r\n"I want to know what you\'re going to write him. Do you think if you\r\ndid what he wants you to I could bear to stay another day in this town,\r\nmother? Do you think I could ever bear even to see you again if you\r\nmarried him? I\'d want to, but you surely know I just--couldn\'t!"\r\n\r\nShe made a futile gesture, and seemed to breathe with difficulty. "I--I\r\nwasn\'t--quite sure," she faltered, "about--about it\'s being wise for us\r\nto be married--even before knowing how you feel about it. I wasn\'t even\r\nsure it was quite fair to--to Eugene. I have--I seem to have that family\r\ntrouble--like father\'s--that I spoke to you about once." She managed a\r\ndeprecatory little dry laugh. "Not that it amounts to much, but I wasn\'t\r\nat all sure that it would be fair to him. Marrying doesn\'t mean so much,\r\nafter all--not at my age. It\'s enough to know that--that people think\r\nof you--and to see them. I thought we were all--oh, pretty happy the way\r\nthings were, and I don\'t think it would mean giving up a great deal\r\nfor him or me, either, if we just went on as we have been. I--I see him\r\nalmost every day, and--"\r\n\r\n"Mother!" George\'s voice was loud and stern. "Do you think you could go\r\non seeing him after this!"\r\n\r\nShe had been talking helplessly enough before; her tone was little more\r\nbroken now. "Not--not even--see him?"\r\n\r\n"How could you?" George cried. "Mother, it seems to me that if he ever\r\nset foot in this house again--oh! I can\'t speak of it! Could you see\r\nhim, knowing what talk it makes every time he turns into this street,\r\nand knowing what that means to me? Oh, I don\'t understand all this--I\r\ndon\'t! If you\'d told me, a year ago, that such things were going to\r\nhappen, I\'d have thought you were insane--and now I believe I am!"\r\n\r\nThen, after a preliminary gesture of despair, as though he meant harm to\r\nthe ceiling, he flung himself heavily, face downward, upon the bed. His\r\nanguish was none the less real for its vehemence; and the stricken lady\r\ncame to him instantly and bent over him, once more enfolding him in her\r\narms. She said nothing, but suddenly her tears fell upon his head; she\r\nsaw them, and seemed to be startled.\r\n\r\n"Oh, this won\'t do!" she said. "I\'ve never let you see me cry before,\r\nexcept when your father died. I mustn\'t!"\r\n\r\nAnd she ran from the room.\r\n\r\n...A little while after she had gone, George rose and began solemnly to\r\ndress for dinner. At one stage of these conscientious proceedings he put\r\non, temporarily, his long black velvet dressing-gown, and, happening\r\nto catch sight in his pier glass of the picturesque and medieval\r\nfigure thus presented, he paused to regard it; and something profoundly\r\ntheatrical in his nature came to the surface.\r\n\r\nHis lips moved; he whispered, half-aloud, some famous fragments:\r\n\r\n"Tis not alone my inky cloak, good mother, Nor customary suits of solemn\r\nblack..."\r\n\r\nFor, in truth, the mirrored princely image, with hair dishevelled on the\r\nwhite brow, and the long tragic fall of black velvet from the shoulders,\r\nhad brought about (in his thought at least) some comparisons of his own\r\ntimes, so out of joint, with those of that other gentle prince and heir\r\nwhose widowed mother was minded to marry again.\r\n\r\n"But I have that within which passeth show; These but the trappings and\r\nthe suits of Woe."\r\n\r\nNot less like Hamlet did he feel and look as he sat gauntly at the\r\ndinner table with Fanny to partake of a meal throughout which neither\r\nspoke. Isabel had sent word "not to wait" for her, an injunction it was\r\nas well they obeyed, for she did not come at all. But with the renewal\r\nof sustenance furnished to his system, some relaxation must have\r\noccurred within the high-strung George. Dinner was not quite finished\r\nwhen, without warning, sleep hit him hard. His burning eyes could no\r\nlonger restrain the lids above them; his head sagged beyond control; and\r\nhe got to his feet, and went lurching upstairs, yawning with exhaustion.\r\nFrom the door of his room, which he closed mechanically, with his eyes\r\nshut, he went blindly to his bed, fell upon it soddenly, and slept--with\r\nhis face full upturned to the light.\r\n\r\nIt was after midnight when he woke, and the room was dark. He had not\r\ndreamed, but he woke with the sense that somebody or something had been\r\nwith him while he slept--somebody or something infinitely compassionate;\r\nsomebody or something infinitely protective, that would let him come to\r\nno harm and to no grief.\r\n\r\nHe got up, and pressed the light on. Pinned to the cover of his\r\ndressing-table was a square envelope, with the words, "For you, dear,"\r\nwritten in pencil upon it. But the message inside was in ink, a little\r\nsmudged here and there.\r\n\r\nI have been out to the mail-box, darling, with a letter I\'ve written to\r\nEugene, and he\'ll have it in the morning. It would be unfair not to let\r\nhim know at once, and my decision could not change if I waited. It would\r\nalways be the same. I think it, is a little better for me to write to\r\nyou, like this, instead of waiting till you wake up and then telling\r\nyou, because I\'m foolish and might cry again, and I took a vow once,\r\nlong ago, that you should never see me cry. Not that I\'ll feel like\r\ncrying when we talk things over tomorrow. I\'ll be "all right and fine"\r\n(as you say so often) by that time--don\'t fear. I think what makes me\r\nmost ready to cry now is the thought of the terrible suffering in your\r\npoor face, and the unhappy knowledge that it is I, your mother who put\r\nit there. It shall never come again! I love you better than anything\r\nand everything else on earth. God gave you to me--and oh! how thankful\r\nI have been every day of my life for that sacred gift--and nothing can\r\never come between me and God\'s gift. I cannot hurt you, and I cannot let\r\nyou stay hurt as you have been--not another instant after you wake up,\r\nmy darling boy! It is beyond my power. And Eugene was right--I know you\r\ncouldn\'t change about this. Your suffering shows how deep-seated the\r\nfeeling is within you. So I\'ve written him just about what I think you\r\nwould like me to--though I told him I would always be fond of him and\r\nalways his best friend, and I hoped his dearest friend. He\'ll understand\r\nabout not seeing him. He\'ll understand that, though I didn\'t say it\r\nin so many words. You mustn\'t trouble about that--he\'ll understand.\r\nGood-night, my darling, my beloved, my beloved! You mustn\'t be troubled.\r\nI think I shouldn\'t mind anything very much so long as I have you "all\r\nto myself"--as people say--to make up for your long years away from me\r\nat college. We\'ll talk of what\'s best to do in the morning, shan\'t we?\r\nAnd for all this pain you\'ll forgive your loving and devoted mother.\r\n\r\nIsabel.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXVII\r\n\r\n\r\nHaving finished some errands downtown, the next afternoon, George\r\nAmberson Minafer was walking up National Avenue on his homeward way when\r\nhe saw in the distance, coming toward him, upon the same side of the\r\nstreet, the figure of a young lady--a figure just under the middle\r\nheight, comely indeed, and to be mistaken for none other in the\r\nworld--even at two hundred yards. To his sharp discomfiture his heart\r\nimmediately forced upon him the consciousness of its acceleration; a\r\nsudden warmth about his neck made him aware that he had turned red,\r\nand then, departing, left him pale. For a panicky moment he thought of\r\nfacing about in actual flight; he had little doubt that Lucy would\r\nmeet him with no token of recognition, and all at once this probability\r\nstruck him as unendurable. And if she did not speak, was it the proper\r\npart of chivalry to lift his hat and take the cut bareheaded? Or should\r\nthe finer gentleman acquiesce in the lady\'s desire for no further\r\nacquaintance, and pass her with stony mien and eyes constrained forward?\r\nGeorge was a young man badly flustered.\r\n\r\nBut the girl approaching him was unaware of his trepidation, being\r\nperhaps somewhat preoccupied with her own. She saw only that he was\r\npale, and that his eyes were darkly circled. But here he was advantaged\r\nwith her, for the finest touch to his good looks was given by this\r\ntoning down; neither pallor nor dark circles detracting from them, but\r\nrather adding to them a melancholy favour of distinction. George had\r\nretained his mourning, a tribute completed down to the final details of\r\nblack gloves and a polished ebony cane (which he would have been pained\r\nto name otherwise than as a "walking-stick") and in the aura of this\r\nsombre elegance his straight figure and drawn face were not without a\r\ntristful and appealing dignity.\r\n\r\nIn everything outward he was cause enough for a girl\'s cheek to flush,\r\nher heart to beat faster, and her eyes to warm with the soft light that\r\ncame into Lucy\'s now, whether she would or no. If his spirit had been\r\nwhat his looks proclaimed it, she would have rejoiced to let the light\r\nglow forth which now shone in spite of her. For a long time, thinking of\r\nthat spirit of his, and what she felt it should be, she had a persistent\r\nsense: "It must be there!" but she had determined to believe this folly\r\nno longer. Nevertheless, when she met him at the Sharons\', she had been\r\nfar less calm than she seemed.\r\n\r\nPeople speaking casually of Lucy were apt to define her as "a little\r\nbeauty," a definition short of the mark. She was "a little beauty," but\r\nan independent, masterful, sell-reliant little American, of whom her\r\nfather\'s earlier gipsyings and her own sturdiness had made a woman ever\r\nsince she was fifteen. But though she was the mistress of her own ways\r\nand no slave to any lamp save that of her own conscience, she had a\r\nweakness: she had fallen in love with George Amberson Minafer at first\r\nsight, and no matter how she disciplined herself, she had never been\r\nable to climb out. The thing had happened to her; that was all. George\r\nhad looked just the way she had always wanted someone to look--the\r\nriskiest of all the moonshine ambushes wherein tricky romance snares\r\ncredulous young love. But what was fatal to Lucy was that this thing\r\nhaving happened to her, she could not change it. No matter what she\r\ndiscovered in George\'s nature she was unable to take away what she had\r\ngiven him; and though she could think differently about him, she could\r\nnot feel differently about him, for she was one of those too faithful\r\nvictims of glamour. When she managed to keep the picture of George away\r\nfrom her mind\'s eye, she did well enough; but when she let him become\r\nvisible, she could not choose but love what she disdained. She was a\r\nlittle angel who had fallen in love with high-handed Lucifer; quite an\r\nexperience, and not apt to be soon succeeded by any falling in love with\r\na tamer party--and the unhappy truth was that George did make better men\r\nseem tame. But though she was a victim, she was a heroic one, anything\r\nbut helpless.\r\n\r\nAs they drew nearer, George tried to prepare himself to meet her with\r\nsome remnants of aplomb. He decided that he would keep on looking\r\nstraight ahead, and lift his hand toward his hat at the very last moment\r\nwhen it would be possible for her to see him out of the corner of her\r\neye: then when she thought it over later, she would not be sure whether\r\nhe had saluted her or merely rubbed his forehead. And there was the\r\nadded benefit that any third person who might chance to look from\r\na window, or from a passing carriage, would not think that he was\r\nreceiving a snub, because he did not intend to lift his hat, but, timing\r\nthe gesture properly, would in fact actually rub his forehead. These\r\nwere the hasty plans which occupied his thoughts until he was within\r\nabout fifty feet of her--when he ceased to have either plans or\r\nthoughts, he had kept his eyes from looking full at her until then, and\r\nas he saw her, thus close at hand, and coming nearer, a regret that was\r\ndumfounding took possession of him. For the first time he had the sense\r\nof having lost something of overwhelming importance.\r\n\r\nLucy did not keep to the right, but came straight to meet him, smiling,\r\nand with her hand offered to him.\r\n\r\n"Why--you--" he stammered, as he took it. "Haven\'t you--" What he meant\r\nto say was, "Haven\'t you heard?"\r\n\r\n"Haven\'t I what?" she asked; and he saw that Eugene had not yet told\r\nher.\r\n\r\n"Nothing!" he gasped. "May I--may I turn and walk with you a little\r\nway?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, indeed!" she said cordially.\r\n\r\nHe would not have altered what had been done: he was satisfied with all\r\nthat--satisfied that it was right, and that his own course was right.\r\nBut he began to perceive a striking inaccuracy in some remarks he had\r\nmade to his mother. Now when he had put matters in such shape that even\r\nby the relinquishment of his "ideals of life" he could not have Lucy,\r\nknew that he could never have her, and knew that when Eugene told\r\nher the history of yesterday he could not have a glance or word even\r\nfriendly from her--now when he must in good truth "give up all idea\r\nof Lucy," he was amazed that he could have used such words as "no\r\nparticular sacrifice," and believed them when he said them! She had\r\nlooked never in his life so bewitchingly pretty as she did today; and as\r\nhe walked beside her he was sure that she was the most exquisite thing\r\nin the world.\r\n\r\n"Lucy," he said huskily, "I want to tell you something. Something that\r\nmatters."\r\n\r\n"I hope it\'s a lively something then," she said; and laughed. "Papa\'s\r\nbeen so glum to-day he\'s scarcely spoken to me. Your Uncle George\r\nAmberson came to see him an hour ago and they shut themselves up in the\r\nlibrary, and your uncle looked as glum as papa. I\'d be glad if you\'ll\r\ntell me a funny story, George."\r\n\r\n"Well, it may seem one to you," he said bitterly, "Just to begin with:\r\nwhen you went away you didn\'t let me know; not even a word--not a\r\nline--"\r\n\r\nHer manner persisted in being inconsequent. "Why, no," she said. "I just\r\ntrotted off for some visits."\r\n\r\n"Well, at least you might have--"\r\n\r\n"Why, no," she said again briskly. "Don\'t you remember, George? We\'d had\r\na grand quarrel, and didn\'t speak to each other all the way home from a\r\nlong, long drive! So, as we couldn\'t play together like good children,\r\nof course it was plain that we oughtn\'t to play at all."\r\n\r\n"Play!" he cried.\r\n\r\n"Yes. What I mean is that we\'d come to the point where it was time to\r\nquit playing--well, what we were playing."\r\n\r\n"At being lovers, you mean, don\'t you?"\r\n\r\n"Something like that," she said lightly. "For us two, playing at being\r\nlovers was just the same as playing at cross-purposes. I had all the\r\npurposes, and that gave you all the crossness: things weren\'t getting\r\nalong at all. It was absurd!"\r\n\r\n"Well, have it your own way," he said. "It needn\'t have been absurd."\r\n\r\n"No, it couldn\'t help but be!" she informed him cheerfully. "The way I\r\nam and the way you are, it couldn\'t ever be anything else. So what was\r\nthe use?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know," he sighed, and his sigh was abysmal. "But what I wanted\r\nto tell you is this: when you went away, you didn\'t let me know and\r\ndidn\'t care how or when I heard it, but I\'m not like that with you. This\r\ntime, I\'m going away. That\'s what I wanted to tell you. I\'m going away\r\ntomorrow night--indefinitely."\r\n\r\nShe nodded sunnily. "That\'s nice for you. I hope you\'ll have ever so\r\njolly a time, George."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t expect to have a particularly jolly time."\r\n\r\n"Well, then," she laughed, "if I were you I don\'t think I\'d go."\r\n\r\nIt seemed impossible to impress this distracting creature, to make her\r\nserious. "Lucy," he said desperately, "this is our last walk together."\r\n\r\n"Evidently!" she said, "if you\'re going away tomorrow night."\r\n\r\n"Lucy--this may be the last time I\'ll see you--ever--ever in my life."\r\n\r\nAt that she looked at him quickly, across her shoulder, but she smiled\r\nas brightly as before, and with the same cordial inconsequence: "Oh, I\r\ncan hardly think that!" she said. "And of course I\'d be awfully sorry to\r\nthink it. You\'re not moving away, are you, to live?"\r\n\r\n"No."\r\n\r\n"And even if you were, of course you\'d be coming back to visit your\r\nrelatives every now and then."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know when I\'m coming back. Mother and I are starting to-morrow\r\nnight for a trip around the world."\r\n\r\nAt this she did look thoughtful. "Your mother is going with you?"\r\n\r\n"Good heavens!" he groaned. "Lucy, doesn\'t it make any difference to you\r\nthat I am going?"\r\n\r\nAt this her cordial smile instantly appeared again. "Yes, of course,"\r\nshe said. "I\'m sure I\'ll miss you ever so much. Are you to be gone\r\nlong?"\r\n\r\nHe stared at her wanly. "I told you indefinitely," he said. "We\'ve made\r\nno plans--at all--for coming back."\r\n\r\n"That does sound like a long trip!" she exclaimed admiringly. "Do you\r\nplan to be travelling all the time, or will you stay in some one place\r\nthe greater part of it? I think it would be lovely to--"\r\n\r\n"Lucy!"\r\n\r\nHe halted; and she stopped with him. They had come to a corner at the\r\nedge of the "business section" of the city, and people were everywhere\r\nabout them, brushing against them, sometimes, in passing.\r\n\r\n"I can\'t stand this," George said, in a low voice. "I\'m just about ready\r\nto go in this drug-store here, and ask the clerk for something to keep\r\nme from dying in my tracks! It\'s quite a shock, you see, Lucy!"\r\n\r\n"What is?"\r\n\r\n"To find out certainly, at last, how deeply you\'ve cared for me! To see\r\nhow much difference this makes to you! By Jove, I have mattered to you!"\r\n\r\nHer cordial smile was tempered now with good-nature. "George!" She\r\nlaughed indulgently. "Surely you don\'t want me to do pathos on a\r\ndowntown corner!"\r\n\r\n"You wouldn\'t \'do pathos\' anywhere!"\r\n\r\n"Well--don\'t you think pathos is generally rather fooling?"\r\n\r\n"I can\'t stand this any longer," he said. "I can\'t! Good-bye, Lucy!" He\r\ntook her hand. "It\'s good-bye--I think it\'s good-bye for good, Lucy!"\r\n\r\n"Good-bye! I do hope you\'ll have the most splendid trip." She gave his\r\nhand a cordial little grip, then released it lightly. "Give my love to\r\nyour mother. Good-bye!"\r\n\r\nHe turned heavily away, and a moment later glanced back over his\r\nshoulder. She had not gone on, but stood watching him, that same casual,\r\ncordial smile on her face to the very last; and now, as he looked back,\r\nshe emphasized her friendly unconcern by waving her small hand to him\r\ncheerily, though perhaps with the slightest hint of preoccupation, as if\r\nshe had begun to think of the errand that brought her downtown.\r\n\r\nIn his mind, George had already explained her to his own poignant\r\ndissatisfaction--some blond pup, probably, whom she had met during that\r\n"perfectly gorgeous time!" And he strode savagely onward, not looking\r\nback again.\r\n\r\nBut Lucy remained where she was until he was out of sight. Then she went\r\nslowly into the drugstore which had struck George as a possible source\r\nof stimulant for himself.\r\n\r\n"Please let me have a few drops of aromatic spirits of ammonia in a\r\nglass of water," she said, with the utmost composure.\r\n\r\n"Yes, ma\'am!" said the impressionable clerk, who had been looking at her\r\nthrough the display window as she stood on the corner.\r\n\r\nBut a moment later, as he turned from the shelves of glass jars against\r\nthe wall, with the potion she had asked for in his hand, he uttered an\r\nexclamation: "For goshes\' sake, Miss!" And, describing this adventure to\r\nhis fellow-boarders, that evening, "Sagged pretty near to the counter,\r\nshe was," he said. "If I hadn\'t been a bright, quick, ready-for-anything\r\nyoung fella she\'d \'a\' flummixed plum! I was watchin\' her out the\r\nwindow--talkin\' to some young s\'iety fella, and she was all right\r\nthen. She was all right when she come in the store, too. Yes, sir; the\r\nprettiest girl that ever walked in our place and took one good look at\r\nme. I reckon it must be the truth what some you town wags say about my\r\nface!"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nAt that hour the heroine of the susceptible clerk\'s romance was engaged\r\nin brightening the rosy little coal fire under the white mantelpiece\r\nin her pretty white-and-blue boudoir. Four photographs all framed\r\nin decorous plain silver went to the anthracite\'s fierce\r\ndestruction--frames and all--and three packets of letters and notes in\r\na charming Florentine treasure-box of painted wood; nor was the box, any\r\nmore than the silver frames, spared this rousing finish. Thrown heartily\r\nupon live coal, the fine wood sparkled forth in stars, then burst into\r\nan alarming blaze which scorched the white mantelpiece, but Lucy stood\r\nand looked on without moving.\r\n\r\nIt was not Eugene who told her what had happened at Isabel\'s door.\r\nWhen she got home, she found Fanny Minafer waiting for her--a secret\r\nexcursion of Fanny\'s for the purpose, presumably, of "letting out"\r\nagain; because that was what she did. She told Lucy everything (except\r\nher own lamentable part in the production of the recent miseries) and\r\nconcluded with a tribute to George: "The worst of it is, he thinks he\'s\r\nbeen such a hero, and Isabel does, too, and that makes him more than\r\ntwice as awful. It\'s been the same all his life: everything he did was\r\nnoble and perfect. He had a domineering nature to begin with, and she\r\nlet it go on, and fostered it till it absolutely ruled her. I never saw\r\na plainer case of a person\'s fault making them pay for having it! She\r\ngoes about, overseeing the packing and praising George and pretending\r\nto be perfectly cheerful about what he\'s making her do and about the\r\ndreadful things he\'s done. She pretends he did such a fine thing--so\r\nmanly and protective--going to Mrs. Johnson. And so heroic--doing what\r\nhis \'principles\' made him--even though he knew what it would cost him\r\nwith you! And all the while it\'s almost killing her--what he said to\r\nyour father! She\'s always been lofty enough, so to speak, and had the\r\ngreatest idea of the Ambersons being superior to the rest of the world,\r\nand all that, but rudeness, or anything like a \'scene,\' or any bad\r\nmanners--they always just made her sick! But she could never see what\r\nGeorge\'s manners were--oh, it\'s been a terrible adulation!... It\'s going\r\nto be a task for me, living in that big house, all alone: you must come\r\nand see me--I mean after they\'ve gone, of course. I\'ll go crazy if I\r\ndon\'t see something of people. I\'m sure you\'ll come as often as you can.\r\nI know you too well to think you\'ll be sensitive about coming there, or\r\nbeing reminded of George. Thank heaven you\'re too well-balanced," Miss\r\nFanny concluded, with a profound fervour, "you\'re too well-balanced to\r\nlet anything affect you deeply about that--that monkey!"\r\n\r\nThe four photographs and the painted Florentine box went to their\r\ncremation within the same hour that Miss Fanny spoke; and a little later\r\nLucy called her father in, as he passed her door, and pointed to the\r\nblackened area on the underside of the mantelpiece, and to the burnt\r\nheap upon the coal, where some metallic shapes still retained outline.\r\nShe flung her arms about his neck in passionate sympathy, telling\r\nhim that she knew what had happened to him; and presently he began to\r\ncomfort her and managed an embarrassed laugh.\r\n\r\n"Well, well--" he said. "I was too old for such foolishness to be\r\ngetting into my head, anyhow."\r\n\r\n"No, no!" she sobbed. "And if you knew how I despise myself for--for\r\never having thought one instant about--oh, Miss Fanny called him the\r\nright name: that monkey! He is!"\r\n\r\n"There, I think I agree with you," Eugene said grimly, and in his eyes\r\nthere was a steady light of anger that was to last. "Yes, I think I\r\nagree with you about that!"\r\n\r\n"There\'s only one thing to do with such a person," she said vehemently.\r\n"That\'s to put him out of our thoughts forever--forever!"\r\n\r\nAnd yet, the next day, at six o\'clock, which was the hour, Fanny had\r\ntold her, when George and his mother were to leave upon their long\r\njourney, Lucy touched that scorched place on her mantel with her\r\nhand just as the little clock above it struck. Then, after this\r\nodd, unconscious gesture, she went to a window and stood between the\r\ncurtains, looking out into the cold November dusk; and in spite of every\r\nreasoning and reasonable power within her, a pain of loneliness struck\r\nthrough her heart. The dim street below her window, the dark houses\r\nacross the way, the vague air itself--all looked empty, and cold and\r\n(most of all) uninteresting. Something more sombre than November dusk\r\ntook the colour from them and gave them that air of desertion.\r\n\r\nThe light of her fire, flickering up behind her showed suddenly a flying\r\ngroup of tiny snowflakes nearing the window-pane; and for an instant she\r\nfelt the sensation of being dragged through a snows drift under a\r\nbroken cutter, with a boy\'s arms about her--an arrogant, handsome,\r\ntoo-conquering boy, who nevertheless did his best to get hurt himself,\r\nkeeping her from any possible harm.\r\n\r\nShe shook the picture out of her eyes indignantly, then came and sat\r\nbefore her fire, and looked long and long at the blackened mantelpiece.\r\nShe did not have the mantelpiece repainted--and, since she did not,\r\nmight as well have kept his photographs. One forgets what made the scar\r\nupon his hand but not what made the scar upon his wall.\r\n\r\nShe played no marche funebre upon her piano, even though Chopin\'s\r\nromantic lamentation was then at the top of nine-tenths of the\r\nmusic-racks in the country, American youth having recently discovered\r\nthe distinguished congeniality between itself and this deathless bit of\r\ndeathly gloom. She did not even play "Robin Adair"; she played "Bedelia"\r\nand all the new cake-walks, for she was her father\'s housekeeper,\r\nand rightly looked upon the office as being the same as that of his\r\nheart-keeper. Therefore it was her affair to keep both house and heart\r\nin what state of cheerfulness might be contrived. She made him "go out"\r\nmore than ever; made him take her to all the gayeties of that winter,\r\ndeclining to go herself unless he took her, and, though Eugene danced\r\nno more, and quoted Shakespeare to prove all lightfoot caperings beneath\r\nthe dignity of his age, she broke his resolution for him at the New\r\nYear\'s Eve "Assembly" and half coaxed, half dragged him forth upon the\r\nfloor, and made him dance the New Year in with her.\r\n\r\nNew faces appeared at the dances of the winter; new faces had\r\nbeen appearing everywhere, for that matter, and familiar ones were\r\ndisappearing, merged in the increasing crowd, or gone forever and missed\r\na little and not long; for the town was growing and changing as it never\r\nhad grown and changed before.\r\n\r\nIt was heaving up in the middle incredibly; it was spreading incredibly;\r\nand as it heaved and spread, it befouled itself and darkened its sky.\r\nIts boundary was mere shapelessness on the run; a raw, new house would\r\nappear on a country road; four or five others would presently be built\r\nat intervals between it and the outskirts of the town; the country road\r\nwould turn into an asphalt street with a brick-faced drugstore and a\r\nframe grocery at a corner; then bungalows and six-room cottages would\r\nswiftly speckle the open green spaces--and a farm had become a suburb\r\nwhich would immediately shoot out other suburbs into the country, on\r\none side, and, on the other, join itself solidly to the city. You drove\r\nbetween pleasant fields and woodland groves one spring day; and in the\r\nautumn, passing over the same ground, you were warned off the tracks by\r\nan interurban trolley-car\'s gonging, and beheld, beyond cement sidewalks\r\njust dry, new house-owners busy "moving in." Gasoline and electricity\r\nwere performing the miracles Eugene had predicted.\r\n\r\nBut the great change was in the citizenry itself. What was left of\r\nthe patriotic old-stock generation that had fought the Civil War, and\r\nsubsequently controlled politics, had become venerable and was little\r\nheeded. The descendants of the pioneers and early settlers were merging\r\ninto the new crowd, becoming part of it, little to be distinguished from\r\nit. What happened to Boston and to Broadway happened in degree to the\r\nMidland city; the old stock became less and less typical, and of the\r\ngrown people who called the place home, less than a third had been born\r\nin it. There was a German quarter; there was a Jewish quarter; there was\r\na negro quarter--square miles of it--called "Bucktown"; there were many\r\nIrish neighbourhoods; and there were large settlements of Italians,\r\nand of Hungarians, and of Rumanians, and of Serbians and other Balkan\r\npeoples. But not the emigrants, themselves, were the almost dominant\r\ntype on the streets downtown. That type was the emigrant\'s prosperous\r\noffspring: descendant of the emigrations of the Seventies and Eighties\r\nand Nineties, those great folk-journeyings in search not so directly\r\nof freedom and democracy as of more money for the same labour. A new\r\nMidlander--in fact, a new American--was beginning dimly to emerge.\r\n\r\nA new spirit of citizenship had already sharply defined itself. It was\r\nidealistic, and its ideals were expressed in the new kind of young men\r\nin business downtown. They were optimists--optimists to the point of\r\nbelligerence--their motto being "Boost! Don\'t Knock!" And they were\r\nhustlers, believing in hustling and in honesty because both paid. They\r\nloved their city and worked for it with a plutonic energy which was\r\nalways ardently vocal. They were viciously governed, but they sometimes\r\nwent so far to struggle for better government on account of the helpful\r\neffect of good government on the price of real estate and "betterment"\r\ngenerally; the politicians could not go too far with them, and knew\r\nit. The idealists planned and strove and shouted that their city should\r\nbecome a better, better, and better city--and what they meant, when they\r\nused the word "better," was "more prosperous," and the core of their\r\nidealism was this: "The more prosperous my beloved city, the more\r\nprosperous beloved I!" They had one supreme theory: that the perfect\r\nbeauty and happiness of cities and of human life was to be brought about\r\nby more factories; they had a mania for factories; there was nothing\r\nthey would not do to cajole a factory away from another city; and they\r\nwere never more piteously embittered than when another city cajoled one\r\naway from them.\r\n\r\nWhat they meant by Prosperity was credit at the bank; but in exchange\r\nfor this credit they got nothing that was not dirty, and, therefore,\r\nto a sane mind, valueless; since whatever was cleaned was dirty again\r\nbefore the cleaning was half done. For, as the town grew, it grew\r\ndirty with an incredible completeness. The idealists put up magnificent\r\nbusiness buildings and boasted of them, but the buildings were begrimed\r\nbefore they were finished. They boasted of their libraries, of their\r\nmonuments and statues; and poured soot on them. They boasted of their\r\nschools, but the schools were dirty, like the children within them. This\r\nwas not the fault of the children or their mothers. It was the fault of\r\nthe idealists, who said: "The more dirt, the more prosperity." They\r\ndrew patriotic, optimistic breaths of the flying powdered filth of\r\nthe streets, and took the foul and heavy smoke with gusto into the\r\nprofundities of their lungs. "Boost! Don\'t knock!" they said. And\r\nevery year or so they boomed a great Clean-up Week, when everybody was\r\nsupposed to get rid of the tin cans in his backyard.\r\n\r\nThey were happiest when the tearing down and building up were most\r\nriotous, and when new factory districts were thundering into life. In\r\ntruth, the city came to be like the body of a great dirty man, skinned,\r\nto show his busy works, yet wearing a few barbaric ornaments; and such\r\na figure carved, coloured, and discoloured, and set up in the\r\nmarket-place, would have done well enough as the god of the new people.\r\nSuch a god they had indeed made in their own image, as all peoples make\r\nthe god they truly serve; though of course certain of the idealists\r\nwent to church on Sunday, and there knelt to Another, considered to\r\nbe impractical in business. But while the Growing went on, this god\r\nof their market-place was their true god, their familiar and\r\nspirit-control. They did not know that they were his helplessly obedient\r\nslaves, nor could they ever hope to realize their serfdom (as the first\r\nstep toward becoming free men) until they should make the strange and\r\nhard discovery that matter should serve man\'s spirit.\r\n\r\n"Prosperity" meant good credit at the bank, black lungs, and housewives\'\r\nPurgatory. The women fought the dirt all they could; but if they let the\r\nair into their houses they let in the dirt. It shortened their lives,\r\nand kept them from the happiness of ever seeing anything white. And\r\nthus, as the city grew, the time came when Lucy, after a hard struggle,\r\nhad to give up her blue-and-white curtains and her white walls. Indoors,\r\nshe put everything into dull gray and brown, and outside had the little\r\nhouse painted the dark green nearest to black. Then she knew, of course,\r\nthat everything was as dirty as ever, but was a little less distressed\r\nbecause it no longer looked so dirty as it was.\r\n\r\nThese were bad times for Amberson Addition. This quarter, already old,\r\nlay within a mile of the centre of the town, but business moved in other\r\ndirections; and the Addition\'s share of Prosperity was only the smoke\r\nand dirt, with the bank credit left out. The owners of the original\r\nbig houses sold them, or rented them to boarding-house keepers, and the\r\ntenants of the multitude of small houses moved "farther out" (where the\r\nsmoke was thinner) or into apartment houses, which were built by dozens\r\nnow. Cheaper tenants took their places, and the rents were lower and\r\nlower, and the houses shabbier and shabbier--for all these shabby\r\nhouses, burning soft coal, did their best to help in the destruction of\r\ntheir own value. They helped to make the quarter so dingy and the air so\r\nfoul to breathe that no one would live there who had money enough to get\r\n"farther out" where there were glimpses of ungrayed sky and breaths of\r\ncleaner winds. And with the coming of the new speed, "farther out" was\r\nnow as close to business as the Addition had been in the days of its\r\nprosperity. Distances had ceased to matter.\r\n\r\nThe five new houses, built so closely where had been the fine lawn of\r\nthe Amberson Mansion, did not look new. When they were a year old they\r\nlooked as old as they would ever look; and two of them were vacant,\r\nhaving never been rented, for the Major\'s mistake about apartment houses\r\nhad been a disastrous one. "He guessed wrong," George Amberson said..\r\n"He guessed wrong at just the wrong time! Housekeeping in a house is\r\nharder than in an apartment; and where the smoke and dirt are as thick\r\nas they are in the Addition, women can\'t stand it. People were crazy for\r\napartments--too bad he couldn\'t have seen it in time. Poor man! he\r\ndigs away at his ledgers by his old gas drop-light lamp almost every\r\nnight--he still refuses to let the Mansion be torn up for wiring, you\r\nknow. But he had one painful satisfaction this spring: he got his taxes\r\nlowered!"\r\n\r\nAmberson laughed ruefully, and Fanny Minafer asked how the Major could\r\nhave managed such an economy. They were sitting upon the veranda at\r\nIsabel\'s one evening during the third summer of the absence of their\r\nnephew and his mother; and the conversation had turned toward Amberson\r\nfinances.\r\n\r\n"I said it was a \'painful satisfaction,\' Fanny," he explained. "The\r\nproperty has gone down in value, and they assessed it lower than they\r\ndid fifteen years ago."\r\n\r\n"But farther out--"\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes, \'farther out!\' Prices are magnificent \'farther out,\' and\r\nfarther in, too! We just happen to be the wrong spot, that\'s all. Not\r\nthat I don\'t think something could be done if father would let me have\r\na hand; but he won\'t. He can\'t, I suppose I ought to say. He\'s \'always\r\ndone his own figuring,\' he says; and it\'s his lifelong habit to keep his\r\naffairs: and even his books, to himself, and just hand us out the money.\r\nHeaven knows he\'s done enough of that!"\r\n\r\nHe sighed; and both were silent, looking out at the long flares of the\r\nconstantly passing automobile headlights, shifting in vast geometric\r\ndemonstrations against the darkness. Now and then a bicycle wound its\r\nnervous way among these portents, or, at long intervals, a surrey or\r\nbuggy plodded forlornly by.\r\n\r\n"There seem to be so many ways of making money nowadays," Fanny said\r\nthoughtfully. "Every day I hear of a new fortune some person has got\r\nhold of, one way or another--nearly always it\'s somebody you never heard\r\nof. It doesn\'t seem all to be in just making motor cars; I hear there\'s\r\na great deal in manufacturing these things that motor cars use--new\r\ninventions particularly. I met dear old Frank Bronson the other day, and\r\nhe told me--"\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes, even dear old Frank\'s got the fever," Amberson laughed. "He\'s\r\nas wild as any of them. He told me about this invention he\'s gone into,\r\ntoo. \'Millions in it!\' Some new electric headlight better than anything\r\nyet--\'every car in America can\'t help but have \'em,\' and all that. He\'s\r\nputting half he\'s laid by into it, and the fact is, he almost talked me\r\ninto getting father to \'finance me\' enough for me to go into it. Poor\r\nfather! he\'s financed me before! I suppose he would again if I had the\r\nheart to ask him; and this seems to be a good thing, though probably\r\nold Frank is a little too sanguine. At any rate, I\'ve been thinking it\r\nover."\r\n\r\n"So have I," Fanny admitted. "He seemed to be certain it would pay\r\ntwenty-five per cent. the first year, and enormously more after that;\r\nand I\'m only getting four on my little principal. People are making such\r\nenormous fortunes out of everything to do with motor cars, it does seem\r\nas if--" She paused. "Well, I told him I\'d think it over seriously."\r\n\r\n"We may turn out to be partners and millionaires then," Amberson\r\nlaughed. "I thought I\'d ask Eugene\'s advice."\r\n\r\n"I wish you would," said Fanny. "He probably knows exactly how much\r\nprofit there would be in this."\r\n\r\nEugene\'s advice was to "go slow": he thought electric lights for\r\nautomobiles were "coming--someday but probably not until certain\r\ndifficulties could be overcome." Altogether, he was discouraging, but\r\nby this time his two friends "had the fever" as thoroughly as old Frank\r\nBronson himself had it; for they had been with Bronson to see the light\r\nworking beautifully in a machine shop. They were already enthusiastic,\r\nand after asking Eugene\'s opinion they argued with him, telling him how\r\nthey had seen with their own eyes that the difficulties he mentioned had\r\nbeen overcome. "Perfectly!" Fanny cried. "And if it worked in the shop\r\nit\'s bound to work any place else, isn\'t it?"\r\n\r\nHe would not agree that it was "bound to"--yet, being pressed, was\r\ndriven to admit that "it might," and, retiring from what was developing\r\ninto an oratorical contest, repeated a warning about not "putting too\r\nmuch into it."\r\n\r\nGeorge Amberson also laid stress on this caution later, though the Major\r\nhad "financed him" again, and he was "going in." "You must be careful\r\nto leave yourself a \'margin of safety,\' Fanny," he said. "I\'m confident\r\nthat is a pretty conservative investment of its kind, and all the\r\nchances are with us, but you must be careful to leave yourself enough to\r\nfall back on, in case anything should go wrong."\r\n\r\nFanny deceived him. In the impossible event of "anything going wrong"\r\nshe would have enough left to "live on," she declared, and laughed\r\nexcitedly, for she was having the best time that had come to her since\r\nWilbur\'s death. Like so many women for whom money has always been\r\nprovided without their understanding how, she was prepared to be a\r\nthorough and irresponsible plunger.\r\n\r\nAmberson, in his wearier way, shared her excitement, and in the winter,\r\nwhen the exploiting company had been formed, and he brought Fanny, her\r\nimportantly engraved shares of stock, he reverted to his prediction of\r\npossibilities, made when they first spoke of the new light.\r\n\r\n"We seem to be partners, all right," he laughed. "Now let\'s go ahead and\r\nbe millionaires before Isabel and young George come home."\r\n\r\n"When they come home!" she echoed sorrowfully--and it was a phrase which\r\nfound an evasive echo in Isabel\'s letters. In these letters Isabel was\r\nalways planning pleasant things that she and Fanny and the Major and\r\nGeorge and "brother George" would do--when she and her son came home.\r\n"They\'ll find things pretty changed, I\'m afraid," Fanny said. "If they\r\never do come home!"\r\n\r\nAmberson went over, the next summer, and joined his sister and nephew in\r\nParis, where they were living. "Isabel does want to come home," he told\r\nFanny gravely, on the day of his return, in October. "She\'s wanted\r\nto for a long while--and she ought to come while she can stand the\r\njourney--" And he amplified this statement, leaving Fanny looking\r\nstartled and solemn when Lucy came by to drive him out to dinner at the\r\nnew house Eugene had just completed.\r\n\r\nThis was no white-and-blue cottage, but a great Georgian picture in\r\nbrick, five miles north of Amberson Addition, with four acres of its\r\nown hedged land between it and its next neighbour; and Amberson laughed\r\nwistfully as they turned in between the stone and brick gate pillars,\r\nand rolled up the crushed stone driveway. "I wonder, Lucy, if history\'s\r\ngoing on forever repeating itself," he said. "I wonder if this town\'s\r\ngoing on building up things and rolling over them, as poor father once\r\nsaid it was rolling over his poor old heart. It looks like it: here\'s\r\nthe Amberson Mansion again, only it\'s Georgian instead of nondescript\r\nRomanesque; but it\'s just the same Amberson Mansion that my father built\r\nlong before you were born. The only difference is that it\'s your father\r\nwho\'s built this one now. It\'s all the same, in the long run."\r\n\r\nLucy did not quite understand, but she laughed as a friend should, and,\r\ntaking his arm, showed him through vast rooms where ivory-panelled walls\r\nand trim window hangings were reflected dimly in dark, rugless floors,\r\nand the sparse furniture showed that Lucy had been "collecting" with a\r\nlong purse. "By Jove!" he said. "You have been going it! Fanny tells me\r\nyou had a great \'house-warming\' dance, and you keep right on being the\r\nbelle of the ball, not any softer-hearted than you used to be. Fred\r\nKinney\'s father says you\'ve refused Fred so often that he got engaged to\r\nJanie Sharon just to prove that someone would have him in spite of his\r\nhair. Well, the material world do move, and you\'ve got the new kind\r\nof house it moves into nowadays--if it has the new price! And even the\r\ngrand old expanses of plate glass we used to be so proud of at the other\r\nAmberson Mansion--they\'ve gone, too, with the crowded heavy gold and red\r\nstuff. Curious! We\'ve still got the plate glass windows, though all we\r\ncan see out of \'em is the smoke and the old Johnson house, which is a\r\ncounter-jumper\'s boardinghouse now, while you\'ve got a view, and you cut\r\nit all up into little panes. Well, you\'re pretty refreshingly out of the\r\nsmoke up here."\r\n\r\n"Yes, for a while," Lucy laughed. "Until it comes and we have to move\r\nout farther."\r\n\r\n"No, you\'ll stay here," he assured her. "It will be somebody else who\'ll\r\nmove out farther."\r\n\r\nHe continued to talk of the house after Eugene arrived, and gave them no\r\naccount of his journey until they had retired from the dinner table\r\nto Eugene\'s library, a gray and shadowy room, where their coffee\r\nwas brought. Then, equipped with a cigar, which seemed to occupy his\r\nattention, Amberson spoke in a casual tone of his sister and her son.\r\n\r\n"I found Isabel as well as usual," he said, "only I\'m afraid \'as usual\'\r\nisn\'t particularly well. Sydney and Amelia had been up to Paris in the\r\nspring, but she hadn\'t seen them. Somebody told her they were there, it\r\nseems. They\'d left Florence and were living in Rome; Amelia\'s become a\r\nCatholic and is said to give great sums to charity and to go about\r\nwith the gentry in consequence, but Sydney\'s ailing and lives in a\r\nwheel-chair most of the time. It struck me Isabel ought to be doing the\r\nsame thing."\r\n\r\nHe paused, bestowing minute care upon the removal of the little band\r\nfrom his cigar; and as he seemed to have concluded his narrative, Eugene\r\nspoke out of the shadow beyond a heavily shaded lamp: "What do you mean\r\nby that?" he asked quietly.\r\n\r\n"Oh, she\'s cheerful enough," said Amberson, still not looking at either\r\nhis young hostess or her father. "At least," he added, "she manages to\r\nseem so. I\'m afraid she hasn\'t been really well for several years. She\r\nisn\'t stout you know--she hasn\'t changed in looks much--and she seems\r\nrather alarmingly short of breath for a slender person. Father\'s been\r\nthat way for years, of course; but never nearly so much as Isabel is\r\nnow. Of course she makes nothing of it, but it seemed rather serious to\r\nme when I noticed she had to stop and rest twice to get up the one short\r\nflight of stairs in their two-floor apartment. I told her I thought she\r\nought to make George let her come home."\r\n\r\n"Let her?" Eugene repeated, in a low voice. "Does she want to?"\r\n\r\n"She doesn\'t urge it. George seems to like the life there-in his grand,\r\ngloomy, and peculiar way; and of course she\'ll never change about being\r\nproud of him and all that--he\'s quite a swell. But in spite of anything\r\nshe said, rather than because, I know she does indeed want to come.\r\nShe\'d like to be with father, of course; and I think she\'s--well, she\r\nintimated one day that she feared it might even happen that she wouldn\'t\r\nget to see him again. At the time I thought she referred to his age and\r\nfeebleness, but on the boat, coming home, I remembered the little look\r\nof wistfulness, yet of resignation, with which she said it, and it\r\nstruck me all at once that I\'d been mistaken: I saw she was really\r\nthinking of her own state of health."\r\n\r\n"I see," Eugene said, his voice even lower than it had been before. "And\r\nyou say he won\'t \'let\' her come home?"\r\n\r\nAmberson laughed, but still continued to be interested in his cigar.\r\n"Oh, I don\'t think he uses force! He\'s very gentle with her. I doubt\r\nif the subject is mentioned between them, and yet--and yet, knowing my\r\ninteresting nephew as you do, wouldn\'t you think that was about the way\r\nto put it?"\r\n\r\n"Knowing him as I do--yes," said Eugene slowly. "Yes, I should think that\r\nwas about the way to put it."\r\n\r\nA murmur out of the shadows beyond him--a faint sound, musical and\r\nfeminine, yet expressive of a notable intensity--seemed to indicate that\r\nLucy was of the same opinion.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXIX\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"Let her" was correct; but the time came--and it came in the spring of\r\nthe next year when it was no longer a question of George\'s letting his\r\nmother come home. He had to bring her, and to bring her quickly if she\r\nwas to see her father again; and Amberson had been right: her danger of\r\nnever seeing him again lay not in the Major\'s feebleness of heart but in\r\nher own. As it was, George telegraphed his uncle to have a wheeled chair\r\nat the station, for the journey had been disastrous, and to this hybrid\r\nvehicle, placed close to the platform, her son carried her in his arms\r\nwhen she arrived. She was unable to speak, but patted her brother\'s and\r\nFanny\'s hands and looked "very sweet," Fanny found the desperate courage\r\nto tell her. She was lifted from the chair into a carriage, and seemed\r\na little stronger as they drove home; for once she took her hand from\r\nGeorge\'s, and waved it feebly toward the carriage window.\r\n\r\n"Changed," she whispered. "So changed."\r\n\r\n"You mean the town," Amberson said. "You mean the old place is changed,\r\ndon\'t you, dear?"\r\n\r\nShe smiled and moved her lips: "Yes."\r\n\r\n"It\'ll change to a happier place, old dear," he said, "now that you\'re\r\nback in it, and going to get well again."\r\n\r\nBut she only looked at him wistfully, her eyes a little frightened.\r\n\r\nWhen the carriage stopped, her son carried her into the house, and up\r\nthe stairs to her own room, where a nurse was waiting; and he came out\r\na moment later, as the doctor went in. At the end of the hall a stricken\r\ngroup was clustered: Amberson, and Fanny, and the Major. George, deathly\r\npale and speechless, took his grandfather\'s hand, but the old gentleman\r\ndid not seem to notice his action.\r\n\r\n"When are they going to let me see my daughter?" he asked querulously.\r\n"They told me to keep out of the way while they carried her in, because\r\nit might upset her. I wish they\'d let me go in and speak to my daughter.\r\nI think she wants to see me."\r\n\r\nHe was right--presently the doctor came out and beckoned to him; and the\r\nMajor shuffled forward, leaning on a shaking cane; his figure, after all\r\nits Years of proud soldierliness, had grown stooping at last, and his\r\nuntrimmed white hair straggled over the back of his collar. He looked\r\nold--old and divested of the world--as he crept toward his daughter\'s\r\nroom. Her voice was stronger, for the waiting group heard a low cry of\r\ntenderness and welcome as the old man reached the open doorway. Then the\r\ndoor was closed.\r\n\r\nFanny touched her nephew\'s arm. "George, you must need something to\r\neat--I know she\'d want you to. I\'ve had things ready: I knew she\'d want\r\nme to. You\'d better go down to the dining room: there\'s plenty on the\r\ntable, waiting for you. She\'d want you to eat something."\r\n\r\nHe turned a ghastly face to her, it was so panic-stricken. "I don\'t\r\nwant anything to eat!" he said savagely. And he began to pace the floor,\r\ntaking care not to go near Isabel\'s door, and that his footsteps were\r\nmuffled by the long, thick hall rug. After a while he went to where\r\nAmberson, with folded arms and bowed head, had seated himself near the\r\nfront window. "Uncle George," he said hoarsely. "I didn\'t--"\r\n\r\n"Well?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, my God, I didn\'t think this thing the matter with her could ever be\r\nserious! I--" He gasped. "When that doctor I had meet us at the boat--"\r\nHe could not go on.\r\n\r\nAmberson only nodded his head, and did not otherwise change his\r\nattitude.\r\n\r\nIsabel lived through the night. At eleven O\'clock Fanny came timidly to\r\nGeorge in his room. "Eugene is here," she whispered. "He\'s downstairs.\r\nHe wants--" She gulped. "He wants to know if he can\'t see her. I didn\'t\r\nknow what to say. I said I\'d see. I didn\'t know--the doctor said--"\r\n\r\n"The doctor said we \'must keep her peaceful,\'" George said sharply. "Do\r\nyou think that man\'s coming would be very soothing? My God! if it hadn\'t\r\nbeen for him this mightn\'t have happened: we could have gone on living\r\nhere quietly, and--why, it would be like taking a stranger into her\r\nroom! She hasn\'t even spoken of him more than twice in all the time\r\nwe\'ve been away. Doesn\'t he know how sick she is? You tell him the\r\ndoctor said she had to be quiet and peaceful. That\'s what he did say,\r\nisn\'t it?"\r\n\r\nFanny acquiesced tearfully. "I\'ll tell him. I\'ll tell him the doctor\r\nsaid she was to be kept very quiet. I--I didn\'t know--" And she pottered\r\nout.\r\n\r\nAn hour later the nurse appeared in George\'s doorway; she came\r\nnoiselessly, and his back was toward her; but he jumped as if he had\r\nbeen shot, and his jaw fell, he so feared what she was going to say.\r\n\r\n"She wants to see you."\r\n\r\nThe terrified mouth shut with a click; and he nodded and followed her;\r\nbut she remained outside his mother\'s room while he went in.\r\n\r\nIsabel\'s eyes were closed, and she did not open them or move her head,\r\nbut she smiled and edged her hand toward him as he sat on a stool beside\r\nthe bed. He took that slender, cold hand, and put it to his cheek.\r\n\r\n"Darling, did you--get something to eat?" She could only whisper, slowly\r\nand with difficulty. It was as if Isabel herself were far away, and only\r\nable to signal what she wanted to say.\r\n\r\n"Yes, mother."\r\n\r\n"All you--needed?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, mother."\r\n\r\nShe did not speak again for a time; then, "Are you sure you\r\ndidn\'t--didn\'t catch cold coming home?"\r\n\r\n"I\'m all right, mother."\r\n\r\n"That\'s good. It\'s sweet--it\'s sweet--"\r\n\r\n"What is, mother darling?"\r\n\r\n"To feel--my hand on your cheek. I--I can feel it."\r\n\r\nBut this frightened him horribly--that she seemed so glad she could feel\r\nit, like a child proud of some miraculous seeming thing accomplished. It\r\nfrightened him so that he could not speak, and he feared that she would\r\nknow how he trembled; but she was unaware, and again was silent. Finally\r\nshe spoke again:\r\n\r\n"I wonder if--if Eugene and Lucy know that we\'ve come--home."\r\n\r\n"I\'m sure they do."\r\n\r\n"Has he--asked about me?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, he was here."\r\n\r\n"Has he--gone?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, mother."\r\n\r\nShe sighed faintly. "I\'d like--"\r\n\r\n"What, mother?"\r\n\r\n"I\'d like to have--seen him." It was just audible, this little regretful\r\nmurmur. Several minutes passed before there was another. "Just--just\r\nonce," she whispered, and then was still.\r\n\r\nShe seemed to have fallen asleep, and George moved to go, but a faint\r\npressure upon his fingers detained him, and he remained, with her hand\r\nstill pressed against his cheek. After a while he made sure she was\r\nasleep, and moved again, to let the nurse come in, and this time there\r\nwas no pressure of the fingers to keep him. She was not asleep, but\r\nthinking that if he went he might get some rest, and be better prepared\r\nfor what she knew was coming, she commanded those longing fingers of\r\nhers--and let him go.\r\n\r\nHe found the doctor standing with the nurse in the hall; and, telling\r\nthem that his mother was drowsing now, George went back to his own room,\r\nwhere he was startled to find his grandfather lying on the bed, and his\r\nuncle leaning against the wall. They had gone home two hours before, and\r\nhe did not know they had returned.\r\n\r\n"The doctor thought we\'d better come over," Amberson said, then was\r\nsilent, and George, shaking violently, sat down on the edge of the bed.\r\nHis shaking continued, and from time to time he wiped heavy sweat from\r\nhis forehead.\r\n\r\nThe hours passed, and sometimes the old man upon the bed would snore a\r\nlittle, stop suddenly, and move as if to rise, but George Amberson would\r\nset a hand upon his shoulder, and murmur a reassuring word or two. Now\r\nand then, either uncle or nephew would tiptoe into the hall and look\r\ntoward Isabel\'s room, then come tiptoeing back, the other watching him\r\nhaggardly.\r\n\r\nOnce George gasped defiantly: "That doctor in New York said she might\r\nget better! Don\'t you know he did? Don\'t you know he said she might?"\r\n\r\nAmberson made no answer.\r\n\r\nDawn had been murking through the smoky windows, growing stronger for\r\nhalf an hour, when both men started violently at a sound in the hall;\r\nand the Major sat up on the bed, unchecked. It was the voice of the\r\nnurse speaking to Fanny Minafer, and the next moment, Fanny appeared in\r\nthe doorway, making contorted efforts to speak.\r\n\r\nAmberson said weakly: "Does she want us--to come in?"\r\n\r\nBut Fanny found her voice, and uttered a long, loud cry. She threw her\r\narms about George, and sobbed in an agony of loss and compassion:\r\n\r\n"She loved you!" she wailed. "She loved you! She loved you! Oh, how she\r\ndid love you!"\r\n\r\nIsabel had just left them.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXX\r\n\r\n\r\nMajor Amberson remained dry-eyed through the time that followed: he\r\nknew that this separation from his daughter would be short, that the\r\nseparation which had preceded it was the long one. He worked at his\r\nledgers no more under his old gas drop-light, but would sit all evening\r\nstaring into the fire, in his bedroom, and not speaking unless someone\r\nasked him a question. He seemed almost unaware of what went on around\r\nhim, and those who were with him thought him dazed by Isabel\'s death,\r\nguessing that he was lost in reminiscences and vague dreams. "Probably\r\nhis mind is full of pictures of his youth, or the Civil War, and the\r\ndays when he and mother were young married people and all of us children\r\nwere jolly little things--and the city was a small town with one cobbled\r\nstreet and the others just dirt roads with board sidewalks." This was\r\nGeorge Amberson\'s conjecture, and the others agreed; but they were\r\nmistaken. The Major was engaged in the profoundest thinking of his\r\nlife. No business plans which had ever absorbed him could compare in\r\nmomentousness with the plans that absorbed him now, for he had to plan\r\nhow to enter the unknown country where he was not even sure of being\r\nrecognized as an Amberson--not sure of anything, except that Isabel\r\nwould help him if she could. His absorption produced the outward effect\r\nof reverie, but of course it was not.
