The Major was occupied with the\r\nfirst really important matter that had taken his attention since he came\r\nhome invalided, after the Gettysburg campaign, and went into business;\r\nand he realized that everything which had worried him or delighted\r\nhim during this lifetime between then and to-day--all his buying and\r\nbuilding and trading and banking--that it all was trifling and waste\r\nbeside what concerned him now.\r\n\r\nHe seldom went out of his room, and often left untouched the meals they\r\nbrought to him there; and this neglect caused them to shake their heads\r\nmournfully, again mistaking for dazedness the profound concentration of\r\nhis mind. Meanwhile, the life of the little bereft group still forlornly\r\ncentering upon him began to pick up again, as life will, and to emerge\r\nfrom its own period of dazedness. It was not Isabel\'s father but her son\r\nwho was really dazed.\r\n\r\nA month after her death he walked abruptly into Fanny\'s room, one night,\r\nand found her at her desk, eagerly adding columns of figures with which\r\nshe had covered several sheets of paper. This mathematical computation\r\nwas concerned with her future income to be produced by the electric\r\nheadlight, now just placed on the general market; but Fanny was ashamed\r\nto be discovered doing anything except mourning, and hastily pushed\r\nthe sheets aside, even as she looked over her shoulder to greet her\r\nhollow-eyed visitor.\r\n\r\n"George! You startled me."\r\n\r\n"I beg your pardon for not knocking," he said huskily. "I didn\'t think."\r\n\r\nShe turned in her chair and looked at him solicitously. "Sit down,\r\nGeorge, won\'t you?"\r\n\r\n"No. I just wanted--"\r\n\r\n"I could hear you walking up and down in your room," said Fanny. "You\r\nwere doing it ever since dinner, and it seems to me you\'re at it almost\r\nevery evening. I don\'t believe it\'s good for you--and I know it would\r\nworry your mother terribly if she--" Fanny hesitated.\r\n\r\n"See here," George said, breathing fast, "I want to tell you once more\r\nthat what I did was right. How could I have done anything else but what\r\nI did do?"\r\n\r\n"About what, George?"\r\n\r\n"About everything!" he exclaimed; and he became vehement. "I did the\r\nright thing, I tell you! In heaven\'s name, I\'d like to know what\r\nelse there was for anybody in my position to do! It would have been a\r\ndreadful thing for me to just let matters go on and not interfere--it\r\nwould have been terrible! What else on earth was there for me to do? I\r\nhad to stop that talk, didn\'t I? Could a son do less than I did? Didn\'t\r\nit cost me something to do it? Lucy and I\'d had a quarrel, but that\r\nwould have come round in time--and it meant the end forever when I\r\nturned her father back from our door. I knew what it meant, yet I went\r\nahead and did it because knew it had to be done if the talk was to be\r\nstopped. I took mother away for the same reason. I knew that would help\r\nto stop it. And she was happy over there--she was perfectly happy. I\r\ntell you, I think she had a happy life, and that\'s my only consolation.\r\nShe didn\'t live to be old; she was still beautiful and young looking,\r\nand I feel she\'d rather have gone before she got old. She\'d had a good\r\nhusband, and all the comfort and luxury that anybody could have--and how\r\ncould it be called anything but a happy life? She was always cheerful,\r\nand when I think of her I can always see her laughing--I can always hear\r\nthat pretty laugh of hers. When I can keep my mind off of the trip home,\r\nand that last night, I always think of her gay and laughing. So how on\r\nearth could she have had anything but a happy life? People that aren\'t\r\nhappy don\'t look cheerful all the time, do they? They look unhappy\r\nif they are unhappy; that\'s how they look! See here"--he faced her\r\nchallengingly--"do you deny that I did the right thing?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, I don\'t pretend to judge," Fanny said soothingly, for his voice and\r\ngesture both partook of wildness. "I know you think you did, George."\r\n\r\n"Think I did!" he echoed violently. "My God in heaven!" And he began to\r\nwalk up and down the floor. "What else was there to do? What, choice did\r\nI have? Was there any other way of stopping the talk?" He stopped, close\r\nin front of her, gesticulating, his voice harsh and loud: "Don\'t you\r\nhear me? I\'m asking you: Was there any other way on earth of protecting\r\nher from the talk?"\r\n\r\nMiss Fanny looked away. "It died down before long, I think," she said\r\nnervously.\r\n\r\n"That shows I was right, doesn\'t it?" he cried. "If I hadn\'t acted as\r\nI did, that slanderous old Johnson woman would have kept on with her\r\nslanders--she\'d still be--"\r\n\r\n"No," Fanny interrupted. "She\'s dead. She dropped dead with apoplexy one\r\nday about six weeks after you left. I didn\'t mention it in my letters\r\nbecause I didn\'t want--I thought--"\r\n\r\n"Well, the other people would have kept on, then. They\'d have--"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know," said Fanny, still averting her troubled eyes. "Things\r\nare so changed here, George. The other people you speak of--one hardly\r\nknows what\'s become of them. Of course not a great many were doing the\r\ntalking, and they--well, some of them are dead, and some might as well\r\nbe--you never see them any more--and the rest, whoever they were, are\r\nprobably so mixed in with the crowds of new people that seem never even\r\nto have heard of us--and I\'m sure we certainly never heard of them--and\r\npeople seem to forget things so soon--they seem to forget anything. You\r\ncan\'t imagine how things have changed here!"\r\n\r\nGeorge gulped painfully before he could speak. "You--you mean to sit\r\nthere and tell me that if I\'d just let things go on--Oh!" He swung away,\r\nwalking the floor again. "I tell you I did the only right thing! If\r\nyou don\'t think so, why in the name of heaven can\'t you say what else\r\nI should have done? It\'s easy enough to criticize, but the person who\r\ncriticizes a man ought at least to tell him what else he should have\r\ndone! You think I was wrong!"\r\n\r\n"I\'m not saying so," she said.\r\n\r\n"You did at the time!" he cried. "You said enough then, I think! Well,\r\nwhat have you to say now, if you\'re so sure I was wrong?"\r\n\r\n"Nothing, George."\r\n\r\n"It\'s only because you\'re afraid to!" he said, and he went on with a\r\nsudden bitter divination: "You\'re reproaching yourself with what you had\r\nto do with all that; and you\'re trying to make up for it by doing and\r\nsaying what you think mother would want you to, and you think I couldn\'t\r\nstand it if I got to thinking I might have done differently. Oh, I know!\r\nThat\'s exactly what\'s in your mind: you do think I was wrong! So does\r\nUncle George. I challenged him about it the other day, and he answered\r\njust as you\'re answering--evaded, and tried to be gentler. I don\'t care\r\nto be handled with gloves! I tell you I was right, and I don\'t need any\r\ncoddling by people that think I wasn\'t! And I suppose you believe I was\r\nwrong not to let Morgan see her that last night when he came here, and\r\nshe--she was dying. If you do, why in the name of God did you come and\r\nask me? You could have taken him in! She did want to see him. She--"\r\n\r\nMiss Fanny looked startled. "You think--"\r\n\r\n"She told me so!" And the tortured young man choked. "She said--\'just\r\nonce.\' She said \'I\'d like to have seen him--just once!\' She meant--to\r\ntell him good-bye! That\'s what she meant! And you put this on me, too;\r\nyou put this responsibility on me! But I tell you, and I told Uncle\r\nGeorge, that the responsibility isn\'t all mine! If you were so sure I\r\nwas wrong all the time--when I took her away, and when I turned Morgan\r\nout--if you were so sure, what did you let me do it for? You and Uncle\r\nGeorge were grown people, both of you, weren\'t you? You were older than\r\nI, and if you were so sure you were wiser than I, why did you just stand\r\naround with your hands hanging down, and let me go ahead? You could have\r\nstopped it if it was wrong, couldn\'t you?"\r\n\r\nFanny shook her head. "No, George," she said slowly. "Nobody could have\r\nstopped you. You were too strong, and--"\r\n\r\n"And what?" he demanded loudly.\r\n\r\n"And she loved you--too well."\r\n\r\nGeorge stared at her hard, then his lower lip began to move\r\nconvulsively, and he set his teeth upon it but could not check its\r\nfrantic twitching.\r\n\r\nHe ran out of the room.\r\n\r\nShe sat still, listening. He had plunged into his mother\'s room, but\r\nno sound came to Fanny\'s ears after the sharp closing of the door;\r\nand presently she rose and stepped out into the hall--but could hear\r\nnothing. The heavy black walnut door of Isabel\'s room, as Fanny\'s\r\ntroubled eyes remained fixed upon it, seemed to become darker and\r\nvaguer; the polished wood took the distant ceiling light, at the end\r\nof the hall, in dim reflections which became mysterious; and to Fanny\'s\r\ndisturbed mind the single sharp point of light on the bronze door-knob\r\nwas like a continuous sharp cry in the stillness of night. What\r\ninterview was sealed away from human eye and ear within the lonely\r\ndarkness on the other side of that door--in that darkness where Isabel\'s\r\nown special chairs were, and her own special books, and the two great\r\nwalnut wardrobes filled with her dresses and wraps? What tragic argument\r\nmight be there vainly striving to confute the gentle dead? "In God\'s\r\nname, what else could I have done?" For his mother\'s immutable silence\r\nwas surely answering him as Isabel in life would never have answered\r\nhim, and he was beginning to understand how eloquent the dead can be.\r\nThey cannot stop their eloquence, no matter how they have loved the\r\nliving: they cannot choose. And so, no matter in what agony George\r\nshould cry out, "What else could I have done?" and to the end of his\r\nlife no matter how often he made that wild appeal, Isabel was doomed to\r\nanswer him with the wistful, faint murmur:\r\n\r\n"I\'d like to have-seen him. Just--just once."\r\n\r\nA cheerful darkey went by the house, loudly and tunelessly whistling\r\nsome broken thoughts upon women, fried food and gin; then a group of\r\nhigh school boys, returning homeward after important initiations, were\r\nheard skylarking along the sidewalk, rattling sticks on the fences,\r\nsquawking hoarsely, and even attempting to sing in the shocking new\r\nvoices of uncompleted adolescence. For no reason, and just as a poultry\r\nyard falls into causeless agitation, they stopped in front of the house,\r\nand for half an hour produced the effect of a noisy multitude in full\r\nriot.\r\n\r\nTo the woman standing upstairs in the hall, this was almost unbearable;\r\nand she felt that she would have to go down and call to them to stop;\r\nbut she was too timid, and after a time went back to her room, and sat\r\nat her desk again. She left the door open, and frequently glanced out\r\ninto the hall, but gradually became once more absorbed in the figures\r\nwhich represented her prospective income from her great plunge in\r\nelectric lights for automobiles. She did not hear George return to his\r\nown room.\r\n\r\nA superstitious person might have thought it unfortunate that her\r\npartner in this speculative industry (as in Wilbur\'s disastrous\r\nrolling-mills) was that charming but too haphazardous man of the world,\r\nGeorge Amberson. He was one of those optimists who believe that if you\r\nput money into a great many enterprises one of them is sure to turn out\r\na fortune, and therefore, in order to find the lucky one, it is only\r\nnecessary to go into a large enough number of them. Altogether gallant\r\nin spirit, and beautifully game under catastrophe, he had gone into a\r\ngreat many, and the unanimity of their "bad luck," as he called it,\r\ngave him one claim to be a distinguished person, if he had no other.\r\nIn business he was ill fated with a consistency which made him, in that\r\nalone, a remarkable man; and he declared, with some earnestness, that\r\nthere was no accounting for it except by the fact that there had been\r\nso much good luck in his family before he was born that something had to\r\nbalance it.\r\n\r\n"You ought to have thought of my record and stayed out," he told Fanny,\r\none day the next spring, when the affairs of the headlight company had\r\nbegun to look discouraging. "I feel the old familiar sinking that\'s\r\nattended all my previous efforts to prove myself a business genius. I\r\nthink it must be something like the feeling an aeronaut has when his\r\nballoon bursts, and, looking down, he sees below him the old home farm\r\nwhere he used to live--I mean the feeling he\'d have just before he\r\nflattened out in that same old clay barnyard. Things do look bleak, and\r\nI\'m only glad you didn\'t go into this confounded thing to the extent I\r\ndid."\r\n\r\nMiss Fanny grew pink. "But it must go right!" she protested. "We saw\r\nwith our own eyes how perfectly it worked in the shop. The light was so\r\nbright no one could face it, and so there can\'t be any reason for it not\r\nto work. It simply--"\r\n\r\n"Oh, you\'re right about that," Amberson said. "It certainly was a\r\nperfect thing--in the shop! The only thing we didn\'t know was how fast\r\nan automobile had to go to keep the light going. It appears that this\r\nwas a matter of some importance."\r\n\r\n"Well, how fast does one have to--"\r\n\r\n"To keep the light from going entirely out," he informed her with\r\nelaborate deliberation, "it is computed by those enthusiasts who have\r\nbought our product--and subsequently returned it to us and got their\r\nmoney back--they compute that a motor car must maintain a speed of\r\ntwenty-five miles an hour, or else there won\'t be any light at all.\r\nTo make the illumination bright enough to be noticed by an approaching\r\nautomobile, they state the speed must be more than thirty miles an\r\nhour. At thirty-five, objects in the path of the light begin to become\r\nvisible; at forty they are revealed distinctly; and at fifty and above\r\nwe have a real headlight. Unfortunately many people don\'t care to drive\r\nthat fast at all times after dusk, especially in the traffic, or where\r\npolicemen are likely to become objectionable."\r\n\r\n"But think of that test on the road when we--"\r\n\r\n"That test was lovely," he admitted. "The inventor made us happy with\r\nhis oratory, and you and Frank Bronson and I went whirling through the\r\nnight at a speed that thrilled us. It was an intoxicating sensation: we\r\nwere intoxicated by the lights, the lights and the music. We must never\r\nforget that drive, with the cool wind kissing our cheeks and the road\r\nlit up for miles ahead. We must never forget it and we never shall. It\r\ncost--"\r\n\r\n"But something\'s got to be done."\r\n\r\n"It has, indeed! My something would seem to be leaving my watch at my\r\nuncle\'s. Luckily, you--"\r\n\r\nThe pink of Fanny\'s cheeks became deeper. "But isn\'t that man going to\r\ndo anything to remedy it? can\'t he try to--"\r\n\r\n"He can try," said Amberson. "He is trying, in fact. I\'ve sat in the\r\nshop watching him try for several beautiful afternoons, while outside\r\nthe windows all Nature was fragrant with spring and smoke. He hums\r\nragtime to himself as he tries, and I think his mind is wandering to\r\nsomething else less tedious--to some new invention in which he\'d take\r\nmore interest."\r\n\r\n"But you mustn\'t let him," she cried. "You must make him keep on\r\ntrying!"\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes. He understands that\'s what I sit there for. I\'ll keep\r\nsitting!"\r\n\r\nHowever, in spite of the time he spent sitting in the shop, worrying\r\nthe inventor of the fractious light, Amberson found opportunity to worry\r\nhimself about another matter of business. This was the settlement of\r\nIsabel\'s estate.\r\n\r\n"It\'s curious about the deed to her house," he said to his nephew.\r\n"You\'re absolutely sure it wasn\'t among her papers?"\r\n\r\n"Mother didn\'t have any papers," George told him. "None at all. All she\r\never had to do with business was to deposit the cheques grandfather gave\r\nher and then write her own cheques against them."\r\n\r\n"The deed to the house was never recorded," Amberson said thoughtfully.\r\n"I\'ve been over to the courthouse to see. I asked father if he never\r\ngave her one, and he didn\'t seem able to understand me at first. Then he\r\nfinally said he thought he must have given her a deed long ago; but he\r\nwasn\'t sure. I rather think he never did. I think it would be just as\r\nwell to get him to execute one now in your favour. I\'ll speak to him\r\nabout it."\r\n\r\nGeorge sighed. "I don\'t think I\'d bother him about it: the house is\r\nmine, and you and I understand that it is. That\'s enough for me, and\r\nthere isn\'t likely to be much trouble between you and me when we come to\r\nsettling poor grandfather\'s estate. I\'ve just been with him, and I think\r\nit would only confuse him for you to speak to him about it again. I\r\nnotice he seems distressed if anybody tries to get his attention--he\'s a\r\nlong way off, somewhere, and he likes to stay that way. I think--I think\r\nmother wouldn\'t want us to bother him about it; I\'m sure she\'d tell us\r\nto let him alone. He looks so white and queer."\r\n\r\nAmberson shook his head. "Not much whiter and queerer than you do, young\r\nfellow! You\'d better begin to get some air and exercise and quit hanging\r\nabout in the house all day. I won\'t bother him any more than I can help;\r\nbut I\'ll have the deed made out ready for his signature."\r\n\r\n"I wouldn\'t bother him at all. I don\'t see--"\r\n\r\n"You might see," said his uncle uneasily. "The estate is just about\r\nas involved and mixed-up as an estate can well get, to the best of my\r\nknowledge; and I haven\'t helped it any by what he let me have for this\r\ninfernal headlight scheme which has finally gone trolloping forever to\r\nwhere the woodbine twineth. Leaves me flat, and poor old Frank Bronson\r\njust half flat, and Fanny--well, thank heaven! I kept her from going\r\nin so deep that it would leave her flat. It\'s rough on her as it is, I\r\nsuspect. You ought to have that deed."\r\n\r\n"No. Don\'t bother him."\r\n\r\n"I\'ll bother him as little as possible. I\'ll wait till some day when he\r\nseems to brighten up a little."\r\n\r\nBut Amberson waited too long. The Major had already taken eleven months\r\nsince his daughter\'s death to think important things out. He had got as\r\nfar with them as he could, and there was nothing to detain him longer\r\nin the world. One evening his grandson sat with him--the Major seemed\r\nto like best to have young George with him, so far as they were able to\r\nguess his preferences--and the old gentleman made a queer gesture:\r\nhe slapped his knee as if he had made a sudden discovery, or else\r\nremembered that he had forgotten something.\r\n\r\nGeorge looked at him with an air of inquiry, but said nothing. He had\r\ngrown to be almost as silent as his grandfather. However, the Major\r\nspoke without being questioned.\r\n\r\n"It must be in the sun," he said. "There wasn\'t anything here but the\r\nsun in the first place, and the earth came out of the sun, and we came\r\nout of the earth. So, whatever we are, we must have been in the sun. We\r\ngo back to the earth we came out of, so the earth will go back to the\r\nsun that it came out of. And time means nothing--nothing at all--so in a\r\nlittle while we\'ll all be back in the sun together. I wish--"\r\n\r\nHe moved his hand uncertainly as if reaching for something, and George\r\njumped up. "Did you want anything, grandfather?"\r\n\r\n"What?"\r\n\r\n"Would you like a glass of water?"\r\n\r\n"No--no. No; I don\'t want anything." The reaching hand dropped back upon\r\nthe arm of his chair, and he relapsed into silence; but a few minutes\r\nlater he finished the sentence he had begun:\r\n\r\n"I wish--somebody could tell me!"\r\n\r\nThe next day he had a slight cold, but he seemed annoyed when his son\r\nsuggested calling the doctor, and Amberson let him have his own way\r\nso far, in fact, that after he had got up and dressed, the following\r\nmorning, he was all alone when he went away to find out what he hadn\'t\r\nbeen able to think out--all those things he had wished "somebody" would\r\ntell him.\r\n\r\nOld Sam, shuffling in with the breakfast tray, found the Major in his\r\naccustomed easy-chair by the fireplace--and yet even the old darkey\r\ncould see instantly that the Major was not there.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXXI\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen the great Amberson Estate went into court for settlement, "there\r\nwasn\'t any," George Amberson said--that is, when the settlement was\r\nconcluded there was no estate. "I guessed it," Amberson went on. "As\r\nan expert on prosperity, my career is disreputable, but as a prophet\r\nof calamity I deserve a testimonial banquet." He reproached himself\r\nbitterly for not having long ago discovered that his father had never\r\ngiven Isabel a deed to her house. "And those pigs, Sydney and Amelia!"\r\nhe added, for this was another thing he was bitter about. "They won\'t\r\ndo anything. I\'m sorry I gave them the opportunity of making a polished\r\nrefusal. Amelia\'s letter was about half in Italian; she couldn\'t\r\nremember enough ways of saying no in English. One has to live quite a\r\nlong while to realize there are people like that! The estate was badly\r\ncrippled, even before they took out their \'third,\' and the \'third\' they\r\ntook was the only good part of the rotten apple. Well, I didn\'t ask them\r\nfor restitution on my own account, and at least it will save you some\r\ntrouble, young George. Never waste any time writing to them; you mustn\'t\r\ncount on them."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t," George said quietly. "I don\'t count on anything."\r\n\r\n"Oh, we\'ll not feel that things are quite desperate," Amberson laughed,\r\nbut not with great cheerfulness. "We\'ll survive, Georgie--you will,\r\nespecially. For my part I\'m a little too old and too accustomed to fall\r\nback on somebody else for supplies to start a big fight with life:\r\nI\'ll be content with just surviving, and I can do it on an\r\neighteen-hundred-dollar--a-year consulship. An ex-congressman can always\r\nbe pretty sure of getting some such job, and I hear from Washington the\r\nmatter\'s about settled. I\'ll live pleasantly enough with a pitcher of\r\nice under a palm tree, and black folks to wait on me--that part of it\r\nwill be like home--and I\'ll manage to send you fifty dollars every now\r\nand then, after I once get settled. So much for me! But you--of course\r\nyou\'ve had a poor training for making your own way, but you\'re only a\r\nboy after all, and the stuff of the old stock is in you. It\'ll come out\r\nand do something. I\'ll never forgive myself about that deed: it would\r\nhave given you something substantial to start with. Still, you have\r\na little tiny bit, and you\'ll have a little tiny salary, too; and of\r\ncourse your Aunt Fanny\'s here, and she\'s got something you can fall back\r\non if you get too pinched, until I can begin to send you a dribble now\r\nand then."\r\n\r\nGeorge\'s "little tiny bit" was six hundred dollars which had come to him\r\nfrom the sale of his mother\'s furniture; and the "little tiny salary"\r\nwas eight dollars a week which old Frank Bronson was to pay him for\r\nservices as a clerk and student-at-law. Old Frank would have offered\r\nmore to the Major\'s grandson, but since the death of that best of\r\nclients and his own experience with automobile headlights, he was not\r\ncertain of being able to pay more and at the same time settle his own\r\nsmall bills for board and lodging. George had accepted haughtily, and\r\nthereby removed a burden from his uncle\'s mind.\r\n\r\nAmberson himself, however, had not even a "tiny bit"; though he got his\r\nconsular appointment; and to take him to his post he found it necessary\r\nto borrow two hundred of his nephew\'s six hundred dollars. "It makes me\r\nsick, George," he said. "But I\'d better get there and get that salary\r\nstarted. Of course Eugene would do anything in the world, and the fact\r\nis he wanted to, but I felt that--ah--under the circumstances--"\r\n\r\n"Never!" George exclaimed, growing red. "I can\'t imagine one of the\r\nfamily--" He paused, not finding it necessary to explain that "the\r\nfamily" shouldn\'t turn a man from the door and then accept favours from\r\nhim. "I wish you\'d take more."\r\n\r\nAmberson declined. "One thing I\'ll say for you, young George; you\r\nhaven\'t a stingy bone in your body. That\'s the Amberson stock in\r\nyou--and I like it!"\r\n\r\nHe added something to this praise of his nephew on the day he left for\r\nWashington. He was not to return, but to set forth from the capital on\r\nthe long journey to his post. George went with him to the station, and\r\ntheir farewell was lengthened by the train\'s being several minutes late.\r\n\r\n"I may not see you again, Georgie," Amberson said; and his voice was a\r\nlittle husky as he set a kind hand on the young man\'s shoulder. "It\'s\r\nquite probable that from this time on we\'ll only know each other by\r\nletter--until you\'re notified as my next of kin that there\'s an old\r\nvalise to be forwarded to you, and perhaps some dusty curios from\r\nthe consulate mantelpiece. Well, it\'s an odd way for us to be saying\r\ngood-bye: one wouldn\'t have thought it, even a few years ago, but here\r\nwe are, two gentlemen of elegant appearance in a state of bustitude.\r\nWe can\'t ever tell what will happen at all, can we? Once I stood where\r\nwe\'re standing now, to say good-bye to a pretty girl--only it was in the\r\nold station before this was built, and we called it the \'depot.\' She\'d\r\nbeen visiting your mother, before Isabel was married, and I was wild\r\nabout her, and she admitted she didn\'t mind that. In fact, we decided we\r\ncouldn\'t live without each other, and we were to be married. But she had\r\nto go abroad first with her father, and when we came to say good-bye\r\nwe knew we wouldn\'t see each other again for almost a year. I thought I\r\ncouldn\'t live through it--and she stood here crying. Well, I don\'t even\r\nknow where she lives now, or if she is living--and I only happen to\r\nthink of her sometimes when I\'m here at the station waiting for a train.\r\nIf she ever thinks of me she probably imagines I\'m still dancing in the\r\nballroom at the Amberson Mansion, and she probably thinks of the Mansion\r\nas still beautiful--still the finest house in town. Life and money both\r\nbehave like loose quicksilver in a nest of cracks. And when they\'re gone\r\nwe can\'t tell where--or what the devil we did with \'em! But I believe\r\nI\'ll say now--while there isn\'t much time left for either of us to get\r\nembarrassed about it--I believe I\'ll say that I\'ve always been fond of\r\nyou, Georgie, but I can\'t say that I always liked you. Sometimes I\'ve\r\nfelt you were distinctly not an acquired taste. Until lately, one had to\r\nbe fond of you just naturally--this isn\'t very \'tactful,\' of course--for\r\nif he didn\'t, well, he wouldn\'t! We all spoiled you terribly when you\r\nwere a little boy and let you grow up en prince--and I must say you took\r\nto it! But you\'ve received a pretty heavy jolt, and I had enough of\r\nyour disposition, myself, at your age, to understand a little of what\r\ncocksure youth has to go through inside when it finds that it can\r\nmake terrible mistakes. Poor old fellow! You get both kinds of jolts\r\ntogether, spiritual and material--and you\'ve taken them pretty quietly\r\nand--well, with my train coming into the shed, you\'ll forgive me\r\nfor saying that there have been times when I thought you ought to be\r\nhanged--but I\'ve always been fond of you, and now I like you! And just\r\nfor a last word: there may be somebody else in this town who\'s always\r\nfelt about you like that--fond of you, I mean, no matter how much it\r\nseemed you ought to be hanged. You might try--Hello, I must run. I\'ll\r\nsend back the money as fast as they pay me--so, good-bye and God bless\r\nyou, Georgie!"\r\n\r\nHe passed through the gates, waved his hat cheerily from the other side\r\nof the iron screen, and was lost from sight in the hurrying crowd.\r\nAnd as he disappeared, an unexpected poignant loneliness fell upon his\r\nnephew so heavily and so suddenly that he had no energy to recoil from\r\nthe shock. It seemed to him that the last fragment of his familiar world\r\nhad disappeared, leaving him all alone forever.\r\n\r\nHe walked homeward slowly through what appeared to be the strange\r\nstreets of a strange city; and, as a matter of fact, the city was\r\nstrange to him. He had seen little of it during his years in college,\r\nand then had followed the long absence and his tragic return. Since that\r\nhe had been "scarcely outdoors at all," as Fanny complained, warning him\r\nthat his health would suffer, and he had been downtown only in a closed\r\ncarriage. He had not realized the great change.\r\n\r\nThe streets were thunderous; a vast energy heaved under the universal\r\ncoating of dinginess. George walked through the begrimed crowds of\r\nhurrying strangers and saw no face that he remembered. Great numbers\r\nof the faces were even of a kind he did not remember ever to have seen;\r\nthey were partly like the old type that his boyhood knew, and partly\r\nlike types he knew abroad. He saw German eyes with American wrinkles at\r\ntheir corners; he saw Irish eyes and Neapolitan eyes, Roman eyes,\r\nTuscan eyes, eyes of Lombardy, of Savoy, Hungarian eyes, Balkan eyes,\r\nScandinavian eyes--all with a queer American look in them. He saw Jews\r\nwho had been German Jews, Jews who had been Russian Jews, Jews who had\r\nbeen Polish Jews but were no longer German or Russian or Polish Jews.\r\nAll the people were soiled by the smoke-mist through which they hurried,\r\nunder the heavy sky that hung close upon the new skyscrapers; and nearly\r\nall seemed harried by something impending, though here and there a women\r\nwith bundles would be laughing to a companion about some adventure of\r\nthe department stores, or perhaps an escape from the charging traffic\r\nof the streets--and not infrequently a girl, or a free-and-easy young\r\nmatron, found time to throw an encouraging look to George.\r\n\r\nHe took no note of these, and, leaving the crowded sidewalks, turned\r\nnorth into National Avenue, and presently reached the quieter but no\r\nless begrimed region of smaller shops and old-fashioned houses. Those\r\nlatter had been the homes of his boyhood playmates; old friends of his\r\ngrandfather had lived here;--in this alley he had fought with two boys\r\nat the same time, and whipped them; in that front yard he had been\r\nsuccessfully teased into temporary insanity by a. Sunday-school class of\r\npinky little girls. On that sagging porch a laughing woman had fed\r\nhim and other boys with doughnuts and gingerbread; yonder he saw the\r\nstaggered relics of the iron picket fence he had made his white pony\r\njump, on a dare, and in the shabby, stone-faced house behind the fence\r\nhe had gone to children\'s parties, and, when he was a little older he\r\nhad danced there often, and fallen in love with Mary Sharon, and kissed\r\nher, apparently by force, under the stairs in the hall. The double front\r\ndoors, of meaninglessly carved walnut, once so glossily varnished, had\r\nbeen painted smoke gray, but the smoke grime showed repulsively, even on\r\nthe smoke gray; and over the doors a smoked sign proclaimed the place to\r\nbe a "Stag Hotel."\r\n\r\nOther houses had become boarding-houses too genteel for signs, but many\r\nwere franker, some offering "board by the day, week or meal," and some,\r\nmore laconic, contenting themselves with the label: "Rooms." One,\r\nhaving torn out part of an old stone-trimmed bay window for purposes of\r\ncommercial display, showed forth two suspended petticoats and a pair\r\nof oyster-coloured flannel trousers to prove the claims of its\r\nblack-and-gilt sign: "French Cleaning and Dye House." Its next neighbour\r\nalso sported a remodelled front and permitted no doubt that its mission\r\nin life was to attend cosily upon death: "J. M. Rolsener. Caskets.\r\nThe Funeral Home." And beyond that, a plain old honest four-square\r\ngray-painted brick house was flamboyantly decorated with a great gilt\r\nscroll on the railing of the old-fashioned veranda: "Mutual Benev\'t\r\nOrder Cavaliers and Dames of Purity." This was the old Minafer house.\r\n\r\nGeorge passed it without perceptibly wincing; in fact, he held his head\r\nup, and except for his gravity of countenance and the prison pallor he\r\nhad acquired by too constantly remaining indoors, there was little to\r\nwarn an acquaintance that he was not precisely the same George Amberson\r\nMinafer known aforetime. He was still so magnificent, indeed, that there\r\ncame to his ears a waft of comment from a passing automobile. This was a\r\nfearsome red car, glittering in brass, with half-a-dozen young people\r\nin it whose motorism had reached an extreme manifestation in dress. The\r\nladies of this party were favourably affected at sight of the pedestrian\r\nupon the sidewalk, and, as the machine was moving slowly, and close to\r\nthe curb, they had time to observe him in detail, which they did with a\r\nfrankness not pleasing to the object of their attentions. "One sees so\r\nmany nice-looking people one doesn\'t know nowadays," said the youngest\r\nof the young ladies. "This old town of ours is really getting enormous.\r\nI shouldn\'t mind knowing who he is."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know," the youth beside her said, loudly enough to be heard at\r\na considerable distance. "I don\'t know who he is, but from his looks I\r\nknow who he thinks he is: he thinks he\'s the Grand Duke Cuthbert!" There\r\nwas a burst of tittering as the car gathered speed and rolled away, with\r\nthe girl continuing to look back until her scandalized companions forced\r\nher to turn by pulling her hood over her face. She made an impression\r\nupon George, so deep a one, in fact, that he unconsciously put his\r\nemotion into a muttered word:\r\n\r\nRiffraff!\r\n\r\nThis was the last "walk home" he was ever to take by the route he was\r\nnow following: up National Avenue to Amberson Addition and the two big\r\nold houses at the foot of Amberson Boulevard; for tonight would be the\r\nlast night that he and Fanny were to spend in the house which the Major\r\nhad forgotten to deed to Isabel. To-morrow they were to "move out," and\r\nGeorge was to begin his work in Bronson\'s office. He had not come to\r\nthis collapse without a fierce struggle--but the struggle was inward,\r\nand the rolling world was not agitated by it, and rolled calmly on.\r\nFor of all the "ideals of life" which the world, in its rolling,\r\ninconsiderately flattens out to nothingness, the least likely to retain\r\na profile is that ideal which depends upon inheriting money. George\r\nAmberson, in spite of his record of failures in business, had spoken\r\nshrewdly when he realized at last that money, like life, was "like\r\nquicksilver in a nest of cracks." And his nephew had the awakening\r\nexperience of seeing the great Amberson Estate vanishing into such\r\na nest--in a twinkling, it seemed, now that it was indeed so utterly\r\nvanished.\r\n\r\nHis uncle had suggested that he might write to college friends; perhaps\r\nthey could help him to something better than the prospect offered\r\nby Bronson\'s office; but George flushed and shook his head, without\r\nexplaining. In that small and quietly superior "crowd" of his he had too\r\nemphatically supported the ideal of being rather than doing. He could\r\nnot appeal to one of its members now to help him to a job. Besides, they\r\nwere not precisely the warmest-hearted crew in the world, and he had\r\nlong ago dropped the last affectation of a correspondence with any of\r\nthem. He was as aloof from any survival of intimacy with his boyhood\r\nfriends in the city, and, in truth, had lost track of most of them. "The\r\nFriends of the Ace," once bound by oath to succour one another in peril\r\nor poverty, were long ago dispersed; one or two had died; one or two\r\nhad gone to live elsewhere; the others were disappeared into the smoky\r\nbigness of the heavy city. Of the brethren, there remained within\r\nhis present cognizance only his old enemy, the red-haired Kinney, now\r\nmarried to Janie Sharon, and Charlie Johnson, who, out of deference\r\nto his mother\'s memory, had passed the Amberson Mansion one day, when\r\nGeorge stood upon the front steps, and, looking in fiercely, had looked\r\naway with continued fierceness--his only token of recognition.\r\n\r\nOn this last homeward walk of his, when George reached the entrance\r\nto Amberson Addition--that is, when he came to where the entrance had\r\nformerly been--he gave a little start, and halted for a moment to stare.\r\nThis was the first time he had noticed that the stone pillars, marking\r\nthe entrance, had been removed. Then he realized that for a long time he\r\nhad been conscious of a queerness about this corner without being aware\r\nof what made the difference. National Avenue met Amberson Boulevard here\r\nat an obtuse angle, and the removal of the pillars made the Boulevard\r\nseem a cross-street of no overpowering importance--certainly it did not\r\nseem to be a boulevard!\r\n\r\nAt the next corner Neptune\'s Fountain remained, and one could still\r\ndetermine with accuracy what its designer\'s intentions had been. It\r\nstood in sore need of just one last kindness; and if the thing had\r\npossessed any friends they would have done that doleful shovelling after\r\ndark.\r\n\r\nGeorge did not let his eyes linger upon the relic; nor did he look\r\nsteadfastly at the Amberson Mansion. Massive as the old house was, it\r\nmanaged to look gaunt: its windows stared with the skull emptiness of\r\nall windows in empty houses that are to be lived in no more. Of course\r\nthe rowdy boys of the neighbourhood had been at work: many of these\r\nhaggard windows were broken; the front door stood ajar, forced open; and\r\nidiot salacity, in white chalk, was smeared everywhere upon the pillars\r\nand stonework of the verandas.\r\n\r\nGeorge walked by the Mansion hurriedly, and came home to his mother\'s\r\nhouse for the last time.\r\n\r\nEmptiness was there, too, and the closing of the door resounded through\r\nbare rooms; for downstairs there was no furniture in the house except a\r\nkitchen table in the dining room, which Fanny had kept "for dinner," she\r\nsaid, though as she was to cook and serve that meal herself George had\r\nhis doubts about her name for it. Upstairs, she had retained her own\r\nfurniture, and George had been living in his mother\'s room, having sent\r\neverything from his own to the auction. Isabel\'s room was still as it\r\nhad been, but the furniture would be moved with Fanny\'s to new quarters\r\nin the morning. Fanny had made plans for her nephew as well as herself;\r\nshe had found a three-room "kitchenette apartment" in an apartment house\r\nwhere several old friends of hers had established themselves--elderly\r\nwidows of citizens once "prominent" and other retired gentry. People\r\nused their own "kitchenettes" for breakfast and lunch, but there was\r\na table-d\'hote arrangement for dinner on the ground floor; and after\r\ndinner bridge was played all evening, an attraction powerful with\r\nFanny. She had "made all the arrangements," she reported, and nervously\r\nappealed for approval, asking if she hadn\'t shown herself "pretty\r\npractical" in such matters. George acquiesced absent-mindedly, not\r\nthinking of what she said and not realizing to what it committed him.\r\n\r\nHe began to realize it now, as he wandered about the dismantled house;\r\nhe was far from sure that he was willing to go and live in a "three-room\r\napartment" with Fanny and eat breakfast and lunch with her (prepared by\r\nherself in the "kitchenette") and dinner at the table d\'hote in "such a\r\npretty Colonial dining room" (so Fanny described it) at a little round\r\ntable they would have all to themselves in the midst of a dozen little\r\nround tables which other relics of disrupted families would have all to\r\nthemselves. For the first time, now that the change was imminent, George\r\nbegan to develop before his mind\'s eye pictures of what he was in for;\r\nand they appalled him. He decided that such a life verged upon the\r\nsheerly unbearable, and that after all there were some things left that\r\nhe just couldn\'t stand. So he made up his mind to speak to his aunt\r\nabout it at "dinner," and tell her that he preferred to ask Bronson to\r\nlet him put a sofa-bed, a trunk, and a folding rubber bathtub behind a\r\nscreen in the dark rear room of the office. George felt that this\r\nwould be infinitely more tolerable; and he could eat at restaurants,\r\nespecially as about all he ever wanted nowadays was coffee.\r\n\r\nBut at "dinner" he decided to put off telling Fanny of his plan until\r\nlater: she was so nervous, and so distressed about the failure of her\r\nefforts with sweetbreads and macaroni; and she was so eager in her talk\r\nof how comfortable they would be "by this time to-morrow night." She\r\nfluttered on, her nervousness increasing, saying how "nice" it would\r\nbe for him, when he came from work in the evenings, to be among "nice\r\npeople--people who know who we are," and to have a pleasant game of\r\nbridge with "people who are really old friends of the family?"\r\n\r\nWhen they stopped probing among the scorched fragments she had set\r\nforth, George lingered downstairs, waiting for a better opportunity to\r\nintroduce his own subject, but when he heard dismaying sounds from the\r\nkitchen he gave up. There was a crash, then a shower of crashes; falling\r\ntin clamoured to be heard above the shattering of porcelain; and over\r\nall rose Fanny\'s wail of lamentation for the treasures saved from the\r\nsale, but now lost forever to the "kitchenette." Fanny was nervous\r\nindeed; so nervous that she could not trust her hands.\r\n\r\nFor a moment George thought she might have been injured, but, before he\r\nreached the kitchen, he heard her sweeping at the fragments, and turned\r\nback. He put off speaking to Fanny until morning.\r\n\r\nThings more insistent than his vague plans for a sofa-bed in Bronson\'s\r\noffice had possession of his mind as he went upstairs, moving his hand\r\nslowly along the smooth walnut railing of the balustrade. Half way to\r\nthe landing he stopped, turned, and stood looking down at the heavy\r\ndoors masking the black emptiness that had been the library. Here he\r\nhad stood on what he now knew was the worst day of his life; here he had\r\nstood when his mother passed through that doorway, hand-in-hand with her\r\nbrother, to learn what her son had done.\r\n\r\nHe went on more heavily, more slowly; and, more heavily and slowly\r\nstill, entered Isabel\'s room and shut the door. He did not come forth\r\nagain, and bade Fanny good-night through the closed door when she\r\nstopped outside it later.\r\n\r\n"I\'ve put all the lights out, George," she said. "Everything\'s all\r\nright."\r\n\r\n"Very well," he called. "Good-night."\r\n\r\nShe did not go. "I\'m sure we\'re going to enjoy the new little home,\r\nGeorge," she said timidly. "I\'ll try hard to make things nice for you,\r\nand the people really are lovely. You mustn\'t feel as if things are\r\naltogether gloomy, George. I know everything\'s going to turn out all\r\nright. You\'re young and strong and you have a good mind and I\'m sure--"\r\nshe hesitated--"I\'m sure your mother\'s watching over you, Georgie.\r\nGood-night, dear."\r\n\r\n"Good-night, Aunt Fanny."\r\n\r\nHis voice had a strangled sound in spite of him; but she seemed not to\r\nnotice it, and he heard her go to her own room and lock herself in with\r\nbolt and key against burglars. She had said the one thing she should\r\nnot have said just then: "I\'m sure your mother\'s watching over you,\r\nGeorgie." She had meant to be kind, but it destroyed his last chance for\r\nsleep that night. He would have slept little if she had not said it, but\r\nsince she had said it, he could not sleep at all. For he knew that it\r\nwas true--if it could be true--and that his mother, if she still lived\r\nin spirit, would be weeping on the other side of the wall of silence,\r\nweeping and seeking for some gate to let her through so that she could\r\ncome and "watch over him."\r\n\r\nHe felt that if there were such gates they were surely barred: they\r\nwere like those awful library doors downstairs, which had shut her in to\r\nbegin the suffering to which he had consigned her.\r\n\r\nThe room was still Isabel\'s. Nothing had been changed: even the\r\nphotographs of George, of the Major, and of "brother George" still stood\r\non her dressing-table, and in a drawer of her desk was an old picture of\r\nEugene and Lucy, taken together, which George had found, but had slowly\r\nclosed away again from sight, not touching it. To-morrow everything\r\nwould be gone; and he had heard there was not long to wait before the\r\nhouse itself would be demolished. The very space which tonight was still\r\nIsabel\'s room would be cut into new shapes by new walls and floors and\r\nceilings; yet the room would always live, for it could not die out of\r\nGeorge\'s memory. It would live as long as he did, and it would always be\r\nmurmurous with a tragic, wistful whispering.\r\n\r\nAnd if space itself can be haunted, as memory is haunted, then some\r\ntime, when the space that was Isabel\'s room came to be made into the\r\nsmall bedrooms and "kitchenettes" already designed as its destiny, that\r\nspace might well be haunted and the new occupants come to feel that some\r\nseemingly causeless depression hung about it--a wraith of the passion\r\nthat filled it throughout the last night that George Minafer spent\r\nthere.\r\n\r\nWhatever remnants of the old high-handed arrogance were still within\r\nhim, he did penance for his deepest sin that night--and it may be that\r\nto this day some impressionable, overworked woman in a "kitchenette,"\r\nafter turning out the light will seem to see a young man kneeling in the\r\ndarkness, shaking convulsively, and, with arms outstretched through the\r\nwall, clutching at the covers of a shadowy bed. It may seem to her that\r\nshe hears the faint cry, over and over:\r\n\r\n"Mother, forgive me! God, forgive me!"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXXII\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nAt least, it may be claimed for George that his last night in the house\r\nwhere he had been born was not occupied with his own disheartening\r\nfuture, but with sorrow for what sacrifices his pride and youth had\r\ndemanded of others. And early in the morning he came downstairs and\r\ntried to help Fanny make coffee on the kitchen range.\r\n\r\n"There was something I wanted to say to you last night, Aunt Fanny," he\r\nsaid, as she finally discovered that an amber fluid, more like tea than\r\ncoffee, was as near ready to be taken into the human system as it would\r\never be. "I think I\'d better do it now."\r\n\r\nShe set the coffee-pot back upon the stove with a little crash, and,\r\nlooking at him in a desperate anxiety, began to twist her dainty apron\r\nbetween her fingers without any consciousness of what she was doing.\r\n\r\n"Why--why--" she stammered; but she knew what he was going to\r\nsay, and that was why she had been more and more nervous.\r\n"Hadn\'t--perhaps--perhaps we\'d better get the--the things moved to the\r\nlittle new home first, George. Let\'s--"\r\n\r\nHe interrupted quietly, though at her phrase, "the little new home," his\r\npungent impulse was to utter one loud shout and run. "It was about this\r\nnew place that I wanted to speak. I\'ve been thinking it over, and I\'ve\r\ndecided. I want you to take all the things from mother\'s room and use\r\nthem and keep them for me, and I\'m sure the little apartment will be\r\njust what you like; and with the extra bedroom probably you could find\r\nsome woman friend to come and live there, and share the expense with\r\nyou. But I\'ve decided on another arrangement for myself, and so I\'m not\r\ngoing with you. I don\'t suppose you\'ll mind much, and I don\'t see why\r\nyou should mind--particularly, that is. I\'m not very lively company\r\nthese days, or any days, for that matter. I can\'t imagine you, or any\r\none else, being much attached to me, so--"\r\n\r\nHe stopped in amazement: no chair had been left in the kitchen, but\r\nFanny gave a despairing glance around her, in search of one, then sank\r\nabruptly, and sat flat upon the floor.\r\n\r\n"You\'re going to leave me in the lurch!" she gasped.\r\n\r\n"What on earth--" George sprang to her. "Get up, Aunt Fanny!"\r\n\r\n"I can\'t. I\'m too weak. Let me alone, George!" And as he released the\r\nwrist he had seized to help her, she repeated the dismal prophecy which\r\nfor days she had been matching against her hopes: "You\'re going to leave\r\nme--in the lurch!"\r\n\r\n"Why no, Aunt Fanny!" he protested. "At first I\'d have been something\r\nof a burden on you. I\'m to get eight dollars a week; about thirty-two\r\na month. The rent\'s thirty-six dollars a month, and the table-d\'hote\r\ndinner runs up to over twenty-two dollars apiece, so with my half of the\r\nrent--eighteen dollars--I\'d have less than nothing left out of my salary\r\nto pay my share of the groceries for all the breakfasts and luncheons.\r\nYou see you\'d not only be doing all the housework and cooking, but you\'d\r\nbe paying more of the expenses than I would."\r\n\r\nShe stared at him with such a forlorn blankness as he had never seen.\r\n"I\'d be paying--" she said feebly. "I\'d be paying--"\r\n\r\n"Certainly you would. You\'d be using more of your money than--"\r\n\r\n"My money!" Fanny\'s chin drooped upon her thin chest, and she laughed\r\nmiserably. "I\'ve got twenty-eight dollars. That\'s all."\r\n\r\n"You mean until the interest is due again?"\r\n\r\n"I mean that\'s all," Fanny said. "I mean that\'s all there is. There\r\nwon\'t be any more interest because there isn\'t any principal."\r\n\r\n"Why, you told--"\r\n\r\nShe shook her head. "No, I haven\'t told you anything."\r\n\r\n"Then it was Uncle George. He told me you had enough to fall back on.\r\nThat\'s just what he said: \'to fall back on.\' He said you\'d lost more\r\nthan you should, in the headlight company, but he\'d insisted that you\r\nshould hold out enough to live on, and you\'d very wisely followed his\r\nadvice."\r\n\r\n"I know," she said weakly. "I told him so. He didn\'t know, or else he\'d\r\nforgotten, how much Wilbur\'s insurance amounted to, and I--oh, it seemed\r\nsuch a sure way to make a real fortune out of a little--and I thought I\r\ncould do something for you, George, if you ever came to need it--and it\r\nall looked so bright I just thought I\'d put it all in. I did--every cent\r\nexcept my last interest payment--and it\'s gone."\r\n\r\n"Good Lord!" George began to pace up and down on the worn planks of the\r\nbare floor. "Why on earth did you wait till now to tell such a thing as\r\nthis?"\r\n\r\n"I couldn\'t tell till I had to," she said piteously. "I couldn\'t till\r\nGeorge Amberson went away. He couldn\'t do anything to help, anyhow, and\r\nI just didn\'t want him to talk to me about it--he\'s been at me so much\r\nabout not putting more in than I could afford to lose, and said he\r\nconsidered he had my--my word I wasn\'t putting more than that in it. So\r\nI thought: What was the use? What was the use of going over it all\r\nwith him and having him reproach me, and probably reproach himself?\r\nIt wouldn\'t do any good--not any good on earth." She got out her lace\r\nhandkerchief and began to cry. "Nothing does any good, I guess, in this\r\nold world. Oh, how tired of this old world I am! I didn\'t know what to\r\ndo. I just tried to go ahead and be as practical as I could, and arrange\r\nsome way for us to live. Oh, I knew you didn\'t want me, George! You\r\nalways teased me and berated me whenever you had a chance from the time\r\nyou were a little boy--you did so! Later, you\'ve tried to be kinder to\r\nme, but you don\'t want me around--oh, I can see that much! You don\'t\r\nsuppose I want to thrust myself on you, do you? It isn\'t very pleasant\r\nto be thrusting yourself on a person you know doesn\'t want you--but I\r\nknew you oughtn\'t to be left all alone in the world; it isn\'t good. I\r\nknew your mother\'d want me to watch over you and try to have something\r\nlike a home for you--I know she\'d want me to do what I tried to do!"\r\nFanny\'s tears were bitter now, and her voice, hoarse and wet, was\r\ntragically sincere. "I tried--I tried to be practical--to look after\r\nyour interests--to make things as nice for you as I could--I walked my\r\nheels down looking for a place for us to live--I walked and walked over\r\nthis town--I didn\'t ride one block on a street-car--I wouldn\'t use five\r\ncents no matter how tired I--Oh!" She sobbed uncontrollably. "Oh! and\r\nnow--you don\'t want--you want--you want to leave me in the lurch! You--"\r\n\r\nGeorge stopped walking. "In God\'s name, Aunt Fanny," he said, "quit\r\nspreading out your handkerchief and drying it and then getting it all\r\nwet again! I mean stop crying! Do! And for heaven\'s sake, get up. Don\'t\r\nsit there with your back against the boiler and--"\r\n\r\n"It\'s not hot," Fanny sniffled. "It\'s cold; the plumbers disconnected\r\nit. I wouldn\'t mind if they hadn\'t. I wouldn\'t mind if it burned me,\r\nGeorge."\r\n\r\n"Oh, my Lord!" He went to her, and lifted her. "For God\'s sake, get up!\r\nCome, let\'s take the coffee into the other room, and see what\'s to be\r\ndone."\r\n\r\nHe got her to her feet; she leaned upon him, already somewhat comforted,\r\nand, with his arm about her, he conducted her to the dining room and\r\nseated her in one of the two kitchen chairs which had been placed at\r\nthe rough table. "There!" he said, "get over it!" Then he brought the\r\ncoffee-pot, some lumps of sugar in a tin pan, and, finding that all the\r\ncoffee-cups were broken, set water glasses upon the table, and poured\r\nsome of the pale coffee into them. By this time Fanny\'s spirits had\r\nrevived appreciably: she looked up with a plaintive eagerness. "I had\r\nbought all my fall clothes, George," she said; "and I paid every bill I\r\nowed. I don\'t owe a cent for clothes, George."\r\n\r\n"That\'s good," he said wanly, and he had a moment of physical dizziness\r\nthat decided him to sit down quickly. For an instant it seemed to him\r\nthat he was not Fanny\'s nephew, but married to her. He passed his pale\r\nhand over his paler forehead. "Well, let\'s see where we stand," he said\r\nfeebly. "Let\'s see if we can afford this place you\'ve selected."\r\n\r\nFanny continued to brighten. "I\'m sure it\'s the most practical plan we\r\ncould possibly have worked out, George--and it is a comfort to be among\r\nnice people. I think we\'ll both enjoy it, because the truth is we\'ve\r\nbeen keeping too much to ourselves for a long while. It isn\'t good for\r\npeople."\r\n\r\n"I was thinking about the money, Aunt Fanny. You see--"\r\n\r\n"I\'m sure we can manage it," she interrupted quickly. "There really\r\nisn\'t a cheaper place in town that we could actually live in and be--"\r\nHere she interrupted herself. "Oh! There\'s one great economy I forgot to\r\ntell you, and it\'s especially an economy for you, because you\'re always\r\ntoo generous about such things: they don\'t allow any tipping. They have\r\nsigns that prohibit it."\r\n\r\n"That\'s good," he said grimly. "But the rent is thirty-six dollars a\r\nmonth; the dinner is twenty-two and a half for each of us, and we\'ve got\r\nto have some provision for other food. We won\'t need any clothes for a\r\nyear, perhaps--"\r\n\r\n"Oh, longer!" she exclaimed. "So you see--"\r\n\r\n"I see that forty-five and thirty-six make eighty-one," he said. "At\r\nthe lowest, we need a hundred dollars a month--and I\'m going to make\r\nthirty-two."\r\n\r\n"I thought of that, George," she said confidently, "and I\'m sure it will\r\nbe all right. You\'ll be earning a great deal more than that very soon."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t see any prospect of it--not till I\'m admitted to the bar, and\r\nthat will be two years at the earliest."\r\n\r\nFanny\'s confidence was not shaken. "I know you\'ll be getting on faster\r\nthan--"\r\n\r\n"Faster?" George echoed gravely. "We\'ve got to have more than that to\r\nstart with."\r\n\r\n"Well, there\'s the six hundred dollars from the sale. Six hundred and\r\ntwelve dollars it was."\r\n\r\n"It isn\'t six hundred and twelve now," said George. "It\'s about one\r\nhundred and sixty."\r\n\r\nFanny showed a momentary dismay. "Why, how--"\r\n\r\n"I lent Uncle George two hundred; I gave fifty apiece to old Sam and\r\nthose two other old darkies that worked for grandfather so long, and ten\r\nto each of the servants here--"\r\n\r\n"And you gave me thirty-six," she said thoughtfully, "for the first\r\nmonth\'s rent, in advance."\r\n\r\n"Did I? I\'d forgotten. Well, with about a hundred and sixty in bank and\r\nour expenses a hundred a month, it doesn\'t seem as if this new place--"\r\n\r\n"Still," she interrupted, "we have paid the first month\'s rent in\r\nadvance, and it does seem to be the most practical--"\r\n\r\nGeorge rose. "See here, Aunt Fanny," he said decisively. "You stay here\r\nand look after the moving. Old Frank doesn\'t expect me until afternoon,\r\nthis first day, but I\'ll go and see him now."\r\n\r\nIt was early, and old Frank, just established at his big, flat-topped\r\ndesk, was surprised when his prospective assistant and pupil walked\r\nin. He was pleased, as well as surprised, however, and rose, offering\r\na cordial old hand. "The real flare!" he said. "The real flare for the\r\nlaw. That\'s right! Couldn\'t wait till afternoon to begin! I\'m delighted\r\nthat you--"\r\n\r\n"I wanted to say--" George began, but his patron cut him off.\r\n\r\n"Wait just a minute, my boy. I\'ve prepared a little speech of welcome,\r\nand even though you\'re five hours ahead of time, I mean to deliver\r\nit. First of all, your grandfather was my old war-comrade and my best\r\nclient; for years I prospered through my connection with his business,\r\nand his grandson is welcome in my office and to my best efforts in his\r\nbehalf. But I want to confess, Georgie, that during your earlier youth I\r\nmay have had some slight feeling of--well, prejudice, not altogether in\r\nyour favour; but whatever slight feeling it was, it began to vanish on\r\nthat afternoon, a good while ago, when you stood up to your Aunt Amelia\r\nAmberson as you did in the Major\'s library, and talked to her as a man\r\nand a gentleman should. I saw then what good stuff was in you--and I\r\nalways wanted to mention it. If my prejudice hadn\'t altogether vanished\r\nafter that, the last vestiges disappeared during these trying times that\r\nhave come upon you this past year, when I have been a witness to the\r\ndepth of feeling you\'ve shown and your quiet consideration for your\r\ngrandfather and for everyone else around you. I just want to add that I\r\nthink you\'ll find an honest pleasure now in industry and frugality\r\nthat wouldn\'t have come to you in a more frivolous career. The law is a\r\njealous mistress and a stern mistress, but a--"\r\n\r\nGeorge had stood before him in great and increasing embarrassment; and\r\nhe was unable to allow the address to proceed to its conclusion.\r\n\r\n"I can\'t do it!" he burst out. "I can\'t take her for my mistress."\r\n\r\n"What?"\r\n\r\n"I\'ve come to tell you, I\'ve got to find something that\'s quicker. I\r\ncan\'t--"\r\n\r\nOld Frank got a little red. "Let\'s sit down," he said. "What\'s the\r\ntrouble?"\r\n\r\nGeorge told him.\r\n\r\nThe old gentleman listened sympathetically, only murmuring: "Well,\r\nwell!" from time to time, and nodding acquiescence.\r\n\r\n"You see she\'s set her mind on this apartment," George explained. "She\'s\r\ngot some old cronies there, and I guess she\'s been looking forward to\r\nthe games of bridge and the kind of harmless gossip that goes on in such\r\nplaces. Really, it\'s a life she\'d like better than anything else--better\r\nthan that she\'s lived at home, I really believe. It struck me she\'s\r\njust about got to have it, and after all she could hardly have anything\r\nless."\r\n\r\n"This comes pretty heavily upon me, you know," said old Frank. "I got\r\nher into that headlight company, and she fooled me about her resources\r\nas much as she did your Uncle George. I was never your father\'s adviser,\r\nif you remember, and when the insurance was turned over to her some\r\nother lawyer arranged it--probably your father\'s. But it comes pretty\r\nheavily on me, and I feel a certain responsibility."\r\n\r\n"Not at all. I\'m taking the responsibility."\r\n\r\nAnd George smiled with one corner of his mouth. "She\'s not your aunt,\r\nyou know, sir."\r\n\r\n"Well, I\'m unable to see, even if she\'s yours, that a young man is\r\nmorally called upon to give up a career at the law to provide his aunt\r\nwith a favourable opportunity to play bridge whist!"\r\n\r\n"No," George agreed. "But I haven\'t begun my \'career at the law\' so it\r\ncan\'t be said I\'m making any considerable sacrifice. I\'ll tell you how\r\nit is, sir." He flushed, and, looking out of the streaked and smoky\r\nwindow beside which he was sitting, spoke with difficulty. "I feel as\r\nif--as if perhaps I had one or two pretty important things in my life\r\nto make up for. Well, I can\'t. I can\'t make them up to--to whom I\r\nwould. It\'s struck me that, as I couldn\'t, I might be a little decent\r\nto somebody else, perhaps--if I could manage it! I never have been\r\nparticularly decent to poor old Aunt Fanny."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I don\'t know: I shouldn\'t say that. A little youthful teasing--I\r\ndoubt if she\'s minded so much. She felt your father\'s death\r\nterrifically, of course, but it seems to me she\'s had a fairly\r\ncomfortable life-up to now--if she was disposed to take it that way."\r\n\r\n"But \'up to now\' is the important thing," George said. "Now is now--and\r\nyou see I can\'t wait two years to be admitted to the bar and begin to\r\npractice. I\'ve got to start in at something else that pays from the\r\nstart, and that\'s what I\'ve come to you about. I have an idea, you see."\r\n\r\n"Well, I\'m glad of that!" said old Frank, smiling. "I can\'t think of\r\nanything just at this minute that pays from the start."\r\n\r\n"I only know of one thing, myself."\r\n\r\n"What is it?"\r\n\r\nGeorge flushed again, but managed to laugh at his own embarrassment. "I\r\nsuppose I\'m about as ignorant of business as anybody in the world," he\r\nsaid. "But I\'ve heard they pay very high wages to people in dangerous\r\ntrades; I\'ve always heard they did, and I\'m sure it must be true. I mean\r\npeople that handle touchy chemicals or high explosives--men in dynamite\r\nfactories, or who take things of that sort about the country in wagons,\r\nand shoot oil wells. I thought I\'d see if you couldn\'t tell me something\r\nmore about it, or else introduce me to someone who could, and then I\r\nthought I\'d see if I couldn\'t get something of the kind to do as soon as\r\npossible. My nerves are good; I\'m muscular, and I\'ve got a steady hand;\r\nit seemed to me that this was about the only line of work in the world\r\nthat I\'m fitted for. I wanted to get started to-day if I could."\r\n\r\nOld Frank gave him a long stare. At first this scrutiny was sharply\r\nincredulous; then it was grave; finally it developed into a threat of\r\noverwhelming laughter; a forked vein in his forehead became more visible\r\nand his eyes seemed about to protrude.\r\n\r\nBut he controlled his impulse; and, rising, took up his hat and\r\novercoat. "All right," he said. "If you\'ll promise not to get blown up,\r\nI\'ll go with you to see if we can find the job." Then, meaning what he\r\nsaid, but amazed that he did mean it, he added: "You certainly are the\r\nmost practical young man I ever met!"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXXIII\r\n\r\n\r\nThey found the job. It needed an apprenticeship of only six weeks,\r\nduring which period George was to receive fifteen dollars a week; after\r\nthat he would get twenty-eight. This settled the apartment question, and\r\nFanny was presently established in a greater contentment than she had\r\nknown for a long time. Early every morning she made something she called\r\n(and believed to be) coffee for George, and he was gallant enough not\r\nto undeceive her. She lunched alone in her "kitchenette," for George\'s\r\nplace of employment was ten miles out of town on an interurban\r\ntrolley-line, and he seldom returned before seven. Fanny found partners\r\nfor bridge by two o\'clock almost every afternoon, and she played until\r\nabout six. Then she got George\'s "dinner clothes" out for him--he\r\nmaintained this habit--and she changed her own dress. When he arrived\r\nhe usually denied that he was tired, though he sometimes looked tired,\r\nparticularly during the first few months; and he explained to her\r\nfrequently--looking bored enough with her insistence--that his work was\r\n"fairly light, and fairly congenial, too." Fanny had the foggiest idea\r\nof what it was, though she noticed that it roughened his hands and\r\nstained them. "Something in those new chemical works," she explained to\r\ncasual inquirers. It was not more definite in her own mind.\r\n\r\nRespect for George undoubtedly increased within her, however, and\r\nshe told him she\'d always had a feeling he might "turn out to be a\r\nmechanical genius, or something." George assented with a nod, as the\r\neasiest course open to him. He did not take a hand at bridge after\r\ndinner: his provisions\' for Fanny\'s happiness refused to extend that\r\nfar, and at the table d\'hote he was a rather discouraging boarder. He\r\nwas considered "affected" and absurdly "up-stage" by the one or two\r\nyoung men, and the three or four young women, who enlivened the elderly\r\nretreat; and was possibly less popular there than he had been elsewhere\r\nduring his life, though he was now nothing worse than a coldly polite\r\nyoung man who kept to himself. After dinner he would escort his aunt\r\nfrom the table in some state (not wholly unaccompanied by a leerish wink\r\nor two from the wags of the place) and he would leave her at the door\r\nof the communal parlours and card rooms, with a formality in his bow of\r\nfarewell which afforded an amusing contrast to Fanny\'s always voluble\r\nprotests. (She never failed to urge loudly that he really must come and\r\nplay, just this once, and not go hiding from everybody in his room every\r\nevening like this!) At least some of the other inhabitants found the\r\ncontrast amusing, for sometimes, as he departed stiffly toward the\r\nelevator, leaving her still entreating in the doorway (though with one\r\neye already on her table, to see that it was not seized) a titter would\r\nfollow him which he was no doubt meant to hear. He did not care whether\r\nthey laughed or not.\r\n\r\nAnd once, as he passed the one or two young men of the place\r\nentertaining the three or four young women, who were elbowing and\r\njerking on a settee in the lobby, he heard a voice inquiring quickly, as\r\nhe passed:\r\n\r\n"What makes people tired?"\r\n\r\n"Work?"\r\n\r\n"No."\r\n\r\n"Well, what\'s the answer?"\r\n\r\nThen, with an intentional outbreak of mirth, the answer was given by two\r\nloudly whispering voices together:\r\n\r\n"A stuck-up boarder!"\r\n\r\nGeorge didn\'t care.\r\n\r\nOn Sunday mornings Fanny went to church and George took long walks. He\r\nexplored the new city, and found it hideous, especially in the early\r\nspring, before the leaves of the shade trees were out. Then the town was\r\nfagged with the long winter and blacked with the heavier smoke that had\r\nbeen held close to the earth by the smoke-fog it bred. Every-thing was\r\ndamply streaked with the soot: the walls of the houses, inside and out,\r\nthe gray curtains at the windows, the windows themselves, the dirty\r\ncement and unswept asphalt underfoot, the very sky overhead. Throughout\r\nthis murky season he continued his explorations, never seeing a face he\r\nknew--for, on Sunday, those whom he remembered, or who might remember\r\nhim, were not apt to be found within the limits of the town, but were\r\ncongenially occupied with the new outdoor life which had come to be the\r\nmode since his boyhood. He and Fanny were pretty thoroughly buried away\r\nwithin the bigness of the city.\r\n\r\nOne of his Sunday walks, that spring, he made into a sour pilgrimage.\r\nIt was a misty morning of belated snow slush, and suited him to a\r\nperfection of miserableness, as he stood before the great dripping\r\ndepartment store which now occupied the big plot of ground where once\r\nhad stood both the Amberson Hotel and the Amberson Opera House. From\r\nthere he drifted to the old "Amberson Block," but this was fallen into a\r\nback-water; business had stagnated here. The old structure had not\r\nbeen replaced, but a cavernous entryway for trucks had been torn in its\r\nfront, and upon the cornice, where the old separate metal letters\r\nhad spelt "Amberson Block," there was a long billboard sign: "Doogan\r\nStorage."\r\n\r\nTo spare himself nothing, he went out National Avenue and saw the piles\r\nof slush-covered wreckage where the Mansion and his mother\'s house had\r\nbeen, and where the Major\'s ill-fated five "new" houses had stood; for\r\nthese were down, too, to make room for the great tenement already shaped\r\nin unending lines of foundation. But the Fountain of Neptune was gone at\r\nlast--and George was glad that it was!\r\n\r\nHe turned away from the devastated site, thinking bitterly that the\r\nonly Amberson mark still left upon the town was the name of the\r\nboulevard--Amberson Boulevard. But he had reckoned without the city\r\ncouncil of the new order, and by an unpleasant coincidence, while the\r\nthought was still in his mind, his eye fell upon a metal oblong sign\r\nupon the lamppost at the corner. There were two of these little signs\r\nupon the lamp-post, at an obtuse angle to each other, one to give\r\npassers-by the name of National Avenue, the other to acquaint them with\r\nAmberson Boulevard. But the one upon which should have been stenciled\r\n"Amberson Boulevard" exhibited the words "Tenth Street."\r\n\r\nGeorge stared at it hard. Then he walked quickly along the boulevard to\r\nthe next corner and looked at the little sign there. "Tenth Street."\r\n\r\nIt had begun to rain, but George stood unheeding, staring at the little\r\nsign. "Damn them!" he said finally, and, turning up his coat-collar,\r\nplodded back through the soggy streets toward "home."\r\n\r\nThe utilitarian impudence of the city authorities put a thought into his\r\nmind. A week earlier he had happened to stroll into the large parlour\r\nof the apartment house, finding it empty, and on the center table he\r\nnoticed a large, red-bound, gilt-edged book, newly printed, bearing\r\nthe title: "A Civic History," and beneath the title, the rubric,\r\n"Biographies of the 500 Most Prominent Citizens and Families in the\r\nHistory of the City." He had glanced at it absently, merely noticing\r\nthe title and sub-title, and wandered out of the room, thinking of other\r\nthings and feeling no curiosity about the book. But he had thought of\r\nit several times since with a faint, vague uneasiness; and now when he\r\nentered the lobby he walked directly into the parlour where he had seen\r\nthe book. The room was empty, as it always was on Sunday mornings, and\r\nthe flamboyant volume was still upon the table--evidently a fixture as\r\na sort of local Almanach de Gotha, or Burke, for the enlightenment of\r\ntenants and boarders.\r\n\r\nHe opened it, finding a few painful steel engravings of placid,\r\nchin-bearded faces, some of which he remembered dimly; but much more\r\nnumerous, and also more unfamiliar to him, were the pictures of\r\nneat, aggressive men, with clipped short hair and clipped short\r\nmoustaches--almost all of them strangers to him. He delayed not long\r\nwith these, but turned to the index where the names of the five hundred\r\nMost Prominent Citizens and Families in the History of the City were\r\narranged in alphabetical order, and ran his finger down the column of\r\nA\'s:\r\n\r\nAbbett Abbott Abrams Adam Adams Adler Akers Albertsmeyer Alexander\r\nAllen Ambrose Ambuhl Anderson Andrews Appenbasch Archer Arszman Ashcraft\r\nAustin Avey\r\n\r\nGeorge\'s eyes remained for some time fixed on the thin space between the\r\nnames "Allen" and "Ambrose." Then he closed the book quietly, and went\r\nup to his own room, agreeing with the elevator boy, on the way, that it\r\nwas getting to be a mighty nasty wet and windy day outside.\r\n\r\nThe elevator boy noticed nothing unusual about him and neither did\r\nFanny, when she came in from church with her hat ruined, an hour later.\r\nAnd yet something had happened--a thing which, years ago, had been the\r\neagerest hope of many, many good citizens of the town. They had thought\r\nof it, longed for it, hoping acutely that they might live to see the\r\nday when it would come to pass. And now it had happened at last: Georgie\r\nMinafer had got his come-upance.\r\n\r\nHe had got it three times filled and running over. The city had rolled\r\nover his heart, burying it under, as it rolled over the Major\'s and\r\nburied it under. The city had rolled over the Ambersons and buried them\r\nunder to the last vestige; and it mattered little that George guessed\r\neasily enough that most of the five hundred Most Prominent had paid\r\nsomething substantial "to defray the cost of steel engraving, etc."--the\r\nFive Hundred had heaved the final shovelful of soot upon that heap\r\nof obscurity wherein the Ambersons were lost forever from sight and\r\nhistory. "Quicksilver in a nest of cracks!"\r\n\r\nGeorgie Minafer had got his come-upance, but the people who had so\r\nlonged for it were not there to see it, and they never knew it. Those\r\nwho were still living had forgotten all about it and all about him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXXIV\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was one border section of the city which George never explored in\r\nhis Sunday morning excursions. This was far out to the north where lay\r\nthe new Elysian Fields of the millionaires, though he once went as far\r\nin that direction as the white house which Lucy had so admired long\r\nago--her "Beautiful House." George looked at it briefly and turned back,\r\nrumbling with an interior laugh of some grimness. The house was white no\r\nlonger; nothing could be white which the town had reached, and the town\r\nreached far beyond the beautiful white house now. The owners had given\r\nup and painted it a despairing chocolate, suitable to the freight-yard\r\nlife it was called upon to endure.\r\n\r\nGeorge did not again risk going even so far as that, in the direction\r\nof the millionaires, although their settlement began at least two miles\r\nfarther out. His thought of Lucy and her father was more a sensation\r\nthan a thought, and may be compared to that of a convicted cashier beset\r\nby recollections of the bank he had pillaged--there are some thoughts to\r\nwhich one closes the mind. George had seen Eugene only once since their\r\ncalamitous encounter. They had passed on opposite sides of the street,\r\ndowntown; each had been aware of the other, and each had been aware that\r\nthe other was aware of him, and yet each kept his eyes straight forward,\r\nand neither had shown a perceptible alteration of countenance. It seemed\r\nto George that he felt emanating from the outwardly imperturbable person\r\nof his mother\'s old friend a hate that was like a hot wind.\r\n\r\nAt his mother\'s funeral and at the Major\'s he had been conscious that\r\nEugene was there: though he had afterward no recollection of seeing him,\r\nand, while certain of his presence, was uncertain how he knew of it.\r\nFanny had not told him, for she understood George well enough not to\r\nspeak to him of Eugene or Lucy. Nowadays Fanny almost never saw either\r\nof them and seldom thought of them--so sly is the way of time with life.\r\nShe was passing middle age, when old intensities and longings grow thin\r\nand flatten out, as Fanny herself was thinning and flattening out; and\r\nshe was settling down contentedly to her apartment house intimacies.\r\nShe was precisely suited by the table-d\'hote life, with its bridge,\r\nits variable alliances and shifting feuds, and the long whisperings\r\nof elderly ladies at corridor corners--those eager but suppressed\r\nconversations, all sibilance, of which the elevator boy declared he\r\nheard the words "she said" a million times and the word "she," five\r\nmillion. The apartment house suited Fanny and swallowed her.\r\n\r\nThe city was so big, now, that people disappeared into it unnoticed, and\r\nthe disappearance of Fanny and her nephew was not exceptional. People no\r\nlonger knew their neighbours as a matter of course; one lived for years\r\nnext door to strangers--that sharpest of all the changes since the old\r\ndays--and a friend would lose sight of a friend for a year, and not know\r\nit.\r\n\r\nOne May day George thought he had a glimpse of Lucy. He was not certain,\r\nbut he was sufficiently disturbed, in spite of his uncertainty. A\r\npromotion in his work now frequently took him out of town for a week,\r\nor longer, and it was upon his return from one of these absences that he\r\nhad the strange experience. He had walked home from the station, and as\r\nhe turned the corner which brought him in sight of the apartment house\r\nentrance, though two blocks distant from it, he saw a charming little\r\nfigure come out, get into a shiny landaulet automobile, and drive away.\r\nEven at that distance no one could have any doubt that the little figure\r\nwas charming; and the height, the quickness and decision of motion,\r\neven the swift gesture of a white glove toward the chauffeur--all were\r\ncharacteristic of Lucy. George was instantly subjected to a shock of\r\nindefinable nature, yet definitely a shock: he did not know what he\r\nfelt--but he knew that he felt. Heat surged over him: probably he\r\nwould not have come face to face with her if the restoration of all the\r\nancient Amberson magnificence could have been his reward. He went on\r\nslowly, his knees shaky.\r\n\r\nBut he found Fanny not at home; she had been out all afternoon; and\r\nthere was no record of any caller--and he began to wonder, then to doubt\r\nif the small lady he had seen in the distance was Lucy. It might as well\r\nhave been, he said to himself--since any one who looked like her could\r\ngive him "a jolt like that!"\r\n\r\nLucy had not left a card. She never left one when she called on Fanny;\r\nthough she did not give her reasons a quite definite form in her own\r\nmind.
