She came seldom; this was but the third time that year, and,\r\nwhen she did come, George was not mentioned either by her hostess or by\r\nherself--an oddity contrived between the two ladies without either of\r\nthem realizing how odd it was. For, naturally, while Fanny was with\r\nLucy, Fanny thought of George, and what time Lucy had George\'s\r\naunt before her eyes she could not well avoid the thought of him.\r\nConsequently, both looked absent-minded as they talked, and each often\r\ngave a wrong answer which the other consistently failed to notice.\r\n\r\nAt other times Lucy\'s thoughts of George were anything but continuous,\r\nand weeks went by when he was not consciously in her mind at all. Her\r\nlife was a busy one: she had the big house "to keep up"; she had a\r\ngarden to keep up, too, a large and beautiful garden; she represented\r\nher father as a director for half a dozen public charity organizations,\r\nand did private charity work of her own, being a proxy mother of several\r\nlarge families; and she had "danced down," as she said, groups from\r\neight or nine classes of new graduates returned from the universities,\r\nwithout marrying any of them, but she still danced--and still did not\r\nmarry.\r\n\r\nHer father, observing this circumstance happily, yet with some\r\nhypocritical concern, spoke of it to her one day as they stood in her\r\ngarden. "I suppose I\'d want to shoot him," he said, with attempted\r\nlightness. "But I mustn\'t be an old pig. I\'d build you a beautiful house\r\nclose by--just over yonder."\r\n\r\n"No, no! That would be like--" she began impulsively; then checked\r\nherself. George Amberson\'s comparison of the Georgian house to the\r\nAmberson Mansion had come into her mind, and she thought that another\r\nnew house, built close by for her, would be like the house the Major\r\nbuilt for Isabel.\r\n\r\n"Like what?"\r\n\r\n"Nothing." She looked serious, and when he reverted to his idea of "some\r\nday" grudgingly surrendering her up to a suitor, she invented a legend.\r\n"Did you ever hear the Indian name for that little grove of beech trees\r\non the other side of the house?" she asked him.\r\n\r\n"No--and you never did either!" he laughed.\r\n\r\n"Don\'t be so sure! I read a great deal more than I used to--getting\r\nready for my bookish days when I\'ll have to do something solid in the\r\nevenings and won\'t be asked to dance any more, even by the very youngest\r\nboys who think it\'s a sporting event to dance with the oldest of the\r\n\'older girls\'. The name of the grove was \'Loma-Nashah\' and it means\r\n\'They-Couldn\'t-Help-It\'."\r\n\r\n"Doesn\'t sound like it."\r\n\r\n"Indian names don\'t. There was a bad Indian chief lived in the grove\r\nbefore the white settlers came. He was the worst Indian that\r\never lived, and his name was--it was \'Vendonah.\' That means\r\n\'Rides-Down-Everything\'."\r\n\r\n"What?"\r\n\r\n"His name was Vendonah, the same thing as Rides-Down-Everything."\r\n\r\n"I see," said Eugene thoughtfully. He gave her a quick look and then\r\nfixed his eyes upon the end of the garden path. "Go on."\r\n\r\n"Vendonah was an unspeakable case," Lucy continued. "He was so proud\r\nthat he wore iron shoes and he walked over people\'s faces with them.\r\nhe was always killing people that way, and so at last the tribe decided\r\nthat it wasn\'t a good enough excuse for him that he was young and\r\ninexperienced--he\'d have to go. They took him down to the river, and put\r\nhim in a canoe, and pushed him out from shore; and then they ran along\r\nthe bank and wouldn\'t let him land, until at last the current carried\r\nthe canoe out into the middle, and then on down to the ocean, and he\r\nnever got back. They didn\'t want him back, of course, and if he\'d been\r\nable to manage it, they\'d have put him in another canoe and shoved him\r\nout into the river again. But still, they didn\'t elect another chief in\r\nhis place. Other tribes thought that was curious, and wondered about\r\nit a lot, but finally they came to the conclusion that the beech grove\r\npeople were afraid a new chief might turn out to be a bad Indian, too,\r\nand wear iron shoes like Vendonah. But they were wrong, because the real\r\nreason was that the tribe had led such an exciting life under Vendonah\r\nthat they couldn\'t settle down to anything tamer. He was awful, but he\r\nalways kept things happening--terrible things, of course. They hated\r\nhim, but they weren\'t able to discover any other warrior that they\r\nwanted to make chief in his place. I suppose it was a little like\r\ndrinking a glass of too strong wine and then trying to take the taste\r\nout of your mouth with barley water. They couldn\'t help feeling that\r\nway."\r\n\r\n"I see," said Eugene. "So that\'s why they named the place\r\n\'They-Couldn\'t-Help-It\'!"\r\n\r\n"It must have been."\r\n\r\n"And so you\'re going to stay here in your garden," he said musingly.\r\n"You think it\'s better to keep on walking these sunshiny gravel paths\r\nbetween your flower-beds, and growing to look like a pensive garden lady\r\nin a Victorian engraving."\r\n\r\n"I suppose I\'m like the tribe that lived here, papa. I had too much\r\nunpleasant excitement. It was unpleasant--but it was excitement. I don\'t\r\nwant any more; in fact, I don\'t want anything but you."\r\n\r\n"You don\'t?" He looked at her keenly, and she laughed and shook her\r\nhead; but he seemed perplexed, rather doubtful. "What was the name of\r\nthe grove?" he asked. "The Indian name, I mean."\r\n\r\n"Mola-Haha."\r\n\r\n"No, it wasn\'t; that wasn\'t the name you said."\r\n\r\n"I\'ve forgotten."\r\n\r\n"I see you have," he said, his look of perplexity remaining. "Perhaps\r\nyou remember the chief\'s name better."\r\n\r\nShe shook her head again. "I don\'t!"\r\n\r\nAt this he laughed, but not very heartily, and walked slowly to the\r\nhouse, leaving her bending over a rose-bush, and a shade more pensive\r\nthan the most pensive garden lady in any Victorian engraving.\r\n\r\n... Next day, it happened that this same "Vendonah" or\r\n"Rides-Down-Everything" became the subject of a chance conversation\r\nbetween Eugene and his old friend Kinney, father of the fire-topped\r\nFred. The two gentlemen found themselves smoking in neighbouring leather\r\nchairs beside a broad window at the club, after lunch.\r\n\r\nMr. Kinney had remarked that he expected to get his family established\r\nat the seashore by the Fourth of July, and, following a train of\r\nthought, he paused and chuckled. "Fourth of July reminds me," he said.\r\n"Have you heard what that Georgie Minafer is doing?"\r\n\r\n"No, I haven\'t," said Eugene, and his friend failed to notice the\r\ncrispness of the utterance.\r\n\r\n"Well, sir," Kinney chuckled again, "it beats the devil! My boy Fred\r\ntold me about it yesterday. He\'s a friend of this young Henry Akers, son\r\nof F. P. Akers of the Akers Chemical Company. It seems this young Akers\r\nasked Fred if he knew a fellow named Minafer, because he knew Fred had\r\nalways lived here, and young Akers had heard some way that Minafer used\r\nto be an old family name here, and was sort of curious about it. Well,\r\nsir, you remember this young Georgie sort of disappeared, after his\r\ngrandfather\'s death, and nobody seemed to know much what had become\r\nof him--though I did hear, once or twice, that he was still around\r\nsomewhere. Well, sir, he\'s working for the Akers Chemical Company, out\r\nat their plant on the Thomasvile Road."\r\n\r\nHe paused, seeming to reserve something to be delivered only upon\r\ninquiry, and Eugene offered him the expected question, but only after a\r\ncold glance through the nose-glasses he had lately found it necessary to\r\nadopt. "What does he do?"\r\n\r\nKinney laughed and slapped the arm of his chair.\r\n\r\n"He\'s a nitroglycerin expert!"\r\n\r\nHe was gratified to see that Eugene was surprised, if not, indeed, a\r\nlittle startled.\r\n\r\n"He\'s what?"\r\n\r\n"He\'s an expert on nitroglycerin. Doesn\'t that beat the devil! Yes,\r\nsir! Young Akers told Fred that this George Minafer had worked like\r\na houn\'-dog ever since he got started out at the works. They have\r\na special plant for nitroglycerin, way off from the main plant, o\'\r\ncourse--in the woods somewhere--and George Minafer\'s been working there,\r\nand lately they put him in charge of it. He oversees shooting oil-wells,\r\ntoo, and shoots \'em himself, sometimes. They aren\'t allowed to carry\r\nit on the railroads, you know--have to team it. Young Akers says George\r\nrides around over the bumpy roads, sitting on as much as three hundred\r\nquarts of nitroglycerin! My Lord! Talk about romantic tumbles! If he\r\ngets blown sky-high some day he won\'t have a bigger drop, when he comes\r\ndown, than he\'s already had! Don\'t it beat the devil! Young Akers said\r\nhe\'s got all the nerve there is in the world. Well, he always did have\r\nplenty of that--from the time he used to ride around here on his white\r\npony and fight all the Irish boys in Can-Town, with his long curls all\r\nhandy to be pulled out. Akers says he gets a fair salary, and I should\r\nthink he ought to! Seems to me I\'ve heard the average life in that\r\nsort of work is somewhere around four years, and agents don\'t write any\r\ninsurance at all for nitroglycerin experts. Hardly!"\r\n\r\n"No," said Eugene. "I suppose not."\r\n\r\nKinney rose to go. "Well, it\'s a pretty funny thing--pretty odd, I\r\nmean--and I suppose it would be pass-around-the-hat for old Fanny\r\nMinafer if he blew up. Fred told me that they\'re living in some\r\napartment house, and said Georgie supports her. He was going to study\r\nlaw, but couldn\'t earn enough that way to take care of Fanny, so he gave\r\nit up. Fred\'s wife told him all this. Says Fanny doesn\'t do anything but\r\nplay bridge these days. Got to playing too high for awhile and lost more\r\nthan she wanted to tell Georgie about, and borrowed a little from old\r\nFrank Bronson. Paid him back, though. Don\'t know how Fred\'s wife heard\r\nit. Women do\' hear the darndest things!"\r\n\r\n"They do," Eugene agreed.\r\n\r\n"I thought you\'d probably heard about it--thought most likely Fred\'s\r\nwife might have said something to your daughter, especially as they\'re\r\ncousins."\r\n\r\n"I think not."\r\n\r\n"Well, I\'m off to the store," said Mr. Kinney briskly; yet he lingered.\r\n"I suppose we\'ll all have to club in and keep old Fanny out of the\r\npoorhouse if he does blow up. From all I hear it\'s usually only a\r\nquestion of time. They say she hasn\'t got anything else to depend on."\r\n\r\n"I suppose not."\r\n\r\n"Well--I wondered--" Kinney hesitated. "I was wondering why you hadn\'t\r\nthought of finding something around your works for him. They say he\'s\r\nan all-fired worker and he certainly does seem to have hid some decent\r\nstuff in him under all his damfoolishness. And you used to be such a\r\ntremendous friend of the family--I thought perhaps you--of course I know\r\nhe\'s a queer lot--I know--"\r\n\r\n"Yes, I think he is," said Eugene. "No. I haven\'t anything to offer\r\nhim."\r\n\r\n"I suppose not," Kinney returned thoughtfully, as he went out. "I don\'t\r\nknow that I would myself. Well, we\'ll probably see his name in the\r\npapers some day if he stays with that job!"\r\n\r\nHowever, the nitroglycerin expert of whom they spoke did not get into\r\nthe papers as a consequence of being blown up, although his daily life\r\nwas certainly a continuous exposure to that risk. Destiny has a constant\r\npassion for the incongruous, and it was George\'s lot to manipulate\r\nwholesale quantities of terrific and volatile explosives in safety, and\r\nto be laid low by an accident so commonplace and inconsequent that it\r\nwas a comedy. Fate had reserved for him the final insult of riding him\r\ndown under the wheels of one of those juggernauts at which he had once\r\nshouted "Git a hoss!" Nevertheless, Fate\'s ironic choice for Georgie\'s\r\nundoing was not a big and swift and momentous car, such as Eugene\r\nmanufactured; it was a specimen of the hustling little type that was\r\nflooding the country, the cheapest, commonest, hardiest little car ever\r\nmade.\r\n\r\nThe accident took place upon a Sunday morning, on a downtown crossing,\r\nwith the streets almost empty, and no reason in the world for such a\r\nthing to happen. He had gone out for his Sunday morning walk, and he was\r\nthinking of an automobile at the very moment when the little car struck\r\nhim; he was thinking of a shiny landaulet and a charming figure stepping\r\ninto it, and of the quick gesture of a white glove toward the chauffeur,\r\nmotioning him to go on. George heard a shout but did not look up, for he\r\ncould not imagine anybody\'s shouting at him, and he was too engrossed\r\nin the question "Was it Lucy?" He could not decide, and his lack of\r\ndecision in this matter probably superinduced a lack of decision in\r\nanother, more pressingly vital. At the second and louder shout he did\r\nlook up; and the car was almost on him; but he could not make up his\r\nmind if the charming little figure he had seen was Lucy\'s and he could\r\nnot make up his mind whether to go backward or forward: these questions\r\nbecame entangled in his mind. Then, still not being able to decide which\r\nof two ways to go, he tried to go both--and the little car ran him down.\r\nIt was not moving very rapidly, but it went all the way over George.\r\n\r\nHe was conscious of gigantic violence; of roaring and jolting and\r\nconcussion; of choking clouds of dust, shot with lightning, about his\r\nhead; he heard snapping sounds as loud as shots from a small pistol, and\r\nwas stabbed by excruciating pains in his legs. Then he became aware\r\nthat the machine was being lifted off of him. People were gathering in a\r\ncircle round him, gabbling.\r\n\r\nHis forehead was bedewed with the sweat of anguish, and he tried to wipe\r\noff this dampness, but failed. He could not get his arm that far.\r\n\r\n"Nev\' mind," a policeman said; and George could see above his eyes the\r\nskirts of the blue coat, covered with dust and sunshine. "Amb\'lance be\r\nhere in a minute. Nev\' mind tryin\' to move any. You want \'em to send for\r\nsome special doctor?"\r\n\r\n"No." George\'s lips formed the word.\r\n\r\n"Or to take you to some private hospital?"\r\n\r\n"Tell them to take me," he said faintly, "to the City Hospital."\r\n\r\n"A\' right."\r\n\r\nA smallish young man in a duster fidgeted among the crowd, explaining\r\nand protesting, and a strident voiced girl, his companion, supported his\r\nargument, declaring to everyone her willingness to offer testimony in\r\nany court of law that every blessed word he said was the God\'s truth.\r\n\r\n"It\'s the fella that hit you," the policeman said, looking down on\r\nGeorge. "I guess he\'s right; you must of been thinkin\' about somep\'m\'\r\nor other. It\'s wunnerful the damage them little machines can do--you\'d\r\nnever think it--but I guess they ain\'t much case ag\'in this fella that\r\nwas drivin\' it."\r\n\r\n"You bet your life they ain\'t no case on me!" the young man in the\r\nduster agreed, with great bitterness. He came and stood at George\'s\r\nfeet, addressing him heatedly: "I\'m sorry fer you all right, and I don\'t\r\nsay I ain\'t. I hold nothin\' against you, but it wasn\'t any more my fault\r\nthan the statehouse! You run into me, much as I run into you, and if you\r\nget well you ain\'t goin\' to get not one single cent out o\' me! This lady\r\nhere was settin\' with me and we both yelled at you. Wasn\'t goin\' a step\r\nover eight mile an hour! I\'m perfectly willing to say I\'m sorry for you\r\nthough, and so\'s the lady with me. We\'re both willing to say that much,\r\nbut that\'s all, understand!"\r\n\r\nGeorge\'s drawn eyelids twitched; his misted glance rested fleetingly\r\nupon the two protesting motorists, and the old imperious spirit within\r\nhim flickered up in a single word. Lying on his back in the middle of\r\nthe street, where he was regarded as an increasing public as an unpleasant\r\ncuriosity, he spoke this word clearly from a mouth filled with dust, and\r\nfrom lips smeared with blood.\r\n\r\nIt was a word which interested the policeman. When the ambulance clanged\r\naway, he turned to a fellow patrolman who had joined him. "Funny what\r\nhe says to the little cuss that done the damage. That\'s all he did call\r\nhim--\'nothin\' else at all--and the cuss had broke both his legs fer him\r\nand God-knows-what-all!"\r\n\r\n"I wasn\'t here then. What was it?"\r\n\r\n"Riffraff!"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter XXXV\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEugene\'s feeling about George had not been altered by his talk with\r\nKinney in the club window, though he was somewhat disturbed. He was not\r\ndisturbed by Kinney\'s hint that Fanny Minafer might be left on the hands\r\nof her friends through her nephew\'s present dealings with nitroglycerin,\r\nbut he was surprised that Kinney had "led up" with intentional tact to\r\nthe suggestion that a position might be made for George in the Morgan\r\nfactory. Eugene did not care to have any suggestions about Georgie\r\nMinafer made to him. Kinney had represented Georgie as a new Georgie--at\r\nleast in spots--a Georgie who was proving that decent stuff had been\r\nhid in him; in fact, a Georgie who was doing rather a handsome thing\r\nin taking a risky job for the sake of his aunt, poor old silly Fanny\r\nMinafer! Eugene didn\'t care what risks Georgie took, or how much decent\r\nstuff he had in him: nothing that Georgie would ever do in this world or\r\nthe next could change Eugene Morgan\'s feeling toward him.\r\n\r\nIf Eugene could possibly have brought himself to offer Georgie a\r\nposition in the automobile business, he knew full well the proud devil\r\nwouldn\'t have taken it from him; though Georgie\'s proud reason would not\r\nhave been the one attributed to him by Eugene. George would never\r\nreach the point where he could accept anything material from Eugene and\r\npreserve the self-respect he had begun to regain.\r\n\r\nBut if Eugene had wished, he could easily have taken George out of\r\nthe nitroglycerin branch of the chemical works. Always interested\r\nin apparent impossibilities of invention, Eugene had encouraged many\r\nexperiments in such gropings as those for the discovery of substitutes\r\nfor gasoline and rubber; and, though his mood had withheld the\r\ninformation from Kinney, he had recently bought from the elder Akers a\r\nsubstantial quantity of stock on the condition that the chemical company\r\nshould establish an experimental laboratory. He intended to buy more;\r\nAkers was anxious to please him; and a word from Eugene would have\r\nplaced George almost anywhere in the chemical works. George need never\r\nhave known it, for Eugene\'s purchases of stock were always quiet ones:\r\nthe transaction remained, so far, between him and Akers, and could be\r\nkept between them.\r\n\r\nThe possibility just edged itself into Eugene\'s mind; that is, he let it\r\nbecome part of his perceptions long enough for it to prove to him that\r\nit was actually a possibility. Then he half started with disgust that he\r\nshould be even idly considering such a thing over his last cigar for\r\nthe night, in his library. "No!" And he threw the cigar into the empty\r\nfireplace and went to bed.\r\n\r\nHis bitterness for himself might have worn away, but never his\r\nbitterness for Isabel. He took that thought to bed with him--and it was\r\ntrue that nothing George could do would ever change this bitterness of\r\nEugene. Only George\'s mother could have changed it.\r\n\r\nAnd as Eugene fell asleep that night, thinking thus bitterly of Georgie,\r\nGeorgie in the hospital was thinking of Eugene. He had come "out of\r\nether" with no great nausea, and had fallen into a reverie, though now\r\nand then a white sailboat staggered foolishly into the small ward where\r\nhe lay. After a time he discovered that this happened only when he tried\r\nto open his eyes and look about him; so he kept his eyes shut, and his\r\nthoughts were clearer.\r\n\r\nHe thought of Eugene Morgan and of the Major; they seemed to be the\r\nsame person for awhile, but he managed to disentangle them and even to\r\nunderstand why he had confused them. Long ago his grandfather had been\r\nthe most striking figure of success in the town: "As rich as Major\r\nAmberson!" they used to say. Now it was Eugene. "If I had Eugene\r\nMorgan\'s money," he would hear the workmen day-dreaming at the chemical\r\nworks; or, "If Eugene Morgan had hold of this place you\'d see things\r\nhum!" And the boarders at the table d\'hote spoke of "the Morgan Place"\r\nas an eighteenth-century Frenchman spoke of Versailles. Like his uncle,\r\nGeorge had perceived that the "Morgan Place" was the new Amberson\r\nMansion. His reverie went back to the palatial days of the Mansion, in\r\nhis boyhood, when he would gallop his pony up the driveway and order\r\nthe darkey stable-men about, while they whooped and obeyed, and his\r\ngrandfather, observing from a window, would laugh and call out to him,\r\n"That\'s right, Georgie. Make those lazy rascals jump!" He remembered his\r\ngay young uncles, and how the town was eager concerning everything about\r\nthem, and about himself. What a clean, pretty town it had been! And in\r\nhis reverie be saw like a pageant before him the magnificence of the\r\nAmbersons--its passing, and the passing of the Ambersons themselves.\r\nThey had been slowly engulfed without knowing how to prevent it, and\r\nalmost without knowing what was happening to them. The family lot, in\r\nthe shabby older quarter, out at the cemetery, held most of them now;\r\nand the name was swept altogether from the new city. But the new\r\ngreat people who had taken their places--the Morgans and Akerses and\r\nSheridans--they would go, too. George saw that. They would pass, as the\r\nAmbersons had passed, and though some of them might do better than\r\nthe Major and leave the letters that spelled a name on a hospital or a\r\nstreet, it would be only a word and it would not stay forever. Nothing\r\nstays or holds or keeps where there is growth, he somehow perceived\r\nvaguely but truly. Great Caesar dead and turned to clay stopped no hole\r\nto keep the wind away. Dead Caesar was nothing but a tiresome bit of\r\nprint in a book that schoolboys study for awhile and then forget. The\r\nAmbersons had passed, and the new people would pass, and the new people\r\nthat came after them, and then the next new ones, and the next--and the\r\nnext--\r\n\r\nHe had begun to murmur, and the man on duty as night nurse for the ward\r\ncame and bent over him.\r\n\r\n"Did you want something?"\r\n\r\n"There\'s nothing in this family business," George told him\r\nconfidentially. "Even George Washington is only something in a book."\r\n\r\nEugene read a report of the accident in the next morning\'s paper. He was\r\non the train, having just left for New York, on business, and with less\r\nleisure would probably have overlooked the obscure item:\r\n\r\nLEGS BROKEN\r\n\r\nG. A. Minafer, an employee of the Akers Chemical Co., was run down by\r\nan automobile yesterday at the corner of Tennessee and Main and had\r\nboth legs broken. Minafer was to blame for the accident according to\r\npatrolman F. A. Kax, who witnessed the affair. The automobile was a\r\nsmall one driven by Herbert Cottleman of 9173 Noble Avenue who stated\r\nthat he was making less than 4 miles an hour. Minafer is said to belong\r\nto a family formerly of considerable prominence in the city. He was\r\ntaken to the City Hospital where physicians stated later that he was\r\nsuffering from internal injuries besides the fracture of his legs but\r\nmight recover.\r\n\r\nEugene read the item twice, then tossed the paper upon the opposite\r\nseat of his compartment, and sat looking out of the window. His feeling\r\ntoward Georgie was changed not a jot by his human pity for Georgie\'s\r\nhuman pain and injury. He thought of Georgie\'s tall and graceful figure,\r\nand he shivered, but his bitterness was untouched. He had never blamed\r\nIsabel for the weakness which had cost them the few years of happiness\r\nthey might have had together; he had put the blame all on the son, and\r\nit stayed there.\r\n\r\nHe began to think poignantly of Isabel: he had seldom been able to "see"\r\nher more clearly than as he sat looking out of his compartment window,\r\nafter reading the account of this accident. She might have been just on\r\nthe other side of the glass, looking in at him--and then he thought of\r\nher as the pale figure of a woman, seen yet unseen, flying through the\r\nair, beside the train, over the fields of springtime green and through\r\nthe woods that were just sprouting out their little leaves. He closed\r\nhis eyes and saw her as she had been long ago. He saw the brown-eyed,\r\nbrown-haired, proud, gentle, laughing girl he had known when first he\r\ncame to town, a boy just out of the State College. He remembered--as he\r\nhad remembered ten thousand times before--the look she gave him when\r\nher brother George introduced him to her at a picnic; it was "like hazel\r\nstarlight" he had written her, in a poem, afterward. He remembered\r\nhis first call at the Amberson Mansion, and what a great personage she\r\nseemed, at home in that magnificence; and yet so gay and friendly. He\r\nremembered the first time he had danced with her--and the old waltz song\r\nbegan to beat in his ears and in his heart. They laughed and sang it\r\ntogether as they danced to it:\r\n\r\n"Oh, love for a year, a week, a day, But alas for the love that lasts\r\nalways--"\r\n\r\nMost plainly of all he could see her dancing; and he became articulate\r\nin the mourning whisper: "So graceful--oh, so graceful--"\r\n\r\nAll the way to New York it seemed to him that Isabel was near him, and\r\nhe wrote of her to Lucy from his hotel the next night:\r\n\r\nI saw an account of the accident to George Minafer. I\'m sorry, though\r\nthe paper states that it was plainly his own fault. I suppose it may\r\nhave been as a result of my attention falling upon the item that I\r\nthought of his mother a great deal on the way here. It seemed to me that\r\nI had never seen her more distinctly or so constantly, but, as you know,\r\nthinking of his mother is not very apt to make me admire him! Of course,\r\nhowever, he has my best wishes for his recovery.\r\n\r\nHe posted the letter, and by the morning\'s mail he received one from\r\nLucy written a few hours after his departure from home. She enclosed the\r\nitem he had read on the train.\r\n\r\nI thought you might not see it.\r\n\r\nI have seen Miss Fanny and she has got him put into a room by himself.\r\nOh, poor Rides-Down-Everything I have been thinking so constantly of his\r\nmother and it seemed to me that I have never seen her more distinctly.\r\nHow lovely she was--and how she loved him!\r\n\r\nIf Lucy had not written this letter Eugene might not have done the odd\r\nthing he did that day. Nothing could have been more natural than that\r\nboth he and Lucy should have thought intently of Isabel after reading\r\nthe account of George\'s accident, but the fact that Lucy\'s letter had\r\ncrossed his own made Eugene begin to wonder if a phenomenon of telepathy\r\nmight not be in question, rather than a chance coincidence. The\r\nreference to Isabel in the two letters was almost identical: he and\r\nLucy, it appeared, had been thinking of Isabel at the same time--both\r\nsaid "constantly" thinking of her--and neither had ever "seen her more\r\ndistinctly." He remembered these phrases in his own letter accurately.\r\n\r\nReflection upon the circumstance stirred a queer spot in Eugene\'s\r\nbrain--he had one. He was an adventurer; if he had lived in the\r\nsixteenth century he would have sailed the unknown new seas, but having\r\nbeen born in the latter part of the nineteenth, when geography was a\r\nfairly well-settled matter, he had become an explorer in mechanics.\r\nBut the fact that he was a "hard-headed business man" as well as an\r\nadventurer did not keep him from having a queer spot in his brain,\r\nbecause hard-headed business men are as susceptible to such spots as\r\nadventurers are. Some of them are secretly troubled when they do not see\r\nthe new moon over the lucky shoulder; some of them have strange, secret\r\nincredulities--they do not believe in geology, for instance; and some of\r\nthem think they have had supernatural experiences. "Of course there was\r\nnothing in it--still it was queer!" they say.\r\n\r\nTwo weeks after Isabel\'s death, Eugene had come to New York on urgent\r\nbusiness and found that the delayed arrival of a steamer gave him a\r\nday with nothing to do. His room at the hotel had become intolerable;\r\noutdoors was intolerable; everything was intolerable. It seemed to him\r\nthat he must see Isabel once more, hear her voice once more; that he\r\nmust find some way to her, or lose his mind. Under this pressure he\r\nhad gone, with complete scepticism, to a "trance-medium" of whom he had\r\nheard wild accounts from the wife of a business acquaintance. He thought\r\ndespairingly that at least such an excursion would be "trying to do\r\nsomething!" He remembered the woman\'s name; found it in the telephone\r\nbook, and made an appointment.\r\n\r\nThe experience had been grotesque, and he came away with an\r\nencouraging message from his father, who had failed to identify himself\r\nsatisfactorily, but declared that everything was "on a higher plane"\r\nin his present state of being, and that all life was "continuous and\r\nprogressive." Mrs. Horner spoke of herself as a "psychic"; but otherwise\r\nshe seemed oddly unpretentious and matter-of-fact; and Eugene had\r\nno doubt at all of her sincerity. He was sure that she was not an\r\nintentional fraud, and though he departed in a state of annoyance with\r\nhimself, he came to the conclusion that if any credulity were played\r\nupon by Mrs. Horner\'s exhibitions, it was her own.\r\n\r\nNevertheless, his queer spot having been stimulated to action by\r\nthe coincidence of the letters, he went to Mrs. Horner\'s after his\r\ndirectors\' meeting today. He used the telephone booth in the directors\'\r\nroom to make the appointment; and he laughed feebly at himself, and\r\nwondered what the group of men in that mahogany apartment would think if\r\nthey knew what he was doing. Mrs. Horner had changed her address, but he\r\nfound the new one, and somebody purporting to be a niece of hers talked\r\nto him and made an appointment for a "sitting" at five o\'clock. He was\r\nprompt, and the niece, a dull-faced fat girl with a magazine under her\r\narm, admitted him to Mrs. Horner\'s apartment, which smelt of camphor;\r\nand showed him into a room with gray painted walls, no rug on the floor\r\nand no furniture except a table (with nothing on it) and two chairs: one\r\na leather easy-chair and the other a stiff little brute with a wooden\r\nseat. There was one window with the shade pulled down to the sill, but\r\nthe sun was bright outside, and the room had light enough.\r\n\r\nMrs. Horner appeared in the doorway, a wan and unenterprising looking\r\nwoman in brown, with thin hair artificially waved--but not recently--and\r\nparted in the middle over a bluish forehead. Her eyes were small and\r\nseemed weak, but she recognized the visitor.\r\n\r\n"Oh, you been here before," she said, in a thin voice, not unmusical. "I\r\nrecollect you. Quite a time ago, wa\'n\'t it?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, quite a long time."\r\n\r\n"I recollect because I recollect you was disappointed. Anyway, you was\r\nkind of cross." She laughed faintly.\r\n\r\n"I\'m sorry if I seemed so," Eugene said. "Do you happen to have found\r\nout my name?"\r\n\r\nShe looked surprised and a little reproachful. "Why, no. I never try\r\nto find out people\'s name. Why should I? I don\'t claim anything for\r\nthe power; I only know I have it--and some ways it ain\'t always such a\r\nblessing, neither, I can tell you!"\r\n\r\nEugene did not press an investigation of her meaning, but said vaguely,\r\n"I suppose not. Shall we--"\r\n\r\n"All right," she assented, dropping into the leather chair, with her\r\nback to the shaded window. "You better set down, too, I reckon. I hope\r\nyou\'ll get something this time so you won\'t feel cross, but I dunno. I\r\ncan\'t never tell what they\'ll do. Well--"\r\n\r\nShe sighed, closed her eyes, and was silent, while Eugene, seated in\r\nthe stiff chair across the table from her, watched her profile, thought\r\nhimself an idiot, and called himself that and other names. And as the\r\nsilence continued, and the impassive woman in the easy-chair remained\r\nimpassive, he began to wonder what had led him to be such a fool. It\r\nbecame clear to him that the similarity of his letter and Lucy\'s needed\r\nno explanation involving telepathy, and was not even an extraordinary\r\ncoincidence. What, then, had brought him back to this absurd place and\r\ncaused him to be watching this absurd woman taking a nap in a chair?\r\nIn brief: What the devil did he mean by it? He had not the slightest\r\ninterest in Mrs. Horner\'s naps--or in her teeth, which were being\r\nslightly revealed by the unconscious parting of her lips, as her\r\nbreathing became heavier. If the vagaries of his own mind had brought\r\nhim into such a grotesquerie as this, into what did the vagaries of\r\nother men\'s minds take them? Confident that he was ordinarily saner than\r\nmost people, he perceived that since he was capable of doing a thing\r\nlike this, other men did even more idiotic things, in secret. And he\r\nhad a fleeting vision of sober-looking bankers and manufacturers and\r\nlawyers, well-dressed church-going men, sound citizens--and all as queer\r\nas the deuce inside!\r\n\r\nHow long was he going to sit here presiding over this unknown woman\'s\r\nslumbers? It struck him that to make the picture complete he ought to be\r\nshooing flies away from her with a palm-leaf fan.\r\n\r\nMrs. Horner\'s parted lips closed again abruptly, and became compressed;\r\nher shoulders moved a little, then jerked repeatedly; her small\r\nchest heaved; she gasped, and the compressed lips relaxed to a\r\nslight contortion, then began to move, whispering and bringing forth\r\nindistinguishable mutterings.\r\n\r\nSuddenly she spoke in a loud, husky voice:\r\n\r\n"Lopa is here!"\r\n\r\n"Yes," Eugene said dryly. "That\'s what you said last time. I remember\r\n\'Lopa.\' She\'s your \'control\' I think you said."\r\n\r\n"I\'m Lopa," said the husky voice. "I\'m Lopa herself."\r\n\r\n"You mean I\'m to suppose you\'re not Mrs. Horner now?"\r\n\r\n"Never was Mrs. Horner!" the voice declared, speaking undeniably from\r\nMrs. Horner\'s lips--but with such conviction that Eugene, in spite of\r\neverything, began to feel himself in the presence of a third party,\r\nwho was none the less an individual, even though she might be another\r\nedition of the apparently somnambulistic Mrs. Horner. "Never was Mrs.\r\nHorner or anybody but just Lopa. Guide."\r\n\r\n"You mean you\'re Mrs. Horner\'s guide?" he asked.\r\n\r\n"Your guide now," said the voice with emphasis, to which was\r\nincongruously added a low laugh. "You came here once before. Lopa\r\nremembers."\r\n\r\n"Yes--so did Mrs. Horner."\r\n\r\nLopa overlooked his implication, and continued, quickly: "You build.\r\nBuild things that go. You came here once and old gentleman on this side,\r\nhe spoke to you. Same old gentleman here now. He tell Lopa he\'s your\r\ngrandfather--no, he says \'father.\' He\'s your father."\r\n\r\n"What\'s his appearance?"\r\n\r\n"How?"\r\n\r\n"What does he look like?"\r\n\r\n"Very fine! White beard, but not long beard. He says someone else wants\r\nto speak to you. See here. Lady. Not his wife, though. No. Very fine\r\nlady! Fine lady, fine lady!"\r\n\r\n"Is it my sister?" Eugene asked.\r\n\r\n"Sister? No. She is shaking her head. She has pretty brown hair. She is\r\nfond of you. She is someone who knows you very well but she is not your\r\nsister. She is very anxious to say something to you--very anxious. Very\r\nfond of you; very anxious to talk to you. Very glad you came here--oh,\r\nvery, glad!"\r\n\r\n"What is her name?"\r\n\r\n"Name," the voice repeated, and seemed to ruminate. "Name hard to\r\nget--always very hard for Lopa. Name. She wants to tell me her name to\r\ntell you. She wants you to understand names are hard to make. She says\r\nyou must think of something that makes a sound." Here the voice seemed\r\nto put a question to an invisible presence and to receive an answer. "A\r\nlittle sound or a big sound? She says it might be a little sound or a\r\nbig sound. She says a ring--oh, Lopa knows! She means a bell! That\'s it,\r\na bell."\r\n\r\nEugene looked grave. "Does she mean her name is Belle?"\r\n\r\n"Not quite. Her name is longer."\r\n\r\n"Perhaps," he suggested, "she means that she was a belle."\r\n\r\n"No. She says she thinks you know what she means. She says you must\r\nthink of a colour. What colour?" Again Lopa addressed the unknown, but\r\nthis time seemed to wait for an answer.\r\n\r\n"Perhaps she means the colour of her eyes," said Eugene.\r\n\r\n"No. She says her colour is light--it\'s a light colour and you can see\r\nthrough it."\r\n\r\n"Amber?" he said, and was startled, for Mrs. Horner, with her eyes still\r\nclosed, clapped her hands, and the voice cried out in delight:\r\n\r\n"Yes! She says you know who she is from amber. Amber! Amber! That\'s it!\r\nShe says you understand what her name is from a bell and from amber. She\r\nis laughing and waving a lace handkerchief at me because she is pleased.\r\nShe says I have made you know who it is."\r\n\r\nThis was the strangest moment of Eugene\'s life, because, while it\r\nlasted, he believed that Isabel Amberson, who was dead, had found means\r\nto speak to him. Though within ten minutes he doubted it, he believed it\r\nthen.\r\n\r\nHis elbows pressed hard upon the table, and, his head between his hands,\r\nhe leaned forward, staring at the commonplace figure in the easy-chair.\r\n"What does she wish to say to me?"\r\n\r\n"She is happy because you know her. No--she is troubled. Oh--a great\r\ntrouble! Something she wants to tell you. She wants so much to tell you.\r\nShe wants Lopa to tell you. This is a great trouble. She says--oh, yes,\r\nshe wants you to be--to be kind! That\'s what she says. That\'s it. To be\r\nkind."\r\n\r\n"Does she--"\r\n\r\n"She wants you to be kind," said the voice. "She nods when I tell you\r\nthis. Yes; it must be right. She is a very fine lady. Very pretty.\r\nShe is so anxious for you to understand. She hopes and hopes you will.\r\nSomeone else wants to speak to you. This is a man. He says--"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t want to speak to any one else," said Eugene quickly. "I want--"\r\n\r\n"This man who has come says that he is a friend of yours. He says--"\r\n\r\nEugene struck the table with his fist. "I don\'t want to speak to any\r\none else, I tell you!" he cried passionately. "If she is there I--" He\r\ncaught his breath sharply, checked himself, and sat in amazement. Could\r\nhis mind so easily accept so stupendous a thing as true? Evidently it\r\ncould!\r\n\r\nMrs. Horner spoke languidly in her own voice: "Did you get anything\r\nsatisfactory?" she asked. "I certainly hope it wasn\'t like that other\r\ntime when you was cross because they couldn\'t get anything for you."\r\n\r\n"No, no," he said hastily. "This was different It was very interesting."\r\n\r\nHe paid her, went to his hotel, and thence to his train for home. Never\r\ndid he so seem to move through a world of dream-stuff: for he knew that\r\nhe was not more credulous than other men, and, if he could believe what\r\nhe had believed, though he had believed it for no longer than a moment\r\nor two, what hold had he or any other human being on reality?\r\n\r\nHis credulity vanished (or so he thought) with his recollection that it\r\nwas he, and not the alleged "Lopa," who had suggested the word "amber."\r\nGoing over the mortifying, plain facts of his experience, he found that\r\nMrs. Horner, or the subdivision of Mrs. Horner known as "Lopa," had told\r\nhim to think of a bell and of a colour, and that being furnished with\r\nthese scientific data, he had leaped to the conclusion that he spoke\r\nwith Isabel Amberson!\r\n\r\nFor a moment he had believed that Isabel was there, believed that she\r\nwas close to him, entreating him--entreating him "to be kind." But with\r\nthis recollection a strange agitation came upon him. After all, had\r\nshe not spoken to him? If his own unknown consciousness had told the\r\n"psychic\'s" unknown consciousness how to make the picture of the pretty\r\nbrown-haired, brown-eyed lady, hadn\'t the picture been a true one? And\r\nhadn\'t the true Isabel--oh, indeed her very soul!--called to him out of\r\nhis own true memory of her?\r\n\r\nAnd as the train roared through the darkened evening he looked out\r\nbeyond his window, and saw her as he had seen her on his journey, a few\r\ndays ago--an ethereal figure flying beside the train, but now it\r\nseemed to him that she kept her face toward his window with an infinite\r\nwistfulness.\r\n\r\n"To be kind!" If it had been Isabel, was that what she would have said?\r\nIf she were anywhere, and could come to him through the invisible wall,\r\nwhat would be the first thing she would say to him?\r\n\r\nAh, well enough, and perhaps bitterly enough, he knew the answer to that\r\nquestion! "To be kind"--to Georgie!\r\n\r\nA red-cap at the station, when he arrived, leaped for his bag,\r\nabandoning another which the Pullman porter had handed him. "Yessuh,\r\nMist\' Morgan. Yessuh. You\' car waitin\' front the station fer you, Mist\'\r\nMorgan, suh!"\r\n\r\nAnd people in the crowd about the gates turned to stare, as he passed\r\nthrough, whispering, "That\'s Morgan."\r\n\r\nOutside, the neat chauffeur stood at the door of the touring-car like a\r\nsoldier in whip-cord.\r\n\r\n"I\'ll not go home now, Harry," said Eugene, when he had got in. "Drive\r\nto the City Hospital."\r\n\r\n"Yes, sir," the man returned. "Miss Lucy\'s there. She said she expected\r\nyou\'d come there before you went home."\r\n\r\n"She did?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, sir."\r\n\r\nEugene stared. "I suppose Mr. Minafer must be pretty bad," he said.\r\n\r\n"Yes, sir. I understand he\'s liable to get well, though, sir." He moved\r\nhis lever into high speed, and the car went through the heavy traffic\r\nlike some fast, faithful beast that knew its way about, and knew its\r\nmaster\'s need of haste. Eugene did not speak again until they reached\r\nthe hospital.\r\n\r\nFanny met him in the upper corridor, and took him to an open door.\r\n\r\nHe stopped on the threshold, startled; for, from the waxen face on the\r\npillow, almost it seemed the eyes of Isabel herself were looking at\r\nhim: never before had the resemblance between mother and son been so\r\nstrong--and Eugene knew that now he had once seen it thus startlingly,\r\nhe need divest himself of no bitterness "to be kind" to Georgie.\r\n\r\nGeorge was startled, too. He lifted a white hand in a queer gesture,\r\nhalf forbidding, half imploring, and then let his arm fall back upon the\r\ncoverlet. "You must have thought my mother wanted you to come," he said,\r\n"so that I could ask you to--to forgive me."\r\n\r\nBut Lucy, who sat beside him, lifted ineffable eyes from him to her\r\nfather, and shook her head. "No, just to take his hand--gently!"\r\n\r\nShe was radiant.\r\n\r\nBut for Eugene another radiance filled the room. He knew that he had\r\nbeen true at last to his true love, and that through him she had brought\r\nher boy under shelter again. Her eyes would look wistful no more.\r\n\r\nThe End\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of Project Gutenberg\'s The Magnificent Ambersons, by Booth Tarkington\r\n\r\n*** END OF THIS PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE MAGNIFICENT AMBERSONS ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 8867.txt or 8867.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n        http://www.gutenberg.org/8/8/6/8867/\r\n\r\nProduced by An Anonymous Volunteer\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions\r\nwill be renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from public domain print editions means that no\r\none owns a United States copyright in these works, so the Foundation\r\n(and you!) can copy and distribute it in the United States without\r\npermission and without paying copyright royalties.
